THE RED PAGE.
In the matter of Tennyson J. Le Gay
Brereton writes :
Historically the significance of Tennyson’s
poetry lies in this? he is tfie representative
English poet of the Victorian Age. Only a man
of decided limitations could hold this position.
Browning was too cosmopolitan ; his Anglicanism
was merged in Humanity ; hewas not sufficiently
commonplace. Tennyson was painfully, blindly
English. The Englishman hates innovation, and
prays that he may be allowed to grovel before the
gods of his fathers ; he will only accept reform on
traditional lines. And Tennyson magniloquently
claimed the august right to make Freedom walk
in hobbles.
* ■♦ * *
He was not a great thinker—not a leader of
men. He was never ahead of his age ; but he
was always abreast of it. And that is the proper
position for a representative. He has been called
a leader of modern thought, but really he was
merely a front-rank man. He never prepared the
way for the flood of public opinion, but he was
always on the crest of the wave. The middle-
class Englishman was sometimes ready to acclaim
hi •> as a prophet, because he gave magnificent
expression to current middle-class sentiment. To-
day the rear-rank man regards him as an intel-
lectual Wellington ; but what about to-inorrow?
* * * •
Is deep and searching thought essential in a
great poet? What of Chaucer, Spenser, Pope,
Keats? If Pope l>e a mere versifier, still what of
the other three? The ideal poet is anthropo-
morphous God, expressing universal beauty in
perfect verbal harmony. He is impossible. “The
perfect round ” is not obtainable, and wo may as
well be thankful for the “broken arcs.”
• • • •
Tennyson’s work was affected by all the ques-
tions which vexed the public mind of his day. He
considered them sanely and unenthusiastically, as
a rule, and his dicta were admirably moderate and
calmly uninspiring. In his pages we see traces of
the transcendental movement; of the mediaeval-
ism which gave us Burne-Jones and Rossetti; of
the scientific spirit which resulted in many
mechanical “ improvements ” and the revolution-
ary discovery of the truths of Evolution. He
took a quiet, artistic interest in the social, politi-
cal, and religious struggles which seethed and
roared about his insulated pinnacle. He jotted
down in sonorous scraps of verse the unremarkable
observations of a comfortable, true-hearted country
gentleman. In a man born and bred in Australia
his vapid sentiments rouse indignation. He is a
representative. Australia is impatient of the
sluggishness of old England. Fingers itch for a
goad.
• • • *
The representative poet of a country must be a
man of decided nationality. [Tennyson repre-
sented England ; Kipling is trying to fill a
vacant niche by celebrating the Empire.] Tenny-
son had a constitutional love of his native land.
He found in himself a reflection of her temper—
self-controlled and abhorrent of sudden change.
England is the chosen home of Freedom, he tells
us, but Freedom is a ‘sober-suited ’ puritan—not
a radiant spirit. She is quiet and level-headed.
And the poet agrees with her views ; he does not
worship her. His patriotism is generally sedate
enough, but occasionally he catches fire, and the
result is magnificent. The ballad of ‘ The
Revenge ’ has a grand glow of sincere feeling, for
example, and is the most stirring piece of patriotic
verse I happen to know. No doubt, however, it
owes a good deal to the narratives of Ralegh and
Linschoten. For Tennyson is not a great creator.
He is at his best when somebody gives him a hint
to expand. In the rich soil of his artistic mind
the germ of a suggestion bursts into splendid
blossom. Is it not England’s way to take the
fruits of foreign culture, acclimatise them, and
turn them to excellent account ?
• * • •
All—or almost all—Tennyson’s limitations as a
poet may be concentrated in a single statement:
he was a man of little spontaneous sympathy.
His descriptions of natural objects and phenomena
are wonderfully precise, scientifically accurate.
They are the result of careful study, not of loving
communion with nature. (For external evidence
of this fact, see the recently-published ‘Memoir.’)
He never had the sense of companionship and
direct contact with the life of surrounding objects.
In describing men hewas at agreater disadvantage.
He lived in quiet seclusion, far from the stress of
life; and the steady self-control of his youth had
so habituated him to half-tones of feeling that he
could only by an effort partially appreciate pas-
sionate emotion. He watched life, hermit-like,
from a distance, and he could hardly distinguish
between the movements of intense passion and the
gesticulations of insanity. He was the cool re-
prover of all extravagance and violence of feeling
or action. Everything, he said, should be done in
due order and without haste ; there was to be no
hurry, no disturbing enthusiasm. In his “ little
room so warm and bright ” he announced his pro-
found respect for Law. His political and social
creed was severe, restrained —generally cold and
unjust. Leading an existence of ordered luxury
and almo! t bearish exclusion, he had no real sym-
pathy with the people, because he knew very little
about them. The spirit of modern Democracy
was for him a riddle—a riddle which he never
tried to guess.
• • * *
He himself said that “he could not truly
pout tray the atmosphere of foreign lands.” He
could only understand England and the English
—dull skies and plodding ways. The French he
sneered at, of course. He js the poetic type of
the gentlemen of England who sit at home at
ease.
• • • •
In these scrappy notes I have kept to the one
point of view. I have not dwelt upon the ex-
quisite tenderness of Tennyson’s lyrics ; the
simplicity, truth and beauty of his domestic
Idylls ; the dignity and pathos of his elegies ; the
ever-increasing power of his dramatic monologues.
These gifts I accept with reverence, and leave to
others the difficult task of attempting to appraise
their value.
. -
Still apropos of Dr. Roth’s book about
the N.W.C.Q. aborigines : Some words in
the Pitta-Pitta vocabulary suggest things.
The P.-P. have learnt to kiss (though
whether from the whites or no Dr. Roth
will not say); and to kiss in their language
is ing-gd-mul-le. But only mothers kiss
infants, and fathers wives (so far as Dr.
Roth has observed) — ‘ a long, long kiss, a
kiss of youth and love ’ ~ seems unknown
to the P.-P. Utta is P.-P. for ‘blood-
relationship.’ Now was not the ancient
essence of the Maori utu a blood-ior-blood re-
taliation 1 Again, kan-mdre is P.-P. for a
‘huge, supernatural water-snake.’ The cor-
respondence with Latin mdre is too easy ;
but cannot some inspired philologist trace a
connection with root an in Maori taniwha?
There is no definite trace of kangaroo in
any of the dozen dialects which Dr. Roth
tabulates. The Pitta-Pitta word is md-
choom-bd ; and the others are as far away or
farther. And there is no trace of boomerang.
On the other hand, woom > a or wommera (as
Dr. Roth shells it), galah, cordla, have un-
doubted al ginal roots. Dr. Roth tabu-
lates aboriginal names for ‘mother-in-law.’
Most of the dialects show great resource—they
have *wo names—one for the man’s and one
for the woman’s mother-in-law. But for the
Goa and Woonamurra mother-in-law, and
for pituri in Goa and Woonamurra, Dr.
Roth supplies no equivalent. This may be,
of course, because he has been unable to find
it; but one likes to think of the Goa and
Woonamurra as tribes to whom mothers-in-
law are absolutely unknown, and who con-
sequently need no eonsoling pituri —can
cheerfully pose as the aboriginal teetotallers
of N.Q. !
• V . * *
Dr. Roth talks interestingly about the
famous letter or message-stick : The message-
stick is ‘ nothing more than a sort of
brand or mark ’ to identify the sender; ‘ there
is nothing on it in the form of a com-
munication which can actually be read, the
substitute or messenger invariably carrying
the message by word, of mouth.’ The marks
on a message-stick are designed either as
ornaments or as rude representations of
natural objects in the sender’s locality.
The message-stick may be put into actual use as
follows ‘Charlie,’ residing at Boulia, we will
say, wants some pituri, but, being employed at
work from which he cannot be spared, or being
sick, is prevented going for it himself: he
therefore sends a brother of his, ‘ ) eter,’ to
the nearest market, which in this case would
be Carlo, to get some for him. Peter takes
the stick which Charlie has just made, and jour-
neying vid Herbert Downs and Glenormiston ar-
rives at his destination on the Mulligan River,
where, being asked his business, he says that he
has come for pituri; and hands in the message-
stick. In reply to questions he will mention who
it is that has sent him—namely, Charlie of Boulia
—and may then be informed by his inqui-
sitor, ‘No, I can’t let you have any. I have
two or three letter-sticks here already, wait-
ing for pituri, and I must serve them
first. Go and ask that fellow over there.’
Approaching the person indicated, he is asked as
before what he wants and whom he comes from :
having satisfied the vendor as to his bona tides, the
latter takes charge of the stick, and tells him he
can supply him in a few days’ time, Peter in the
meantime making friends with some ‘ brother’ or
* sister ’in the local camp. A dilly-bag-full of
pituri being at last forthcoming, it is handed over
with the letter-stick (which the vendor has been
taking careful mental note of, so as to recognise
again), and Peter takes his departure back to
Charlie afi Boulia. Charlie, on receipt of the
parcel, will now forward some boomerangs,
spears, a Government blanket, etc., in pay-
ment : if he can get Peter to take a second
trip all well and good, but if not, as is generally
the case for such a journey, he gets another mes-
senger with the goods and the identical message-
stick as before. This second messenger, arriving
at Carlo, finds the vendor, and hands him over the
goods, together with the .stick : recognising the
latter, the seller accepts the various articles in
payment for the particular bagful of pituri which
he had parted with some weeks previously, know-
ing now that he has been paid by the right party
(probably personally unknown to him)— He., the
sender of the original stick. But, supposing he
never gets paid, or does not receive what he con-
siders to be sufficient, he will bear the fact
well in mind that Charlie of Boulia is a bad char-
acter not to be depended on, and take good care
that neither he nor his friends have any dealings
with him in the future. The seller, under circum-
stances such as have been just narrated, does not,
as a rule, ask for the particular commodities he
expects to exchange, each head-encampment hav-
ing its own special and well-known line of goods.
Instead of trade or barter, the letter-stick may be
sent round with any ordinary message or private
communication.
But why doesn’t Charlie send his barter-
stuff by his first messenger ? And could he
not, when discredited, forge another ‘ signa-
ture ’ on another stick ? Presumably, as_
indeed appears indirectly from Dr. Roth’s
book, blackfellow is in many things more
honest and reliable than whitefeilow.

Writes Ernest F.-’ -'oc —
Oiik great error n. l y Prof. Morris in the
‘ Dictionary of Austral English ’ is to quote and
rely on worthless authorities. I could name
several he constantly quotes who are not worth a
damn as authorities on Australia. Take, for
instance, T. B. Wilson, who writes of the bell-
bird : ‘During the night the bell-bird supplied to
us the place of the wakeful nightingale.’ This
nocturnal bell-bird is a stranger to me. Up to
dusk the bird bells, and starts again at dawn,
being especially loud about noon.
Other examples hastily noted. —Prof. Morris
defines ‘ back-slanging ’ as * asking for hospitality
at a station ’!
‘ Dabchick.’— Morris states that there is no
species in Australia. Birds so called are common
enough in Queensland, Northern Territory, and
Westralia.
‘ Joey ’ is denned as ‘ a young kangaroo ; * but
the word is just as commonly applied to young
opossums. Opossums often carry their.<young on
their backs, and consequently wear bald patches.
The skins of such are called ‘joey-ridden skins,’
and, as skins, are of little value.
‘Johnny-Cake.’—A johnny-cake is never ‘ baked
on the ashes,’ as Prof. Morris states, but is baked
on live coals. (Johnny-cake is synonymous with
Prof. Morris’s ‘ Devil-on-the-coals ’ —though bush-
men don’t say ‘devil,’ but something unprint-
able. )
Prof. Morris two or three times quotes Wal-
lace’s erroneous description of mulga as an ‘ acacia
bush armed with strong spikes.’
‘ Packer ’ is hardly ever applied to a horse —
pack-horse being almost invariably used. A
packer is a man who carries rations and stores
over rough country by means of pack-horses.
So applied, the term is common to Australia,
Tasmania and M.L., but Prof. Morris, does not
notice it.
‘Doing a perish’ is not ‘modern slang from
Western Australia,’ but is a phrase in use for
years in all Australian provinces. To ‘do a
perish’means to undergo the deprivation of any
necessary article, not (as represented) of water in
particular.
Prof. Morris defines ‘Poddy ’ as ‘ a Vic. name
for sand-mullet,’ but leaves out its meaning of
motherless calf or fo„l (common in the bush). A
poddy calf or a poddy foal is heard all over Aus-
tralia. A possible derivation is that the young-
sters having to forage for themselves, their irre-
gular diet makes them pot-bellied.
‘ Slab.’ —A slab is not ‘ axe-hewn,’ but split.
CROSSING THE BAR.
Sunset and evening star,
And one clear call for me !
And may there be no moaning of the bar,
When I put out to sea,
But suoh a tide as moving seems asleep,
Too full for sound and foam,
When that which drew from out the boundless
deep
Turns again home.
Twilight and evening be! l ,
And after that the dark
And may there be no sadness of farewell
When t embark ;
For tho’ from out our bourne of Time and Place
The flood may hear me far,
I hope to see my Pilot face to face
When I have crost the bar.
Alfred Tennyson.
EPILOGUE TO ‘ ASOLANDO.’
At the midnight in the silence of the sleep-time,
When you set your fancies free,
Will they pass to where—by death, fools think,
imprisoned—
Low he who once so loved you, whom you
loved so,
—Pity me ?
Oh to love so, be so loved, yet so mistaken !
What had I on earth to do
With the slothful, with the mawkish, the un-
manly ?
Like the aimless, helpless, hopeless, did I drivel
—Being—who ?
One who never turned his back but marched breast
forward,
Never doubted clouds would break,
Never dreamed, though right were worsted, wrong
would triumph,
Held we fall to rise, are baffled to fight better,
Sleep to wake.
No, at noonday in the hustle of man’s work-time
Greet the unseen with a cheer !
Bid him forward, breast and back as either should
be,
“Strive and thrive!” cry “Speed,—fight on,
fare ever
There as here ! ”
Robert Brouning.

SURGEON LANGSTON, M.R.C.S., Eng.,
THE GREATEST AND MOST SUCCESSFUL PRACTI-
TIONER IN THE WORLD.
But few of noted specialists have sprung’ into pre-emin-
ence and an immense practice as Surgeon Langston, a
resident and practitioner at 121 Collins-street, Melbourne.
His name has become almost literally a household word ;
almost every other man you meet has a wonderful story to
relate of some almost miraculous cure he has performed
in an acute or chronic case of long standing, where other
physicians have failed. He addresses himself in particular
to cases that have baffled the skill and science of other
doctors. His name is a sufficient guarantee of a perfect
cure of every case he undertakes. Consult him this day.
It may save you much mental and physical suffering, and
add golden years to your-life.
YOUNG MEN. You will find this distinguished doctor
your best friend and benefactor. If you are troubled
with want of sleep, a tired, gloomy feeling, and loss
of energy, which impair both your body and brain,
and absolutely unfit you for study, business, or the
proper enjoyment of life ; if you are suffering from these
or similar symptoms you know the cause. Before it is
everlastingly too late, consult this reliable old specialist,
who thoroughly understands your trouble, and can treat
it with unfailing success.
MIDDLE- AGED MEN. There are many of you afflicted
with weak, aching back and kidneys, loss of mental and
physical power and vigor, and not unfrequently accom-
panied by an impairment of the general health. In justice
to this celebrated specialist it can be truthfully stated
that in all such cases of middle-aged men, as well of
young men, his success has been almost invariable.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SUMMER : A WAIL OF THE WEST.
( Written for The Bulletin in the ice-chcst.)
Sky-line a-dim in a shrouding of haziness,
Grass that is withered and yellow and sere,
Limbs that are limp with the languor of laziness,
Palate that pines for the gurgle of beer;
Sun that is glowing, a.ball of vermilion,
Wind that assures us Gehenna lies west,
Grasshoppers, beetles and flies by the billion,
Truly the Dead and the Damned are the Blest.
Hundred and ten in the shade—and deliciously,
Mercury mounting at sinking of sun,
Echoes of curses that bark around viciously,
Felo-de-sefulous thoughts of a gun ;
Sweat that has flowed till your visage is furrowful,
Cattle a-perish wherever you go,
Odour of rabbits that die by the burrowful,
Dolorous dirge from the throat of the crow.
Eyes all a-blink in the plague that has smitten us,
Growls from the boys who were once all a-grin ;
Itch that is token the sandflies have bitten us ;
Tempers as touchy and cranky as Sin ;
Nothing but drought and the whisky to talk about,
Too hot to live or to decently die,
Too hot to sleep or to work or to walk about,
Almost too hot for a loafer to lie.
Dancing horizon of sand-drift and dreariness,
Only asylum—a camp-oven shed ;
Never a song or an outburst of cheeriness,
Only ambition—to drink till you’re dead ;
Dead and content, with no chance of luxurious
Satin-lined crib in the Halls of the Blest,
Glad of a Nest with his Nibs, the Sulphureous
Lord of the Deep, where the Damned are at
Rest!
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
“HAIL TO THE NIGHT.”
Hail to the solemn Night,
Whose stately steps and slow
Move softly, lightly, in the train of Day,
While sunbeams glide away,
And on the sunset glow
Lingers the wan gray light.
Hail to the Night !
Hail to the silent Night!
The wondering stars unfold
Dreams of the vastness of Eternity,
While dim Futurity
Seems nearer than of old,
Wrapped in the slumbering light.
Hail to the Night!
ALICE M'KINON.
IF I WERE YOU.
If I were you, when breaks the morn
Not with a visage long and lorn,
Of sombre hue,
Would I salute the budding day,
But glad with resolution say :
“ I’ll sue my love for yea or nay ”
If I were you.
If I were you, when noon is here
I’d banish all my trembling fear
And Phyllis woo
As only bold true-lovers can,
And have done since the world began,
I’d prove I was at least a man
If I were you.
If I were you, when night is nigh
I would not sit apart and sigh
As lovers do.
Noll would throw away despair
And seek her out —iny lady fair—
And show that I could do and dare,
If I were you.
A.W.
SMITH’S EMILY.
As I was gallopin’ into town
From Simpson’s place, out back o’ the hill,
I met Smith’s Emily drivin’ down
For bran and pollard from Thompson’s mill.
“ Good’ay ! ” says Emily ; “ Day ! ” says I,
An’ pulls my nag up into a walk.
“ An’ ’ow’s the folks at the Errenbri ? ”
(She ain’t a gel as is give to talk).
Oh ! Emily ! Emily Smith !
A lot I care for your kin and kith,
But drivin’ yer cart
You’ve got ’olt of me ’eart,
An’ I long to kiss yer, Emily Smith !
She looks me up, an’ she looks me down,
An’ “ Middlin’, thanks! ” she says, with a
smile.
Her eyes are tender and dark and brown,
An’ keeps a-mockin’ yer all the while.
She plucks a spray from a passing bush,
An’ starts to chew the tip of a twig,
An’ says “ I reckon I’ll make a push ”
Her mouth screwed up an’ her eyes all big.
Ah ! Emily ! Emily Smith !
I don’t quite know what yer does it with ;
But right or wrong,
You can pull me along,
An’ I think yer knows it, Emily Smith.
I says, “ Oh ! wat’s yer ’urry ? ” says I.
Then—“ Don’t yer know as I loves yer so ! ”
Yes ! Just like that, an’ I don’t know why.
But she only laughs, and says “Yes ! I know ! ”
“ Then will yer marry me, Em’ ?” I says.
The cart was stopped ; I ’eld ’er ’and.
I’ll love yer true to my dyin’ days ! ”
She only says, “ Ain’t the sunset grand ! ”
My Emily ! Emily Smith !
If life’s a muddle and love’s a myth
It’s nothin’ to me what the answer may be,
For you’re all I care about, Emily Smith !
NIL.
Ironbark : “A few weeks ago a corres-
pondent told The Bulletin that kangaroos never
sat with their tails between their legs. This is
not correct. The position is a common one when
hunger is satisfied and when the wily flea, or
whatever may be the particular parasite, demands
attention. I have seen both kangaroo and wal-
laby holding up the tail (brought between the legs)
with one paw, and turning it over with the other
in search of the before-mentioned flea in exactly
the same manner as a monkey pursues his investi-
gations.”

London, Saturday.—James Wray, of New bourn
Wales, and George Towns, the Hunter River sculler, both
competed in the Christmas handicap yesterday on the
[Tyne.
The race was won by J. White, who defeated Wray
in the semi-final. Wray had conceded a start of 17sec. to
the winner.
Towns was beaten early in his heat.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 8, 1898.
A Reform Not Worth Having.
The New South Wales general election,
which falls due any time between now and
next August, is to be fought on the question
of the reform of the Legislative Council.
The cry is getting somewhat into the sere
and yellow whoop ; the same Government
fought the last election on the same ques-
tion, and the country gave it a strong
majority with which to carry out the reform,
and the reform never came off. On that
occasion the mending of the Upper House
was demanded because it had rejected the
land and income-tax ; this time it is wanted
because of the rejection of the probate
duties ; so the occasion as well as the war-
whoop is almost exactly the same as it was
three years ago. Still, the cry of Council
reform is so popular that it will probably do
duty a second time. The chief danger in
such a case is that the country, which is an
unreasoning mass at the best and not much
given to detail, will probably forget to ask
what kind of a reform it is to have, and will
assume that alteration and reform must mean
the same thing. Which they certainly don’t.
For instance, Victoria, a good many years
ago, so reformed its Council that it merely
exchanged the devil it knew for the devils
with which it wasn’t previously acquainted.
* * * *
The troubles of the present New South
Wales Council are these : Its members are
unpaid, therefore only rich men are eligible.
They don’t represent the people, and the
only possible excuse for the existence of any
House is that it represents the people. They
hold their billets for life, therefore they are
mostly old men, and old men are almost all
conservative because when men grow old
they want peace and quiet, and they take no
interest in changes because they don t ex-
pect to live to see the results of them. They
can’t be compelled by any lawful process to
do the will of the people. And they are
not even nominated by the people, in the
first place, but by the Government —which
may be just on the verge of being thrown out
because it is at variance with the popular
sentiment.
* • * •
Premier Reid’s proposed reform consists
in the appointment of Councillors for a term
of years instead of for life. Apparently mem-
bers will still be unpaid and rich men will
still be the only ones eligible. They will
still be wholly unrepresentative of the
people. They will still be appointed
without any reference to the people.
Also there will still be no power of dissolu-
tion if they refuse to do the popular will.
The new Council is to be a perpetual body,
the members retiring by batches at fixed
periods—probably one-third of them going
out every two or three years. Therefore, if
the Council puts the people’s Government at
defiance by an almost unanimous vote, as
the present Council has frequently done, the
only remedy will consist in waiting patiently
till the first batch retires in one or two
or three years, and then if the same
Government is still in power—putting men
of a different way of thinking in their place.
This will at least strengthen the Government
minority, though that is about all it will do.
The next proceeding is to wait patiently
another two or three years till a second batch
of fossils falls due, and then if the same
Government is still in office, and is still of
the same way of thinking—two things which
are eminently improbable—it can send up
another "lot, also of its way of thinking.
Then if the first batch hasn’t deteriorated —
which is also eminently improbable, for
democrats deteriorate very fast when they
are created irresponsible tyrants —the mea-
sure will be carried. And if the country
changes its mind about this time, which is
more than likely, for public opinion is
always changing and advancing and taking
new forms, and the Council, which repre-
sents the former way of thinking, refuses to
change its mind also, the only thing to be
done is to sit down patiently and do all the
waiting over again.
* * * *
The proposed “ reformed ” Council, in
fact, may easily prove worse than the unre-
formed one. When the latter resists the
will of the people on some important point,
the Government can dissolve the Assembly
and appeal to the nation on that special
issue, and get a tolerably definite mandate
from the people thereupon. If the mandate
is backed by a large enough majority, it can
generally persuade the Imperial figurehead
of the State to sanction the appointment of
enough new nominees to carry its point.
Generally, however, the Council gives way
without that extreme measure, merely to avoid
having its Sabbath calm disturbed by an
irruption of democratic Huns and Vandals.
The “reformed” Council can peacefully
watch a dozen Assembly dissolutions and
popular mandates, and simply tell the
Government to wait till its chance comes in
the slow procession of the years, and trust to
the Ministry dying in the interim. More-
over, the system by which the Councillors
retire in batches, automatically, at fixed
periods, may enable a dying Ministry to
crowd the Upper House with its nominees a
day or two before its own fall, and the new
Government may come in with a large
majority and a perfectly different policy, only
to find that it will be four or six years before
it can bring the Council in line with popular
opinion.
• • • *
Premier Reid’s theory is that his “ re-
formed ” Council of nominees will be a
valuable “ revising ” body. But it may not
be content to revise ; it will have power to
obstruct also. And the “ revising ” theory
is absurd in any case. If it is intended that
the Council shall merely suggest amend-
ments and let the Assembly accept or reject
them at its pleasure, then it should have no
legislative powers whatever, and in that case
there is no need for any Council. The mere
work of suggestion might be entrusted to the
boss lodge of Freemasons, or the Baptist
Conference, or the Judges, or the Australian
Natives Association ; or anybody and every-
body could take a turn at it and publish
their suggestions in the S.M. Herald. If the
Council is only to suggest the things that the
Assembly agrees with, it is useless, because
the Assembly could do the same thing itself.
If it is also to suggest the things the Assem-
bly doesn’t agree with, it is equally useless,
for the Assembly won’t accept its suggestions.
If it is to be a revising body without power
to revise, it is useless. If it is to be a re-
vising body vith power to revise, that
means that it will have power to insist on
its revisions. Which brings it back to
exactly where it is now. The present Coun-
cil is a revising body with power to insist
on its revisions—and that is exactly what
all the trouble is about.
* * • *
Either there must be no Upper House at
all or there must be one with full power to
legislate—a Legislative House without power
to legislate might as well be replaced by a
wooden image. If there is to be a Second
Chamber, then the only logical course, in a
democratic community, is to make it fully
and absolutely representative of the people ;
the fact that it fully represents the people is
the only excuse for having any Chamber at
all. Then the question arises—in what
other aspect do the people require to be
represented besides the aspect in which the
Assembly already represents them ? It is
no use having the Council elected on the
same system as the Assembly ; that would
only mean that there would be two depart-
ment-trotters to demand the immediate
erection of a culvert at the township of
Smith’s Curse, instead of one as at present.
The one thing to be done with a Second
Chamber is to make it the Chamber that
represents the nation in general. There is
a magnificent opening for such a Chamber.
Neither New South Wales nor any of
the other Australias has ever possessed
anything even distantly resembling it.
In the Lower House there is the
man who represents Kiama, and the one
who represents Pyrmont, and the tin-pot
magnate who is authorised to speak for
Albury, and the uninfluential personage
who possesses the entire confidence of Illa-
warra, or Dandaloo, or Milparinka. There
is a member for every little, desolate town of
GoD-forgotten; but there isn’t one not
even a single one—who represents New
South Wales, or is entitled to speak for the
people of New South Wales. Tbe only place
which is wholly unrepresented in New South
Wales is New South Wales itself. Every
separate bridge and culvert that is wanted
has someone to specially represent it. But,
if something is -wanted which isn’t the cul-
vert of any particular lost-dog township—
something like Federation, which is the pro-
perty of the nation at large—it is nobody’s
special mission to look after it. No one
is the member for the people at large, and
consequently the interests of the people at
large pass almost unnoticed.
• • • •
The province wants two Houses, both
elected by manhood suffrage (or manhood
and womanhood suffrage, as the community
may decide), both consisting of paid repre-
sentatives, with the same tenure and the
same liability to dissolution. The one
should be elected by constituencies after
the fashion of the present Assembly. Even
if the present system has its disadvantages
it is not to be supposed that the electorates
would abandon the privilege of having each
its separate department-trotter, and pro-
bably it is advisable that there should be one
House whose members possess a certain
local knowledge. But the other should be
elected by the people as one constituency, so
that its members shall represent New South
Wales and its interests—not merely Jam-
beroo or Queanbeyan, or the fast asleep old
town of Parramatta. The Federal election
showed that such a system is possible. It
also showed that there is a national interest
that wants representation quite distinct from
the multitude of separate local interests,
when it put Edmund Barton —whom the
constituency system threw out. of Parliament
altogether—at the head of the poll by the
biggest vote ever recorded in Australia. No
separate constituency badly wanted Barton
because, as a hustler after culverts, he is pro-
bably the most inefficient man in the politics
of the Seven Provinces, but New South
Wales in general wanted him badly. The
House which represents New South Wales
in general should also be the one to which
the Ministry is responsible ; in other words,
the present Lower House should be made
the Upper House, and a new Assembly be
constituted on a distinctly national basis.
And if each House consisted of the same
number of members, and each rested on
manhood suffrage, then it would be feasible
to settle deadlocks by letting the two
bodies sit as one Chamber and decide
the disputed point by a joint vote. When
one House represents the people, and the
other, as is the case both with the pre-
sent Council and the proposed “reformed”
Council of Premier Reid, represents
nothing, there is no possible compro-
mise in case of deadlocks. The only way
out is for the House which doesn’t represent
the people to give way to the one that does.
And, if the Council is always to give way,
then there is no compromise ; also there is
no earthly use in having any Council.
* * * *
The Bulletin presents this scheme as
being, in its opinion, the only way out of a
difficulty of many years standing. The
present Council is admittedly a useless and
senile stumbling-block in the way of pro-
gress. The proposed reformed Council of
Premier Reid is the feeble expedient of a
mentally-barren politician following in the
old useless rut because he lacks the courage,
or the intelligence, to get out of it. And
Reid’s reformed Council would be, almost to
a certainty, just about as hopeless and use-
less and unmanageable as the one now exist-
ing. In other words, it would be just about
as unreformed as the one which New South
Wales possesses already, and of which it has
grown so very, very weary.
Trade.
Captain Russell, the Maoriland Tory leader
who has been seven years in a minority, and
whose party, badly licked at three consecutive
elections, is thinking of throwing him overboard
and giving his billet to Stout, was interviewed
the other day in Sydney. It was a long inter-
view', mostly devoted to remarks about the
brightening prospects of the stupid section of
Maoriland Parliament, but, towards the end,
Russeix got off a hint about the policy he in-
tends to adopt should lie ever get into the
office he lias vainly pm-sued so long :
I would like to add that I believe entirely in a policy of
Freetrade. I believe that the protective duties in New
Zealand hamper trade, and are very prejudicial to the
best interests of the colony. A policy of Fr eetrade, given
effect to from time to time as circumstances permit,
would he the best policy for New Zealand, and I believe
it would lead to a very large trade between New South
Wales and New Zealand.
The interesting feature of this deliverance lies
in the last sentence. The inducement to a
policy of Freetrade lies in the fact that it
would probably lead to “a very large trade
with New South Wales. This is the same in-
ducement which the Freetrader always holds
out, and if he can show that his policy, where
adopted, has led to a very large trade, or that
the other policy, where it was adopted, didn t
lead to a very large trade, he thereupon con-
siders his case proved absolutely and beyond
all question. Yet the advantages of a very
large trade are no more self-evident than those
of a very large wen, or carbuncle, or of a term
in a very large gaol. In fact, trade is, in the
main, a fetish.
* * • •
It consists, in the first place, of shifting
something from one place and putting it down
in another, and somebody being paid for the
act of removal, his payment going to increase
the cost of the'article shifted. In this respect
it is analogous to sand-shifting for rations, and
also bears some relationship to professional
cricket, in which a ball is shifted rapidly from
one place to another for a cash consideration.
Sometimes it is profitable, and many other
times it isn’t, but the Freetrader’s logic, which
estimates the prosperity of a country by the
amount of its exports and imports in proportion
to population, takes no account of that.
According to the theory that trade is neces-
sarily an evidence of prosperity, about the
most prosperous places in this world are
Holland, Belgium, Singapore and Hongkong,
which, in their annual commercial returns,
announce more exports and imports per head
than almost any other places on this earth. By
the same criterion the United States, with all
its millionaires and its alleged immense wealth,
is just about one-fourth as rich as Hongkong,
and the average New T Yorker has just
about one-quarter of the wealth of the
half-naked and apparently poverty-stricken
brown man of the Straits Settlements.
On the same basis, the average Westralian
is nearly tw’ice as rich as the Cornstalk and
more than twice as rich as the average English-
man. There are many other strange anomalies,
but the one fact which sticks out conspicuously
is that the Hongkong Chow and the Asiatic
mongrel of Singapore and the Dutchman are
the three boss plutocrats of this world. If they
are not, then the theory that trade is neces-
sarily a proof of prosperity goes broke on the
instant: and if that breaks then the theory
that Freetrade is necessarily better than Pro-
tection because it leads to increased trade goes
broke also. And, in view of these two break-
ages, there remains no sense or reason why
Maoriland should go in for Freetrade merely
because it will lead to increased trade with
Sydney, or with any other place. Of course, if
trade is invariably a proof of wealth, and the
country with most trade is the richest country,
and the nigger of Singapore and the yellow
man of Hongkong are about the richest men on
earth, then Maoriland should go in baldheaded
for Freetrade. But in view of the well-known
condition of the brown man and the yellow man
aforesaid, it would almost seem as if a place
might have an immense trade and be very poor,
or a comparatively small trade and be very
rich, and that it might get poorer as its trade
increased, or richer as its trade fell off, and
other ridiculous things of the same kind.
* * ♦ *
The whole trade theory, so far as it is based
on exports and imports, is a fallacy to begin
with. All that these returns take cognisance
of is the amount of goods which one country
sends to and receives from other countries or
states : the dealings of one part of the country
with another are not reckoned at all. Thus, if
Albury in N.S. W. sends goods three miles to
Wodonga in Victoria —that is trade, and the
transaction appears in the returns of the
commerce of both provinces, and the more
goods are humped back and forward over these
three miles, even if they go at a heavy loss, the
greater, according to the Freetrade logic, is the
prosperity of both provinces. But if ten times,
or a hundred times, the quantity of merchan-
dise were carried 893 miles from Milparinka to
Sydney, or 375 miles from Mildura to Mel-
bourne, at a large profit to everybody concerned,
that wouldn’t be trade. The event wouldn’t
be even mentioned in the returns, and the
philosophy which judges a country by its im-
ports and exports would take no account of it
whatsoever.
* * » *
The relative situations of Britain and Russia
are a fine example of how the trade theory
works out. They are the two greatest empires
on earth; they are nearly equal in size; and their
beginnings were about equally small. One
sprang from the two little kingdoms of Eng-
land and Scotland ; the other from the little
grand duchy of Muscovy. Butt by the acci-
dent of situation, when they started spread-
ing one spread by land in a solid mass,
and the other by sea over hundreds of
separate islands and fragments of continents.
Because Russia is solid it is conveniently go-
verned from one centre, and the empire is called
a single state ; because the British empire isn’t
solid it is governed, as a matter of convenience,
from fifty different centres, and is a multitude
of autonomous or semi - autonomous states.
Therefore, all the goods which Britain receives
from or sends to its empire are called imports
and exports, and are entered in the trade re-
turns ; while all those which the original Mus-
covy sends to its empire are simply called
internal commerce, and are not reckoned at all.
As a result, Freetrade Britain has a very large
commerce, and points to Protectionist Russia,
which has a very small one, as a horrible
example of backwardness. Yet if Britain,
like Russia, reckoned its dealings with
other parts of its own empire as internal,
instead of external, trade, #180,000,000
per annum of its business, as it appears on
paper, would vanish altogether. And if
Russia adopted the British system, its com-
merce, without being any bigger, would in-
crease enormously. Similarly, if Austria fol-
lowed Britain’s example, and called its dealings
with its possessions apart from the original
Austria proper (Hungary, Bohemia, Moravia,
Silesia, Galicia, Transylvania, Carinthia, Bosnia,
Herzegovina, Styria, Croatia, the Tyrol, &c.)
as external commerce, it might rank as one of
the greatest commercial nations on earth with-
out being any more commercial than it is now


And if the United States conceded to its 45
otates, as Britain has done to the separate
States of its dominion, the right to establish
each its own tariff, then all the dealings of one
State with another would be reckoned, as is
case Britain, as “ trade,” and the
U.S. would have a commerce which would “lay
over that of every other country on the earth’s
surface. Simply because it keeps its accounts
differently it has comparatively little “ trade,”
and the British Freetrader, who does his
national book-keeping the other way, says
that the devastating influence of Protection
here shows itself distinctly.
* * » •
At present, Australia has a vast commerce,
and is inordinately proud of the fact. But it is
a subject for painful reflection that, if it fede-
rates, it will immediately thereafter have so
very little commerce that the total will hardly
be w'orth bragging about. Apart from Maori-
land, this country is divided into six States, and
every single article which one sends to or
through another or receives from another, is
reckoned in the commercial returns. When
Broken Hill ore comes round to Illawarra
for treatment, it is first exported from
N.S.W., next it is imported into S.A.
by rail) and then exported from S.A. by
steamer, and then imported into N.S.W.
again ; and, similarly, stores for Broken Hill
may be exported from Melbourne, imported
into S.A., exported out of S.A., and finally im-
ported into N.S. W. By thus reckoning the
same goods over and over again an enormous
“ trade ” is created, and, if Australia were
divided into 29 States instead of only six, it
might easily, with exactly the same amount of
business, have twice as big a trade per head
as any other country in the world. All the
South Queensland produce which finds its sea-
outlet at Sydney or Newcastle, and the Western
N.S. W. and Victorian goods which go out by
Adelaide, and the Riverina trade which reaches
Europe via Melbourne, are counted three times
over in the same fashion. When Federation
abolishes all these separate reckonings, the
trade of Australia, even if it remains the same
in reality, will drop nearly one-half on paper,
and this country, according to the Freetraders’
logic, will fall into miserable insignificance.
* * * *
All of which leads back by a circuitous
course to Captain Russell’s theory that Free-
trade must necessarily be good for Maorilaml
because it w’ill probably increase its external
trade with Sydney. Assuming that trade is a
good thing—and, in the main, it probably is—it
is much more pertinent to ask just here
whether there is any prospect of Freetrade in-
creasing the commerce between Dunedin and
Christchurch, and making a rush of business
along the dirt-road from Auckland to Onehunga.
Also, whether there is any reason to believe it
will accelerate trade between High-street,
Dunedin, and Princes-street, just round the
corner. Russell holds out no such promise,
and yet this kind of commerce, though it
doesn’t appear among the published exports
and imports, is vastly more important than the
long, cumbersome, expensive oversea trade
with Sydney. Also the one is just as much
“ trade ”as the other. To the economists who
hold that external buyings and sellings—the
kind which appear in the trade statistics—
are the one great test of prosperity it is a
solemn reflection that Maoriland might get up
a business of <£500,000,000 a year with Austra-
lia, and then it might federate with this
country, and the above business would cease to
be external commerce, and would appear in the
trade returns no longer, and Maoriland, even
though it bought and sold as much as ever,
would be a poor, wretched place with no com-
merce at all. And, as a still more awful con-
tingency, there is just a far-off chance that the
whole world may some day be combined under
one Government, and be all one State, and then,
no matter if trade is a hundred times more
active than it is now, there will be no trade at
all —in the Foreign shoddy sense—because
every place will be one country and there will
be no foreign country to export to or import
from. Meanwhile, if Maoriland can’t be pro-
sperous without foreign trade, it is consoling to
reflect that it can get there at once by dividing
itself into twenty different States, so that the
same train-load of goods going from Dunedin to
Christchurch w'ill appear in the imports and
exports of perhaps eight States all in the same
day. Then M.L. as a whole might have the
biggest commerce on earth, and the Russell
party would be quite happy.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Two Lost Provinces.
After a long struggle with local geological
problems, it has been decided that there is no
artesian water under the great dry Wimmera
plains of western Victoria, and that, if the
bores went down till they struck perdition, the
situation would be as hopeless as it is now.
Therefore the Age issues its ukase that a huge
irrigation scheme must be set on foot at an
expense, in borrowed money and interest, the
mere thought of which is calculated to make an
economical Treasurer shudder. The situation
is something like this : The population of Vic-
toria, the railways, roads, and public works of
all kinds, are crowded into a narrow space in
the centre of the province. The East is empty
forest; the West is empty plain, and salt and
sand. About one,-third of Victoria is described
on the map as heath and sandhills, and salt
patches and howling arid plains, where the
foliage averages about one blasted gum-tree in
a century. To find room for more population,
it is necessary either to irrigate this shrieking
waste and make it decently cultivable, or
else to open up the other end—the Gipps-
land end —of the province by roads and
railways, and supply the immediate demand
for elbow - room in that direction. Pro-
bably the latter would be the more promising
scheme just now, but, by some unaccountable
kink in the political mind, every Vic. states-
man pins his faith to the sun-and-salt-blasted
mallee. Yet eastern Gippsland is the best-
watered part of Victoria, and one of the best-
watered parts of Australia. It is covered with
dense forests, and the timber alone should be
worth as much as the whole selling price of the
whole mallee wilderness. The land is reason-
ably fertile, and, despite the rugged character
of the country, muoli of it is cultivable ; there
is a fine climate and fine scenery; and the popu-
lation along the principal main-road in Eastern
Gippslaud runs to three families in 70 miles.
On the bye-roads it is less. If any Vic. Govern-
ment would wrestle with the question of carry-
ing the unfinished railway from Bairnsdale
through Orbost to the N.S.W. border, and
would go in for systematic road-construction in
the counties of Dargo, Tambo, and Croajingo-
long, it would easily find room for 50,000 people
in the latter alone, instead of the present 1779.
(Croajingolong is at present a large county
with 420 inhabited dwellings, or about one-
twelfth of a home to the square mile.) The
difficulty is that the railways required to open
it up wouldn’t pay interest on the borrowed
money for many years, and nobody has the
energy or the courage to suggest that
the necessary coin should be raised by
taxation. Also, though the increased land
revenue would probably pay in the end for rail-
roading eastern Gippsland it is necessary to
have the railroads first before the land-revenue
begins to come in. Victoria is losing popula-
tion for want of land to meet the demands of
agriculturists, while one quarter of the pro-
vince is empty and inaccessible for want of rail-
ways, and another quarter ditto for want of
water. Considering all the circumstances this
should be a good opportunity for a heaven-born
financier to come to the front. The question is,
how to get the temporary use of, say, <£500,000,
without interest, for opening up east Gipps-
land, the money to be repaid out of the pro-
ceeds of thesale or leaseof the lands thus opened.
If the State took over the local bank-note issue,
which runs to about .£1,000,000, and kept, say,
<£300,000 in gold to cover the issue, it certainly
looks as if it could have the other <£700,000 at
practically no interest, to spend on opening up
its lost provinces and making them habitable.
Then the proceeds of the experiment would go
to pay off the principal instead of being swal-
lowed up by interest. The country couldn’t, by
doing this, make the paper currency any less
secure than it is now, when it rests largely on
the lOU of various reconswindled banks. It
couldn’t owe the money any more than if it
borrowed it at interest from J. Bull. If any-
thing awful and unforeseen went wrong with
the new system it could always borrow the
money from Bull or somebody else in the old,
wretched, stereotyped way, and put matters
on the old footing. It is practically the same
device which N.S.W. has attempted on a smaller
scale with the Centennial Park and Martin-
place (Sydney) suspense-accounts, the difference
being that in one case the cash was borrowed
without interest out of the trust funds, while
in the other it would be borrowed without in-
terest out of the State paper-money redemption-
fund. The experiment looks worth trying,
anyhow.
Nice, Quiet Saturday Divorces.
During the hearing of the Carruthers-Nor-
ton (N.S.W.) libel case last week it was stated
in evidence that the plaintiff, who is Minister
for Lands in the present Cabinet, had been
divorced by the late Judge Windeyer on a
Saturday morning (when the Court does not
usually sit) under exceptional circumstances.
Said J. G. Leary, chief clerk in Divorce, in
reply to Norton :
“ Was the Divorce Court sitting at this time?”—“ No,
I don’t think it was.” “Who were present?”—“The
Judge, the tipstaff, and others.” “Was the Associate
present?”—■“ Ido not think so.” “Was there any clerk
of the court present ?”—“ I don’t think so.” “Who ad-
ministered the oath?” —“The tipstaff, named Byrne.”
“No reporters there?” —“No.” “Any notice given of
the case in the press ?”—“ No.” “ Any notice posted up
in the precincts of the court?” —“ Nofthat is not usual.”
“ Could the public have known that the case was on ?”
“No.” “How long did the case last? Under half-
an-hour.” “Did you see any report of the case in the
daily papers? “ No.” “ Would you say that the decree
was obtained in the same regular public manner as an
ordinary divorce ? “ No. The fact of the case being
taken on Saturday and not being reported makes it un-
usual.” To Mr. Garland : “The original application was
that the case should be heard on Tuesday morning. It
was witness’ suggestion that it should be taken on Satur-
day. It was not the practice now to publish cause lists.
Looking at the manner in which the divorce had been ob-
tained, and the absence of any notification in the press,
he would hardly say that anyone entitled to intervene
would have been in a position to do so.
And said Albert Byrne, tipstaff to the late
Judge Windeyer :
The case was not heard with closed doors, but it was
not conducted in the usual way—that is, the Judge’s
Associate and the press were absent, and there was no one
in the public seats. Witness had seen the late Mr.
Justice Windkyer’s notes on the case, and it was there
set down that the respondent admitted adultery. To
Mr. Norton : “ A law reporter was round on the Saturday
morning that the case was heard, and he asked witness if
anything was going on. Witness evaded the question.
He turned away quickly, as though someone were calling
him.”
Said Mr. Carruthers :
“ The case was heard in open court. The court was as
open as this is to-day, and no attempt was made by me,
or on my behalf, to have any concealment of the trial.”
Mr. Garland : “ Did you, or anyone on your behalf, apply
to have the case heard on Saturday morning ?” Witness :
“No.” “Was any' application made to have the case
tried in camera ? ” “No; I was informed that no such
application was made. I saw persons in the court while
the case was on, and saw the court door opened. Some
person looked in and went out again. A decree nisi was
granted for four months, and on this point I felt
aggrieved, for it was usual to make it for either two or
three months. The rule absolute was granted in open
court, and I saw paragraphs in the papers about that time
in which the matter was reported.
The Bulletin, of course, accepts Mr. Car-
ruthers’ word ; but it is nonsense to say that
the proceedings were not unusual, and the
natural inference from the unusual character of
the proceedings is that, either on account of the
position of the parties or for some other reason,
the Judge varied the ordinary course of justice
as he would not have varied it for John Smith,
laborer. And Mr. Carruthers’ case does not
stand alone in the list of nice, quiet Saturday
divorces—in which, with or without the know-
ledge of the chief parties, the late Judge
Windeyer colluded to suppress publicity and
make things as easy as possible. Without ex-
pressing any opinion on the merits of any par-
ticular case, The Bulletin affirms the prin-
ciple that the nice, quiet, Saturday divorce
is a very undesirable departure from the
ordinary course of public justice. Mr.
Leary’s declinature to affirm “ that anyone
entitled to intervene would be in a position to
do so ”is sufficient proof of this. For a great
and overwhelming advantage of publicity of
justice is that it helps to make better
justice. Justice awarded by a tribunal
which sits secretly, in deference to a priori
representations, is—even when the Judge
has the very best intentions—markedly liable
to be perverted. And to enforce this sufficient
consideration come a host of others depending
upon the commonplace that all men should be
held equal before the law ; and that it is wrong
and pernicious for any Judge to extend to par-
ties who come before him peculiar favors or
peculiar privileges on account of personal
friendship or social position, or for any similar
reason. The publicity of justice is the com-
munity’s best guarantee of pure justice ; and
The Bulletin hopes never again to hear that
fish has been set apart from flesh in N.S.W.
Divorce Court.
The Devil—Australia—the Deep Sea.
That is just where Australia is. If she cuts
the British connection probably very few
years would pass before Russia, or France, or
Germany, or all together—under some specious
pretext, or without any pretext —would want
to annex some unoccupied portion or portions
of her territory. Tilings in 1897 are very
different from things in 1776, and the Aus-
tralian Republic would have nothing like the
chance to grow unmolested to greatness that
the American Republic enjoyed. The earth
has grown very small since 1776, and the Powers
have grown very large, and very greedy
for territory, and have adopted many of
Britain’s very unpleasant w'ays of getting
it. And, within the last quarter-century,
the chances of a small people with a very desir-
able country being left in peace and quietness
have not improved. There is not only Russia
to reckon with—her fine, large, expansive policy
is unchanged—but France and Germany, and,
in a less degree, Italy, have learnt the histori-
cal lesson so well taught by Britain, that you
cannot build a large pyramid on a small base.
Colonial extension has come to be considered a
necessary of life for any Great Power that
would remain great; a very simple pro-
portion sum shows France and Germany,
for example, that if Britain, Russia, and
the United States continually increase in terri-
tory and population, while the other two prac-
tically stand still, in a few years they will be
left hopelessly in the ruck of second or third-
rate Powers, no matter how perfect their mili-
tary organisation. So France and Germany
have of necessity developed an itching palm for
trifles in the way of continents and islands, and
it is pretty clear that, without Britain at her
back, thinly - settled Australia would run
a very great risk of attack. On the
other hand, with Britain at her back,
Australia, in a sense, takes upon her
infantile shoulders the whole staggering bur-
den of that Empire upon which the sun,
etc. Britain’s enemies immediately become
her enemies ; and Britain is probably the best-
hated nation on earth, and is also probably the
nation with the greatest opportunities for fall-
ing-out with other nations, for she has grabbed
so much of the earth’s surface that another
nation can hardly put its foot down anywhere
without treading on British property and es-
tablishing a promising grievance to make war
about. And when Britain conflicts with the
U.S. in Venezuela, or with Germany in Africa,
or with Russia in Turkey, or with half-a-dozen
other States in half-a-thousand other places,
Australia is at once exposed to a very imminent
risk of seeing her commerce destroyed, and her
cities attacked, and her country laid waste, and
her population killed and wounded—all in Bri-
tain’squarrel. To separate from Britain would
make this country liable to attack. Not to
separate from Great Britain also makes it very
liable to attack. So there is the Devil—and there
is the Deep Sea—and there is Australia between
thetwo. And, by way of showinghow thoroughly
she realised what her position would be in case
of the British war which may break out at any
moment, Australia is split up into half-a-dozen
jealous provinces, with varying railway-gauges
and railway-systems ; and she pays a few' hun-
dred soldiers, and two or three thousand half-
trained militia, armed with obsolete or not-up-
to-date and without ammunition
enough to fight a single campaign ; and she
builds fortifications which an enemy could walk
round with the greatest ease. The Bulletin
wants Australians who realise that any day
they may.be compelled to fight for their exist-
ence to do their utmost to bring about (1) Fede-
ration ; (2) Compulsory Military Training for
Able-Bodied Males; (3) A uniform railway-
gauge so that her army can be quickly concen-
trated when necessary. And the governments
should have sense enough to start their own
arms and ammunition factories at once.
Sydney’s Art-Gallery Trustee and Society-of-
Artists President, Julian Ashton, has been
getting further tangled up in his tw'o capacities.
As trustee, it is part of his duty to say whether
the Art Gallery ought to buy the pictures of
the horrid other-fellows in the rival Art
Society ; and Julian now says that to this ex-
tent he won’t do his duty—he’ll see it varnished
first. Ashton is a very decent fellow, and a
very decent artist, and he’s done a great deal to
keep N.S.W. Art a-moverin’ along—all which
makes his obstinate exhibition of himself in a
false position the more pitiful. For it’s beyond
dispute that many artists w'ould now be as glad
to see him resign his trusteeship as they were
to see him take it—in the days when the famous
round-robin went about. That round-robin
was essentially a selfish document—the idea
was that a practising artist would buy prac-
tising artists’ pictures for the Gallery—and
now r that Ashton says he won’t help to buy any
more pictures by other fellows—let alone his
own—where, if you please, is the use of him?
Besides, his case is even w'orse than that, for some
artists seem to fancy he prevents the other trus-
tees from buying. Here is a current artistic duo-
logue resurrected show by show' with some detail-
alterations: “ Well, Palette, so the trustees
W'ouldn’t buy your ‘ Sea-Serpent of the
Pacific ’? ” “ No, Mahlstick, they wouldn’t.”
“ Well, you can’t say Ashton had a hand in it
this time, for he didn’t take part in deciding
any purchase.” “No”—venomously—“but
the walks round with the others ; and I
know he didn’t say a word, but when he passed
vtiy picture he shrugged his shoulders,
and that was enough.” Of course, this is non-
sense ; but the point is that the local-artistic
mind is prone to believe it isn’t nonsense by a
long tube. Why won’t Mr. Ashton take Scrip-
tural advifce, and “avoid all appearance oi
evil ? ”
S.S.: “In my opinion the Victorian elector has
come to the conclusion that theTußNEßGovern-
ment has about run its course, and the said
elector is casually looking forward, to the day
when that Government will die quietly and he
done with. This is a large conclusion, but I
seem to hit up against it everywhere ; it is ‘in
the air.’ The elector does not know why the
Turner Government will die ; it just occurs to
him that the death is inevitable, and that is
enough. He has no idea who will take Tur-
ner’s place ; he cannot think of anyone better,
if he thinks about the matter at all; and possi-
bly he is satisfied that the successor will be
ever so much worse ; but that is beside the
question— Turner has got to go. Some mys-
terious dispensation of Providence hasprovided
for that, and it is vain to fly in the face
of Providence, anyhow, even in protest
against D. Gillies. How these conclusions get
about, heaven only knows. This one has cer-
tainly not been propagated by the press. Pro-
bably they are epidemic. Governments have
been abolished before because electors woke
one fine morning with an impression that
the abolition was. fixed and inevitable, like
a solar eclipse, and that, in my opinion, is
the trouble with Turner and co. The elec-
tor does not want the Turner Govern-
ment abolished, but he does not protest,
because it seems to him that that would be a
vain and foolish proceeding, much the same as
if he were impelled to get up in public and
speak a few words on the advisability of post-
poning the next comet. Talk about free will—
‘ I don’t believe there ain’t no sicli thing.’ ”
Some recent paragraphs concerning the N.S. W.
Royal Commission’s report on mine accidents evi-
dently disturbed somebody, for, after being
quietly withheld for several weeks, the docu-
ment was suddenly produced in the Assembly.
After all, there was some reason for keeping it
in a pigeon-hole, though; for Judge Murray,
the Commissioner, after fully considering the
matter, concludes that two of the most impor-
tant regulations (those relating to the liability
of owners) and three-fourths of the general
rules for the conduct of mining operations, are
ultra vires on account of the insufficient
authority to frame regulations conveyed by the
Mining Act of’74. The Bulletin, basing its
opinion partly on higher Court rulings, urged
long ago that the regulation which made the
occurrence of an accident in a mine prima facie
evidence of the owner’s neglect or default was
simply a delusion and a snare to the workman,
and so the Royal Commission now reports.
Moreover, it reports in effect that, as there are
no general rules that are any good,
the owner can run his show as he likes, and
then tell the inspector to join the fallen angels.
When it was discovered that a bank or two was
rocky, Parliament lost no time in putting a Bill
through. Now that a Royal Commission has
discovered that pretty well all the mining regu-
lations which are supposed to protect the
32,000 men engaged in metal mining in N.S. W.
are worse even than rocky, Parliament will—
now, what will Parliament do?
4.
A number of Vic. municipalities are urging an
increase in tobacco duty as a means of raising
revenue for charity purposes, and there is not,
nor is there likely to be, a whisper against the
injustice of such an act. We have become ac-
customed to the stigma which inferentially or
otherwise attaches to the weed, and nine out of
ten people, albeit smokers themselves, will (ell
you that tobacco, being “ a luxury,” should
bearaheavy tax. Justso! But,then, tobaccoisn’t
a luxury. In the average home it is a necessity.
A tax upon tobacco is a tax upon almost every
household, and a heavy tax at that. But the
pipe-lover is a crushed and non-resistant animal.
He represents an overwhelming majority
amongst men, but he represents it stealthily—
he keeps an apology for his evil habit on tap.
He will stand the abuse of men who can’t
smoke, and of women who would if they could.
He makes no defence; he feels no anger nor
sorrow—simply doesn’t care a rap. At home,
he sits in the kitchen, and makes himself as
small as possible, and takes the abuse of a very
small woman in a chastened spirit, and spits
furtively into the fireplace or through the
cracks in the floor. He likes the cracks best.
He is a tame animal; but he has possibilities.
Possibilities ! He is numerous enough to make
smoking compulsory and part of the school
curriculum, if he were roused, and to abolish
the tobacco and cigar duty, and lay a heavy
tax on non-smokers, and to demand that the
smoker shall be subsidised out of the proceeds.
Dear Bulletin,— What is “the unimproved
value ” of land that so many people talk so much
about ? It has never been defined. A piece of
land has a value now, as it is. Thai I can
understand and define with some accuracy.
Now strip it of obvious improvements, and re-
duce it to a surface of earth without fences,
vegetation, or buildings. It has a second value
—but it is not the unimproved value, by a long
way. To get at that you must remote its arti-
ficial environment of roads, railways, bridges
—even if the nearest railway is 50 ifiiles away
it is an improvement to my particular piece of
land, for land within 50 miles of a railway is
of more value than land on a continent where
there are no railways at all; and the same logic
applies to reads and bridges. Also, you must
remove the artificial improvements of towns,
cities, ocean steamers, posts, telegraphs, and
you must shift the white man off this continent,
for the white man was an improvement which
made itself more or less felt on the value of
every yard of land in all the Seven Provinces.
Then you must grow the primeval bush and the
primeval ’possum again, and leave Australia in
absolute possession of the primeval nigger ; and,
having thus eliminated all improvements, you
get to the real unimproved value of land for the
first time. And it is nil. There is no such
thing as an unimproved value.— lonotus.
