At Sydney Lyceum, two freaks, in the
shape of a dancing bear and a soprano-voiced boy,
have been inserted into the cast since opening
night, and the gambols of the large, spotty-yellow
cat have been curtailed, to allow of more extra
turns by the rest of the co. The shining light of
the piece is Daisy Chard, as Bobolink, a grey-and-
pink striped sprite, whose stage-boyishness is tem-
porarily eclipsed in a nautical rig-out; she is gay
and cheeky, and the gallery shouts with joy when
she appears. Foley and Coughlan, as the two
Premiers —Reid and Turner, alias Hobson and
Jobson —are as melancholy as if they had wandered
out of an Ibsen drama. They are broken-spirited
beings ; the fact that they are dressed in the D.T.
and the Age respectively can’t hearten them up.
These two blighted politicians are bossed by a pic-
turesque gr-een-and-red demon, who rides in the
whirlwind and directs the storm generally. He has
a fine entry from among the canvas waves in the
wreck scene, in which the ship containing all the
principals is lost, and H. Shine, after yearning
loudly for a life-saving pig, walks ashore. Edith
Barrow, as one of the ship’s company, is good to •
look at, but inarticulate and pallid. The captain
of the Lively Polly is probably the tallest and
whitest mariner afloat. She’s over a fathom from
the top of her white cocked hat to the heel of her
white shoes. The bear calls for no special remark,
but when he wrestles with his dusky leader, the
sympathies of the audience are unanimously with
the native, hugged tightly in that vast, warm,
woolly grasp.
• • • •
The audience is largely juvenile, and this
family journal can conscientiously recommend the
Lyceum show as a great night out for children who
are not too particular. Fanny Liddiard sings two
or three fairly catchy ditties, but, like all the shows
now running, the piece is quite wanting in a popu-
lar song—a lay that is taken at once to the great
heart of the people and whistled on its lips. The
harlequinade, wedged in before the glittering
transformation scene, is as old-fashioned, and
sausagey, and red-hot-pokery as the most con-
servative child could wish. With a little more
Daisy and much less Hobson and Jobson, the
Lyceum panto, might have a useful career before
it; but there are things about it which are better
buried in oblivion.
* * * *
The “Babes in the Wood” prosper
greatly at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), and the
methodical drunkenness of Uncle maintains a very
steady spirit level. Uncle hasn’t breathed one
sober breath since the date of last week’s notice;
in fact, he is about the most consistent rum-
breather on record. The “ Babes ’’can’t be said
to have shaken down after the first week as most
pantomimes do ; that magnificent spectacle is one
of the few pantomimes that never required
shaking. In fact, it was a perfectly-shook spec-
tacle from the first. The one fact about it which
keeps striking the public in the eye with the biff
of an election egg is the grievous waste of talent.
The spectacle of clever and pretty Lily Titheradge
with nothing to do except be a bushed offspring
and a lost infant in the timber looks like a reckless
throwing away of valuable girl. So far, nobody
has written that clever local song that the panto,
wants so badly, but perhaps when the lamentable
intoxication of Uncle Bert Gilbert begins to pall,
and the spectacle of that relative taking arms
against a sea of gin has lost some of its freshness,
the management intends to come in with a lyrical
effort that will wreck the Reid Government and
make Carruthers wish he had never been born. If
it produces both these effects all manner of people
will rise up and call it blessed—and then fall down
again, unless they have the luck to get a good hold
of the bar-counter first.
* * **
“ Charley’s Aunt ” will lead her troublous
existence in skirts at Sydney Criterion some little
time longer. The co., which was going very well
when last this brilliant journal felt called upon to
make some remarks about it, is now going still
better. The Stephen Spettigue of George Carey
is probably even an improvement on the last
Spettigue ; Carey is an artist who does many
things well, and never does anything very badly.
The girl element of the show has a lot to recom-
mend it; Misses Madge Corcoran, Florence Poole
and Manifield are a clever and very pretty trio,
though there is comparatively little for them to
do, and Elsie Carew is a fine artist at all times.
It is always a comforting thing to meet with a
co. which has no visible tail to it even among the
humblest characters, and if there is a tail to
Thornton’s co., that able manager has evidently
wrapped something round it and put it carefully
away out of sight where it can’t be trodden upon.
* * * *
“ Jack the Giant-killer ” is doing its best
to live up to its pretentions as the most pictorially
glittersome pantomime of the year, and it meets
with reasonable success so far. Addie Conyers,
having recovered from the injury to her small,
snow-white foot, now personally mangles the
giant, and though Miss Haytor, during the prin-
cipal’s absence, conducted a very capable campaign
against that large, meaty monster, still everybody
is pleased to see Miss Conyers back again. The
show has been compressed a little, so that people
living in marine suburbs don’t have to swim for
home as they did on the opening nights, and
what was taken off the intervals seems to have
been added on to the giant—at least he looks
like it.
* * « *
Harry Rickards has thrown himself per-
sonally into the gap at the Tivoli, where his wild,
sad song “ Never let yer Donah go upon the
Styge ” makes the gallery shed tears of sentiment
mingled with tobacco every night, and surreptiti-
ously wipe its eye with its coat-tail. The per-
sonal popularity of Rickards is a truly overwhelm-
ing circumstance. Probably no one ever mistook
him for a nightingale, nor did he ever make the
laurels of Sims Reeves totter on his brow, yet
when ’Arry lifts up his voice to explain why your
donah should be kept as far away from the styge
as possible the joy of a Tivoli audience is un-
speakable. Perhaps the reason why this paper
fails to understand it is that it is a cold, unemo-
tional rag which doesn’t care whether its own
donah goes on the stage, or the top of the Post-
office tower, or any other public elevation.
* * * *
The remarks which were made last week
about Fitzgeralds’ circus may be considered as
still in force. Nothing has happened within the
past seven days to impair this paper’s good opinion
of that meritorious show. Mademoiselle le Blonde
and La Belle Rose and the Alma Sisters—the
ladies who earn their humble but honest crust on
the precarious high wire—and the rest, have done
nothing in the interval to cause this journal to
withdraw its moral support from them, and it
especially desires the elephant and the lion to
understand that its views continue favorable. The
exceedingly trim appearance of the back part of
the latter animal, by the way, gives rise to an im-
pression that he is shaved. Unreliable legend has
it that he was put under an anaesthetic and was
barbered and had his tail shampooed, and, when
he revived and discovered the outrage, he was so
crushed that he has been a perfectly harmless
beast ever since. Anyhow, this is mere specula-
tion.
* * * *
“ Claudian,” atMelb. Princess’s, has been
a beautiful object-lesson in summer clothing these
hot nights, nevertheless the wandering Roman
didn’t make much headway against the ther-
mometer. “Claudian” finishes on Friday (7th).
If the weather will permit Holy Clement’s sultry
curse to make itself felt, the last appearances of
that vengeful ecclesiastic should be liberally pat-
ronised. On Saturday the Wilson Barrett co.
will show what it can do with “ The Manx-
man,” an adaptation of Hall Caine’s novel (by
W.B. aforesaid), which, on the authority of critic
Archer, is a better drama than “The Silver
King.” Also the man who wrote the story affirms
that Wilson Barrett fulfils his ideal of the hero.
* * * *
“Robinson Crusoe,” at the Bijou, is a
small and active entertainment that owes a
good deal to the chief toilers. Two or three
ordinary pantomime dazzles dropped at ran-
dom upon the arid desert of dialogue might
have saved the Bijou spec, from any risk of
failure. Willie and Amy Gourlay are bright,
nimble artists, Miss Lucy Fraser is a broth of a
principal bhoy, and The Bulletin’s old friends,
Martyn Hagan and John Forde, are learned in
the art of cheerful fooling under difficulties.
“ Robinson Crusoe ” is played for all it is worth,
and Melbourne, at time of writing, seems disposed
to regard the matter with a kindly eye.
* * * *
“Dick Whittington” was pruned and
pulled together last week, and the business out-
look for the Royal pantomime is promising. It
isn't a specially gorgeous production of “ Whit-
tington,” but there are many dozens of men,
women, and children employed in pushing the
ancient fiction along, and Some of the girls who
do duty for angels in John Hennings’s transforma-
tion scene are as much like heavenly bodies as
anybody could fairly expect. Moreover, the
transformation scene, apart from plump female
accessories, is a gaudy success of its kind,
now that the machinery has been greased
and the Realms of Bliss are in proper working
order. Maggie Moore and Harry Roberts,
assisted by Miss Laura Roberts, perpetrate the
most part of the Royal panto., and fire off some
local allusions with considerable energy. They
don’t sing catchy songs —there are no smart new
songs on the market just at present—neither do
they dance divinely, but they bring an air of
briskness into the proceedings, anyhow. A clever
clog-dancing turn is supplied by the two Mintons,
and the show terminates at 11 p.m. Matinees of
“Dick Whittington” on Wednesday and Satur-
day until further notice.
** * *
Of the Three Hegelmanns, at Melb. Opera
House, this family journal is now ready to take
oath that they are the Greatest Gymnasts who
have staggered the Southern Hemisphere thus far.
If they have any superiors elsewhere, the end of
all things is probably due. The Hegelmanns
(made in Germany, the home of modern gym-
nastics) perform on and from horizontal bars a
series of aerial feats more marvellous than the
deeds ordinarily accomplished from a flying
trapeze. The apparent ease with which one
Hegelmann hurls himself through space and grips
the hands of a Hegelmann who hangs head-down-
wards from the furthermost bar is a matter for
the audience’s calm reflection after it has left the
theatre. At the same time, it may as well try to
comprehend how it is possible for a gymnast to
loose his hold of the hands he was hanging to,
twirl himself three times around in mid air, and
then resume his former hold without having
dropped into the net meanwhile. The Hegelmanns
are great, beyond all question, but it seems im-
possible to work an act of this kind to the best
effect in a theatre.
** * *
Dan Barry’s four-act shocker, “A Gilded
Sin,” is filling a large portion of the Alexandra
with holiday attendances. Nobody can quite fill
the Alexandra nowadays, and manager Barry
seems to be the one and only person who can put
its vacant spaces in a distinct minority. “ A
Gilded Sin ” is an awful literary outrage, intro-
ducing to notice a reverend vicar whose wife fled
from him long ago on account of his sermons.
When they meet again the lady is driving across
the stage in a pony-chaise, and the mud from her
chariot-wheels splashes the old man’s white
choker, nevertheless the canker-worm of re-
morse is gnawing at her heart, and she
presently makes arrangements (in a large hat
with bold, unremorseful trimmings) to save her
forgotten daughter from a life of shame. As an
illustration of how female virtue gets undermined
by moral precepts in vicarages, “A Gilded Sin”
is somewhat disquieting to the orthodox mind.
** * *
Harmston's Circus continues to brave the
elements in the neighborhood of Prince’s Bridge,
and a few occasional shows in the city have seized
Xmas by the forelock.
At Poverty Point.
Melb. : “ A Maorilander informs me that the worthy
and ample Mr. Love, of Harmston's circus, began life in
the Dunedin theatre, selling oranges, like Nell Gwynne.
He has been telling an interviewer of circus shifts.
For instance, going' over from Australia to Java, in an
eighty-ton schooner, the horse-feed ran out, and for eleven
days the steeds had to be maintained on chopped old
bamboo straw, taken out of chairs and other furniture,
and soaked in jam. I remember Barlow losing his whole
troupe of ponies, dogs and monkeys, in an Oriental ship-
wreck, but in a few months he had another.”
Terriss, the lately-murdered actor, in early life played
in farces with G. S. Titheradge. He died a wealthy man;
his salary for years being £BO a week. As a speculator he
was highly successful, his best deals being in London
hotel property. One of his investments was the Market
House Hotel, Covent Garden, London, a house which
never closes its doors.
N. : “The important secessions from the Salvation
Army in Australia are through Mr. Herbert Booth’s
Hebraic divagations into the realm of money-making com-
monsense. He is a splendid showman. On Christmas
Sunday afternoon he opened his monster tabernacle circus
tent in Studley Park. Alongside was a big holy restau-
rant, doing a prodigious striotly-cash business, especially
in drinks, on a salamander day; also a large traffic in
sixpenny hymn-books, which were as indispensable as
the food and drink. Then came Sunday night, with a
combination of exciting illuminated circuses, firefly
weather, the romantic Yarra purling by, the hilly forest
at hand, splendid brass-band playing, frenzied franticisms
immediately opposite the Lunatic Asylum, the brakes
fluttering with muslin, gingerbeer-eorks popping, cash
tinkling in the tills. I understand the financial results
were simply splendid.”
Colonel Hicks, of Harmston’s circus, the old gentle-
man who looks like an American Jew and talks through
his nose by reason of both races, is now on his third visit
to Australia. He came here first in charge of the Georgia
Minstrels, those black-and-tan melodists, or shaved ele-
phant-skin gentry, who so mashed the women of Hobart
and various other places. His second visit was with
the party of whom Pope and Sayles are industrious
remnants.
Melb. : “ Our pubs, are broad, stark, staring open all
Christmas Day and Good Friday, to which the world of
Goshen does not object, though it keeps the theatres
rigidly closed on both these days. Allow me to suggest
that the Pantomime might be given on Christmas night,
without substantial offence to anyone.”
Paper Cap. : “ About the times Whittington has been
done, as Christmas Panto., in Melbourne. The first was
at the Royal, in 1857, when Fanny Morgan, afterwards
known as Mrs. F. M. Phelps, made such a hit as Dick,
and Harwood made his panto, debut, also the Leopolds.
About sixteen years ago “ Whittington” was the Christ-
inas piece at two Melb. theatres, the Royal and Princess.
George Smithson took Dick at the Royal, and Annie Tay-
lor at the Princess’s under Fred Marshall’s management.
Then there was the Royal production again with Miss
Billee Barlow. Nor does this, I think, exhaust the num-
ber of times Whittington has been given at the Royal.
Robinson Orusoe, the Bijou subject this Christmas, has
been once before driven at that theatre, once at the Opera
House, and twice at the Royal, Harwood taking Robinson
the first time, and Miss Johns the second.”
The friends of Miss Marie Boucicault, who has been
going to distinguish herself anytime during the past seven
years, state that she will “add another to the long list of
Australian successes ” this year. She would, it seems,
“ have come before' the London public last season as a
singer but for her indifferent health.” Miss Boucicault
may be all that her friends protest, but we seldom or never
hear of a great singer suffering from bad health. , A
strong constitution is essential to vocal triumphs.
Composer Lecocq wrote very little music for Nellie
Stewart’s part in “The Scarlet Feather,” and her inter-
polated numbers by Lionel Monckton were not received
with much genuine enthusiasm. Nellie'seems to leave
her luck behind her when she crosses the sea. Given an
effective character and plenty of charming music, she
would hardly have failed in London. ,
Miss Maud Jeffries is'strikingly like Mary Anderson.
George Ridgway, of Melb'. Royal pantomime, caine here
over 30 years ago as star, of the Len.ton troupe, intro-
ducing the first performance of the flying trapeze.
Barry Sullivan audaciously'got together a party of half a-
dozen Melb. fellow's, named them the Leotard troupe, and
they did all the feats of the Lentons. No nets were used.
The double trapeze and ladder in the air were done right
up by the roof of the theatre. The Lentons and Ridgway
were at the Melb. Haymarket. Horatio Nelson, of this
troupe, made himself suddenly famous 1 by falling into the
stalls one night. He escaped unhurt, and so did the man
he fell upon—which latter is an unusual circumstance.
The Elder Conservatorium of Music (in connection with
Adelaide University), which is under the supervision of
Professor Ives, has secured- Herr Heinicke as teacher of
the violin and conductor of the orchestra. Another Herr
will dominate the pianoforte, and some more Herr is to
teach the ’cello, violin and piano.
A Melbourne weekly made a bad mistake in stating
that an infant son of tenor Newbury had inet with
a serious accident. Mr. and Mrs. Newbury are childless.
The display of mummeric grief at Terriss’s funeral was
doubtless ridiculous enough. Consider the size of the
opportunity. A really popular and good-looking actor
cut down in his “ prime,” as the purfeshun would call it,
by an assassin’s dagger at the very door of his gaudy
workshop. An artist done to death on the threshold of
his—er—studio. One can picture the fraternity posing in
attitudes of heroic resignation, particularly a group of
melodrama principals, overcoated, fur-collared, and silk-
mufflered, seeming to be saying to themselves, “ To such
a fate all such as us may come, but no mattah, we must
play on and feah not! ”
Melbourne pantomine references to poor old Graham
Berry’s pension are in the worst of taste, although
evidently intended to be sympathetic. Tub- Bulletin
warns tlie aged politician against going to hear himself
mentioned as a man “ on his uppers ” who neglected to
make provision against a rainy day.
Of a skeleton cable message received by the Melbourne
dailies one day last week, the Age made an item to the
effect that Miss Dudley had made a successful appearance
in “Cinderella” in a London theatre, while the Argus
reading was to the effect that Mr. Dudley’s new play,
“ Cinderella,” had been immensely successful in London.
All famous Australians are made in London.
Godfrey’s song, “Their heads nestled closer together,”
probably died with poor Andy Blanchard, who collapsed
whilst singing it. The management of one of the Xmas
pantomimes wanted the bushed infants to sing it before
thev proceeded to die, but they emphatically refused,
saying they would sooner throw up their engagements.
One country person in town during the holidays
created some excitement in the stalls of a.Sydney theatre,
when the lights were turned down, by striking matches
during the performance to look at his programme, and
dropping them about the floor without putting them out.
After nearly every lady in his vicinity had been nearly
set alight the usher threatened to put the bushy out,
but refrained when the uncouth monster arose and offered
to fight him for the takings.
Calcutta Statesman remarks, in a patronising tone, of
the Brough company’s first appearance in “ Sowing the
Wind : ” —“ How a colonial company would treat so
difficult a play was an anxious question. The Brough
combination come with a sterling reputation from a land
where the critical faculty has, however, but little chance
of guidance from any ideal but its own, and a painful
crudity would have been more a disappointment than a
surprise. The crudity, however, was not there. The
company which left London, close upon the success of the
play, to tour in the English provinces did nothing better
than the Broughs and did some things infinitely worse.”
The unsophisticated critics of this demd barbarous land
ought to feel very grateful to the Calcutta johnnie for
assuring them that the Brough combination in India is
equal to an English co. touring the pwovinces—the more
especially as the co. now in India isn’t quite as strong as
the original B. and B. co., which earned the “sterling
reputation” aforesaid. Brough plays Titheradge’s old
part, MTntyre is promoted to the important character of
Watkins, little Wilfred Shine is Dick Cursitor, and Plim-
mer takes Cecil Ward’s place. They all give complete
satisfaction to the Statesman censor, howevah. He finds
only the small defect that there is “ very little regard for
the period ” about the acting, except in the “ specially
noteworthy performance of Mr. M. Majeroni as Lord
Petvvorth —a figure always within the period, and an
admirable character study.”
Melba, who has now definitely promised to visit Aus-
tralia, has secured Douglas Powell, who was out here
before, as her baritone.
Melb. : “ The Captive Balloon, at the Exhibition, takes
people up 1200 feet. Hitherto the highest Melbourne
point attainable was the Exhibition dome, about 150
feet.”
Rudyard Kipling has given Olga Nethersole the right to
dramatise his “ Light that Failed.”

COLUMN
jh e Australian Star remarks that Premier
lteid’s financial fakes and dodges encf with the annexation
of the £198,000 or so at the credit of the Education trust
v? I ], The Bulletin respectfully bets that they don’t — if
Keid remains in office. The next thing to be done is
obviously to point out that there are never any claims on
heal Property Assurance Fund, and to pass an
Act that the said fund (£156,000) be put into revenue, and
that, if any claims should arise, they be paid out of
revenue. This would mean that Reid would have the
n « 0 * l 's successors would have the claims —or most
of them. After that, there is £450,000 waiting to be
annexed out of the Superannuation Fund. The plain and
logical course in that case is to point out that the fund is
utterly and hopelessly insolvent—it is admitted that, in-
stead of £450,000, it should amount to nearly £3,000,000 to
meet the liabilities reasonably expected—and that, unless
something is done, the just claims of many pensioners
will have to be repudiated, and the State will be a
defaulter. And, as default would be obviously dis-
graceful and immoral and utterly impossible, the way
out is for the Treasury to come to the rescue, and assume
all the future obligations of the fund, and, as there would
tuen be no need for a separate fund, the simplest process
sfould be to put the miserable £450,000 into the Treasury.
Then Reid would have that also, and his successors would
have to pay the pensions. Seeing that Premier Reid has
made a beginning by annexing one trust fund, there is
no earthly reason why he should stop there. The Real
Property Assurance balance and the Superannuation
money (over £600,000 in all) are waiting to be grabbed by
any Treasurer who can get his majority to pass the neces-
sary bill.
* * * m '
The N.S. W. revenue for December, 1897
is £21,895 better than the figure for December,
1896. Even with this, however, the Cornstalk national in-
come for the first half of the financial year (July-Decem-
ber, 1897) is £201,327 less than that for the corresponding
half of 1896, and the latter was £228,074 less than the in-
come for the corresponding half of 1895. And considering
that the estimates are based on the assumption that the
present financial year’s revenue will be £40,000 more than
last year’s, it follows that the first half of 1893 needs to
yield £241,000 more revenue than the first half of ’97 to
realise Treasurer Reid’s expections. Even if they are
realised the revenue will still be absurdly inadequate.
New South Wales needs to raise at least a million
more, probably a million and a-half more, revenue annu-
ally to keep honest. It gets through now on less revenue
than it did by charging nearly a million per annum of its
ordinary expenditure to loans ; the soul-blasting virtue of
economy, as practised in N.S.W., only consists in doing
with as little revenue as possible, and paying as little out
of revenue as possible, and charging the balance to loans.
Some day the boss economical Treasurer will appear in the
man who can do with £5,000,000 a year revenue, and
borrow the other £5,000,000 a year from J. Bull.
* * * *
For the half-year N.S.W.’s expenditure was
£4,843,120, and the deficit so far is £430,801. At the end
of November the shortage was only £211,343, but the ex-
penditure bolted during December and before Treasurer
Reid could pull it up some serious breakages happened.
* * * *
Victorian revenue for the half-year ended 31st
December, 1897, was £3,374,252, which‘is an increase of
£149,931 as compared with the half-year ended 31st De-
cember, 1896. A month ago the revenue had only im-
proved by £10,780, but the judicious deaths of sundry
rich residents brought in a lot of probate-duty in Decem-
ber. Treasurer Turner estimated that the revenue for
the present financial year would be £173,583 more than
last year’s cash, and, with half the year gone and nearly
£150,000 improvement already realised, his expectations
ought to come out all right, unless a severe blight sets in.
* * * -
Premier Turner, when in London, made a new
arrangement whereby the payment to the L. and W.
Bank for managing the Vic. public debt is reduced by
£5866 a year. Premier Reid does his corresponding finan-
cial stroke by seizing upon the old Church Education
Endowment Fund of £195,000 or so ; £5866 a year is just
about equal to £195,000 in a lump. The two transactions
only go to show the difference between the man of hard
economy and the man of brilliant fake.
* * * *
S. A. revenue for the quarter ended 30th Decem-
ber, 1897 (£608,329), is £3966 ahead of that for the last
quarter of 1896. Probate-duties show an increase of
£9075, so here also the judicious death of the wealthy
citizen has done a lot of good. And the decease of brewer
Simms, who left a pile of money behind him, may enable
Holden to pull through without a particularly awful
deficit.

Last year these provinces (not reckoning Maori-
land) raised £6,660,000 in loans (£5,100,000 in England and
£1,560,000 locally), and the S.M. Herald alludes to this
great roaring burst as “ borrowing on a small scale.” Wes-
tralia borrowed £2,600,000 (including half-a-million in
Treasury bills); Queensland, £1,500,000; New South
Wales, £1,440,000 ; South Australia, £1,000,000 ; and Vic-
toria (for the redemption of previous loans only), £120,000.
* * * m
The balance-sheet of the English, Scottish and
Australian Bank (casually alluded to in issue of 27th
November) is now to hand. In one respect it is the most
complete document published by any reconstructed bank,
inasmuch as it gives a full statement of the interest pay-
able on its reconstructed deposits. A comparison of the
amount paid each year with the profit or loss is in-
teresting :
Deposit Interest.
1894 .. £154,177 Profit .. £10,154
1895 .. 168,687 Loss .. 6,279
1896 .. 164,389 ~ .. 24 576
1897 .. 119,665 Profit .. 10,870
The 1896 loss was really £3373 more, but that item con-
sisted of reconstruction expenses, and was not a loss made
in the ordinary course of business. The figures show that
the ordinary banking business has got continuously
worse. In 1895 the interest outlay was £14,500 more than
in ’94, and the profits went £16,400 to the bad, which
means a net decline of £l9OO. In ’96 the interest outlay
was £4300 less than ’95, and the loss was £18,300 more—a
slump, of £22,600. In ’97 the outgoings for interest
decreased £44,700, but the profits only improved by
£35,500, a further slump of £9200. This is a pretty bad
record, for, if the E.S. and A. has another year like the
last, it will again be left, despite its two reconstructions,
with very nearly no profit at all. If things were as well
now as they were in ’94, the profit would be £44,000
instead of under £ll,OOO.
During the past year the E.S. and A. has paid
off £461,000 of its reconstructed deposits, and if it can go
on at this rate it will possibly manage to keep ahead of
the slump in business profits and come out all right in the
end. So far as can be seen it is a reasonably sound insti-
tution. But, seeing that its old deposits now cost barely
£3 11s. per cent, the profits look miserably small. The
capital is being written down to £539,437, and the profits
only amount to £2 and a few coppers per cent, per annum
on this much written-down figure. Two per cent, and a
few coppers looks a very small profit for a concern which
has written its values down to bedrock. As a small side-
issue, the accumulated loss (£34,228) was carried, in 1896,
to Contingent Fund, but neither before then or since did
any Contingent Fund appear in the balance-sheet to the
writer’s knowledge. Therefore, the place where that loss
was carried to looks somewhat akin to the unfindable
grave of Moses.
* * • *
The fearfully and wonderfully re-constructed N.
Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co. for the year ended 30th
June, 1897, shows a loss of £2216. The previous balance-
sheet showed a loss of £5670, and the one before that a
loss of £28,393, so there is over £36,000 of accumulated
shortage to make up. It is now over three years since
this concern did its firstgigantic reconstruction, and largely
reduced the outlay for debenture interest, and some
months since it did . its second big reconstruction and
made another large saving, yet it doesn’t seem even yet
to be round the corner. This year it is reckoned will
show a profit; the interest payable to debenture-holders
will then be only £120,450, against £132,992 last year, and
the saving of £12,542 should turn the present loss into a
profit of £10,300. So it undoubtedly will if the gross
income remains the same and no more losses turn up, but
the income for the year just ended was £23,000 less than
that for 1895, and there is no guarantee that it won’t keep
on declining; and though the present fall is set
down to drought and low prices, 1895 was also a year of
drought and low prices. And even if things get no worse
£10,300 annual profit isn’t much. The paid-up
capital was £1,168,000 at June, 1896, but the
awful writing-down process to provide for more losses
has since reduced it to £135,798. And the one
incurable trouble is that the N.Z. Loan and Mercantile is
now working on £135,798 of its own capital (or, rather,
about £99,000, for over a quarter of the nominal capital
is lost), and £3,311,272 borrowed on debentures, whereon
the interest has to be paid alike in good seasons and bad —
which is a fearfully top-heavy arrangement. Still, if the
institution could see its way, when things improve, to set
aside £lOOO a year or so towards wiping off this liability,
it might, at compound interest, get entirely free of it in
about 10 centuries, and then have a prosperous and plea-
sant future before it. It only requires about £6790 a year
to pay a 5 per cent, dividend on the much-diminished
capital, and, if the N.Z. L. and M. A. is ever going to get
round the corner (supposing there is any corner), it should
be able to pay that, and have something over towards a
much-needed reserve. But, on present appearances, it
needs to keep the div. hard down to the 5 per cent, limit
for the next half-century.
* * * *
Something phenomenal in the way of wretched-
ness is the report of the Victorian Farmers’ Loan and
Agency Co., a large land-owning concern of the mallee
district. This institution has been in a bad v/a,y for a long
time, but the directors seem at last to have made a clean
breast of things, or something like it. After writing-down
the value of their properties in the land of Godforgotten
to what they think is about present value, it appears that
the paid-up capital is £86,339, and the accumulated losses
are £177,174, which, on the face of things, seems to imply
that the concern is insolvent to the extent of £90,000
unless it can collect something very large in the way of
calls. But as it appears to have only got in about £llOO
during the past year, and over £51,000 of calls are
in arrear, the prospect looks very blank. It con-
fesses to a bank overdraft of £201,134 (£12,000 more
than it was two years ago) against which it puts down
its live assets at £113,000, consequently the present writer
wouldn’t like to be that bank. (The balance-sheet states,
by the way, that the co.’s bankers are the Commercial of
Australia, which isn’t a very good advt. for the Commer-
cial.) Until this year the Vic. Farmers’ and Agency
reckoned the goodwill of its awful business as an asset
worth £25,000, but the item now disappears; the co.
apparently hadn’t the gall to keep on pretending to itself
any longer that anybody could possibly give £25,000, or
even Jd. for such a business. The usual auditor certifies
that, now that goodwill and various other ruins—over
£lOO,OOO in all —have been written off, the sheet gives “ a
correct view of the company’s affairs.” And last year,
when all these losses still appeared as assets, The Bulle-
tin presumes that some auditor or other certified just as
cheerfully that that was also “ a correct view of the com-
pany’s affairs.” The Bulletin never yet met with a
balance-sheet that the average auditor wouldn’t cheer-
fully put his certificate to.

* * * *
Twelve months ago there were 5300 men and
youths employed at the Broken Hill mines. Before the
fire at Block 12, the number had risen to 6400. Imme-
diately after the fire, when it became necessary to run
down the furnaces, 4800 men were at work ; but, by the
end of the year, the number had increased again to 5600.
At the same mines, when production was highest,
there were under 4900 employes, and, a little later, only
4100. The change from carbonate and kaolin to sulphides
has demanded the outlay of more labor in production.
Seeing, however, that almost exactly half of the aggre-
gate of B.H. divs. goes direct to Europe, while wages remain
m Australia, the change is not one to be grieved over.
• « * «
It’s amusing to compare the official reports of
the tin-pot concerns of Tinned Dog Gully and those of the
big Broken Hill Proprietary, with its hundreds of differ-
ent workings. The Proprietary people don’t worry about
reports. For instance, two weekly statements were printed
together on Dec. 22—one for Dec. 8 and the other for
Dec. 15. The former ran into just four lines, and told a
breathless public that something had been driven 28ft.,
and something else 13ft.—no more. The latter was even
more exhilarating : “ Started north drive, Brodribb’s No.
3, from No. 1 east crosscut, and extended 16ft. to connec-
tion with rise from No. 4 level.” Of course, these ordinary
reports are a farce ; ditto the output returns. Only the
divs. and half-yearly accounts speak articulately.

In face of the fact that no divs., and nothing
but baby crushings, ever come out of Wattle Flat (N.S.W.)
the amount of mining news from that camp is becoming
unendurable. Everything, no matter how bad, is “satis-
factory ” or “ looking well.” One man is going to crush for
Bs. a ton, and this means a boom for the field ; another is
going to resume work in his mine after the holidays, and
the shareholders of a co. are to have a meeting soon—“ to
decide on a site for the plant.” This is mining news !
♦ * * *
From London we learn that, though zinc of the
highest quality was produced in the first four months’ run
of the Ashcroft electrolytic process at Cockle Creek,
N.S.W., the results were commercially unprofitable. Ash-
croft attributed this to defects in the plant, which was
closed down till the necessary alterations were made.
Another start has been made under Ashcroft’s direct
supervision, but it is understood that the electrolytic
works may possibly close down, and operations at Cockle
Creek be confined to smelting concentrates produced at
the Central Broken Hill mine.
» * * *
Big things were said and big results foretold by
the chairman of the Anglo-Australian Exploration in London
last month. All the representations concerning the Gibral-
tar—-the first off-shoot—had been realised; the mine was im-
proving with depth, and the developments to-day entitled
it to be considered one of the biggest things in N.S.W.
They were now fully assured of the ultimate success of
the Gallymont—the second off-shoot —the ore assaying
from 2oz. to 3oz. to the ton. Some £12,000 had been spent
in opening-up the Bimbimbi area in the southern district
of N.S.W., and their consulting-engineer (Eissler) reported
that a tunnel had proved the lode for 400 ft., and shafts,
sunk on its strike, proved its existence 1400 ft. in length.
In this development, he estimates 40,0000 z. of gold to
be in sight, with a permanent reef going down under foot.
Also mentioned that the Gibraltar would be on the div.-
list shortly.
* » » •
Mining as it is done at Trunkey—N.S.W., of
course : “ A check crushing from the Rob Roy reef proved
satisfactory. The crashing was onl,» a small one, and the
result is not known.” How very satisfactory!
'* • * •
Victory Forest Reefs, N.S.W. : “The manager
promises that the next crushing will consist of dirt from
all the faces.” Also : “Whenever time will permit, intend
putting down canvas tables to catch the fine and coated
gold which otherwise would be lost.” If there is virtue in
canvas tables, why not put them down at once ? Good as
they are in places, they- are not a universal solvent, but an
experiment might be made with a little one at the cost of
£l.
* * • •
Miners and others throughout Australasia are
reading and listening to the great lies told of Klondyke,
and the probable result will be a big rush next year. The
world has been worked up for a Klondyke boom, and
Klondyke is just the remote, unapproachable, God-for-
gotten place which people always take for granted. Hun-
dreds of thousands will start for the frozen fields, and in
all probability some thousands will go from Australia.
The sort of boom that is coming is indicated by the
“floats” already offering in America. A fair sample :
“The Jas. Ladue Gold Mining and Developing C 0.,”
capital, 5,000,000 dollars, of which the promoters, some of
the biggest non-mining men of the U.S., take 1,500,000
dollars cash, a heap of stock, and leave 1,000,000 dollars
working capital. The Engineering and Mining Journal
of New York jumps on it in deadly earnest. It wants to
know if any of the big guns acting as directors, who grab
vast sums out of the affair, yet disclaim all responsibility
on the ground that they know nothing of mining, would
put up money themselves on any scheme whose promoters
refused to be responsible for anything. It also declares
that from information supplied by reliable mining men
who have examined the field the boom is largely unwar-
ranted. But the boom will boom all the same. It is
much easier to stop a rising tide than a mining-boom
when humanity is bent on having one.
* * * *
The Rosebery co.—it ought to be King Black
Jack—West Coast, Tasmania, is trying to come to some
arrangement with the Colebrook mine re using the ore of
the latter show as a flux. It is confidently stated that the
Mount Lyell co. has offered to smelt the Colebrook ore
free of any charge if delivered at the big mine. Nothing
like making early arrangements re fluxes. They are not
over-plentiful near the West Coast mines.
* * * *
Tasmanian item : “ The Tasmania mine
crushed 2435 tons for 8501 oz. amalgam. Work has been
retarded owing to a rise of water in the mine.” That has
always been the trouble. The mine was practically shut
down for years till the water was mastered. It cost some
£40,000 to do it. The water pouring out of it would keep
all the Hannan’s mills running, and the mine would be
flooded in no time if anything serious happened to the
pumping plant.
» * » *
The London co. that offered =£250,000 for the
new Golden Gate mine, Tas., and could not get it at that
figure, has taken up the adjoining mines and provided
£25,000 to put them in shape. The statement that over
£30,000 has already been spent upon them in plant and
development work is no recommendation.

Charters Towers estimated gold-yield for ’97 :
357,0000 z. This includes all got by cyanide. About
200,000 tons crushed for 241,0000 z. Value of all gold pro-
duced, £1,039,000; divs., £340,243. “This,” says one
paper, “beats Kalgoorlie both in gold and dividends.”
The figures of each district are not sufficiently complete to
say definitely, but Charters Towers may already take it
for granted that this year Kalgoorlie will leave it hope-
lessly behind—unless the water gives out.

* * * *
The sewer catastrophe at South Yarra, where
four men perished by foul air, recalls Foul-air Point at
Taradale (Vic.,) nearly 40 years ago—probably the very
worst country for deadly gases in Australian mining his-
tory. The symptoms at Foul-air Point were exactly the
same as at South Yarra, but the gas was met with only
down to about 19ft. Many men were killed at Taradale
whilst being lowered into the shaft.

A start has been made to treat about half-a-
million tons of tailings from the old Black Horse mine,
Vic., by cyanide. Trial lots so treated returned 2dwt.’
but ljdwt. is estimated from the bulk, and this is
expected to mean a profit to the operators, while the co
will get from lOd. to Is. a ton as royalty.
* » * *
That awful mining bore, Ballard, who has
written so much about the richness of the Lake View
mine, turned up at the Associated meeting in London,
and declared that he would rather pay £4 each for the
new shares than take them at the price offered, £2 10s.
Pity the directors didn’t oblige him. His reason was that
he knew he would get 1000 per cent, on his money 111
future. He had come prepared with a big speech again B * l
the idea of selling the Iron Duke block. “It was an inr
mense mass of metal. It was freely intermixed
with gold, and the tree gold itself would pay, but
it was all a mass of metal interspersed with gold,
and it would be a great folly to part with it.”
The audience got very much intoxicated with joy
over Ballard, who, the writer believes, has never seen the
mines on which he poses as an authority. Some time
back he issued a circular, wanting to know what had
become of the vast stores of bullion lying at the Lake
View mine, and under the blissful idea that all tellurida
ore contained 33 per cent, of pure gold, went into fear
some calculations of the gold tonnage in sight in the mine.

A returned Queenslander tells how a specimen
was found in a Westralian mine, on which were plainly tc
be seen the mystic letters F.D. By a singular coincidence,
the same letters may be found on a British sovereign, and
sometimes they remain there, if the melting of the coin
isn’t done properly.

In the Black Flag Proprietary Westralia has
one low-grade mine, at any rate. Last crushing, under
two dwt. to the ton. Capital, £650,000, and some
£50,000 spent on plant alone, including a 50-head battery
erected regardless of cost. The very low-grade nature of
the mine having been established by the crushing of
thousands of tons, 70 men have been paid off, and only
sufficient kept on to hold the leases.
• * * *
Sydney people who own the Pride of the Valley
mine at Ivalgoorlie have had the straight “ griffin ” from
R. D. Thompson about the potential richness of their
show. He says : “If there ever was any 3oz. stone on
your mine there is none now. There is no stone, reef, or
formation that will average sdwt. per ton. . . . The
belt 16ft. wide that has been called a formation is not a
formation, as known here ; it is an intrusive bed of horne-
blende schist, which has never been known to carry gold.
The rock now being sunk on in the shaft is talcose schist,
the class of rock generally found in the gold-bearing belts
of this district. ... I wish it to be clearly understood
that your mine is a prospecting one only, nothing of value
having been found.” Only another recurrence of poor
Sydney’s luck in outside camps.
* * * *
Decided to prospect the Boulder Main Reef
mine at Hannan’s by a diamond drill instead of by cross-
cutting. This has been tried in many Australian mines,
but has seldom been a success. As a rule the section of
country represented by the core is too small to base
calculations upon, and when anything good-looking is
found the cross-cut has got to follow, anyhow.
* * * *
From Westralia comes another story of a miner
who showed real pluck at a critical moment. He was
winding his mate up out of the shaft, and had got him
almost to the top when his foot slipped, and, in trying to
save himself, he let go the handle and it “ bolted.” Most
men who know anything of the infernal machine would
sooner try to stop a steam tram or a mad bull or an
avalanche than a “ bolting ” windlass with a good weight
on the rope, but the aforesaid miner threw himself on it
at once, and managed to stop it just in time, with a
fractured jaw and a generally-wrecked countenance as
the results of the struggle.
* * * *
Bull seems to have a consistent producer in the
Bellevue mine, Mt. Sir Samuel, away in the north-eastern
part of the Goolgardie fields. Last crushing, 180 tons,
3350 z. For some time, a Sydney syndicate fondly imagined
it owned this mine. It certainly equipped a prospector
who had something- to do with the lease. That man is
dead, and the syndicate has to console itself with one of
the adjoining leases. The awful endings of nearly all the
W.A. prospecting syndicates are remembered with so
much bitterness that no man can propose sending a pro-
spector to Klondyke without endangering his life.
• * * *
Kalgoorlie gold output for '97 is said to be
306,0000 z., or as nearly as possible double that of the
previous year, and about ten times that of ’95. What it
will be this year is hard to indicate. With an abundance
of water it would be possible to double the ’97 production.
This is almost assured by the new producing mines
coming forward, and the arrangements being made by the
leading mines to enlarge their output. The number of
distinct lodes found in its wonderful auriferous belt, the
length to which they have been opened up, the consistent
richness of both the oxidised and sulphide zones, with the
many signs of permanency and value at greater depths,
secure for Kalgoorlie the position of the leading gold-field
of Australasia, and it is not difficult to imagine that, with
equal conditions, it would become the world’s greatest
gold-producer. It has no boom—it has had it,
and is still suffering therefrom, but had it not
to shoulder the effects of a blasted boom outside
its boundaries, Kalgoorlie would have had another
boom by this time. If it were a questioned more gold, or
another boom, the boom would win hands down. In a
mining manual, issued in London, appears a list of Wes-
tralian mines floated during the boom, with the amounts
paid in cash and shares to the vendors and promoters.
There are some hundreds of them. Many are in liquida-
tion ; fully two-thirds of them ought to be; others are
going through the throes of reconstruction ; and the div.-
payers can be counted on the fingers. Some men even
argue that the finding of Kalgoorlie pardons all this
villainy.
* * * *
Brown Hill, Hannan’s—2lo tons treated at
Dry Greek—l696oz., or an average of Boz. to the ton.
This co. will soon get to work with a second new plant,
the last, on which some £20,000 was spent, having proved
an . ignominious failure. The present plan is on the same
principle as that of the Associated Mines —drying the ore,
then dry crushing it with ball mills, cyaniding direct, and
if the refuse is found good enough, recrushing and running
the pulp over amalgamating tables. By this means the
Associated Mines are doing the best work on the camp.
The process uses little water, and it is, the writer is
informed, the only one on the camp which leaves so little
gold in the tailings that they are regarded as valueless
and are thrown away in proof of it.
* » * *
Smith and Brown were mates on the Coolgardie
goldfields, and concluded that all that was the matter
with their blocks was the want of British capital. Brown
went “’Ome,” steerage, to get it. He happened to
arrive in London when the Westralian boom was at fever-
heat. When first asked how much he wanted for a block,
he replied, “I want £lOOO, anyway”—expecting to come
down £5OO, but, almost in the twinkling of an eye, the
£lOOO was placed to his ciedit. Brown was jubilant, and
thought London was a great place for speculation.
Confidence begets confidence, and he determined to have
a shot at share-dealing himself. A few days after making
the £lOOO, he saw advertised the prospectus of the Great
Bonanza Something Mine. It fascinated and fixed him,
and, proceeding to the place where subscriptions were
invited, he put himself down for £lOOO of the stock. A
few days later, he was found tearing his hair and kicking
himself all over London. He had invested his £lOOO in
his own “cat,” and the interest he now held might be
represented by the tip of its tail. He tried to get out,
but failed, and, as the shares didn’t boom after all, the
affluence of Smith and Brown vanished on the spot.

A.J.8.” writes from Tasmania :
Some years ago I went on the tin-fields of N.E. Tas-
mania, and located near a Chinese camp. When I got
there the pig tailed multitude was in a state of great
excitement, and the camp hummed like a hive of
bees. The trouble was over the disappearance of two
white girls whom the Chinese had recently bought by
subscription for £1 a-piece. I gathered the tale in
broken English from the boss heathen, and afterwards
pieced it together. Two sailors, deserters from theii
ships, had come up to make a fortune on the tin-fields.
They brought with them a girl apiece (ages 17 and 19),
whom they had met in Hobart. Tin-mining didn’t
pay, however, and growing tired of it, and tired of the
girls, and the rum having given out, and, moreover, there
being barely enough rations for two to take the back-
track with and not nearly enough for four, they started
their retreat in the night, and left their companions
behind them. Next day, however, the girls caught
them up, and refused to be thus deserted. That night
four ill-assorted people camped close to Chinatown—
two profane sailors and two dusty, hysterical, footsore,
dishevelled girls, and the loud remnants of love’s young
dream made the Tasmanian night hideous. Naturally
the inhabitants of Chinatown took in the w'hole circum-
stances—it was impossible to avoid doing so, for the cir-
cumstances were being explained hysterically all the
night through and in the morning a Mongol deputation
offered to buy the girls for 20s. apiece. The bargain was
made, though it was only after a very severe thrashing
apiece that the goods were got into a sufficiently subdued
frame of mind to make delivery possible. There was no
township and not even a single white resident for very
many miles, and it was nearly three months before the
pair made their escape. One of them afterwards became
a barmaid, and the other, I heard, was married to a rail-
way employb. I never heard of anything happening to
the Chinamen who made this remarkable deal. Do you
reckon that £1 is cheap for a healthy white Tasmanian
girl of 17 or 19 ? What are the latest quotations in the
girl-market of Van Dobson Land ?

Viator : “ Sabbatism and holidayism are
carried to a maniacal extent in Melb. ; even
Glasgow is not in it. Now here came Christmas
Day on Saturday, Boxing Day on Sunday, and
another Boxing Day on Monday, with infernal
weather, so that, in the first place, pretty well all
the meat was spoiled on Saturday, and putrid on
Sunday, and on Monday it got up “on its own”
and went out for a walk with the other meat from
round the corner. And in this city of barbaric
snuffle fresh meat did not come till Tuesday.
Imagine an attempt to put London, New York,
or Paris on the Melb. regimen, with the white
choker round its gullet, twisted tight behind
with a stick ! What is the use of making pious
regulations which the Almighty will not conform
to ? He sends maggots just the same on Xmas
and Sunday. A fiendish regulation has just been
made in Melb. about Sunday milk to prevent poor
little children from getting this necessarily fresh
article. These pietists would even look up the
mothers’ breasts on Sunday if they could.”

THE ETERNAL TOPIC.
SCHOOLMASTER: “ What have the people most to fear in Australia?”
1ST PUPIL: “Drought, sir.”
2ND PUPIL: “No, sir; heat, sir.”
SCHOOLMASTER: “ Why heat?”
2ND PUPIL: “Because it has knocked over three of our best cricketers!”
A very well-known Victorian jury-squarer
appears to be suffering from the effects of the
depression. The pristine gorgeousness of his
waistcoat is now disfigured with beer and soup-
stains, and • the one-time shiny hat is garnished
with more than a suspicion of grease. The fall
is said to be all owing to want of confidence and
a woman. In the jury-squaring business the
operator receives the cash, and defendant has to
trust to his honor that the jury is squared. This
particular practitioner, after a long course of
honorable squaring, somehow fell from grace, and
spent on his own unholy pleasures the money
given him to procure the acquittal of a lady in
difficulties. So the lady went to gaol, and confi-
dence departed, and there is now an opening in
Victoria for an upright, God-fearing jury-squarer.

The elders of a Melb. suburban church
advertised for an organist, and received replies
from 35 candidates for the billet. Each applicant
was required to give one Sunday’s performance
gratis, as a specimen of what he could do with the
“ kist o’ whustles.” As 27 of the candidates com-
plied with this requirement, the congregation pro-
vided itself with gratis music for six months, and
has now, to the delight of the musicians, decided
to keep the billet open until the kirk becomes
“ mair feenaneial.”

System is hardly dead yet in the Tinned Dog
Land.
* * * *
A Melbourne girl, “ well-educated and re-
fined,” as the advt. required, turned up at a
Toorak house after the billet of companion. To
her the stately, spectacled dame explained that
she was replacing her* present companion, as she
objected to having her wages reduced to a reason-
able figure. The sum she had been receiving was
3s. a week, and, to put her out of the sumptuous
job, the applicant was offered 2s. ! Whereupon
she naturally fainted with joy.
* * * «
Melbourne had a comedy divorce-case on
hand recently. It began tamely enough with the
usual tale about how the respondent began to have
longabsencesfrom home and how it ultimately trans-
pired that he was away with the other woman.
But he was a mild respondent, quite unlike the
usual variety. He had attacks of violent repent-
ance during which he shed tears at his own sinful-
ness, and fell ill through grief, and asked his wife
to drag him away from his wicked existence. On
one occasion he had three weeks of loud woe and,
at the end of that time, announced his attention of
going back to the co-respondent lady on the ground
that she “ had become a Christian.” He felt that
he couldn’t leave a Christian co-respondent in the
lurch, so he took a Bible under his arm and de-
parted at 3 a.m. for his old haunts. About a fort-
night later the wife called round late at night to
see how he and the converted co-respondent
were getting on. They both came to the door,
in a state of casual undress, and they both
repented bitterly in the passage by the light
of one candle with the cold wind blowing
about their feet. The co-respondent, especi-
ally, wept something tremendous and asked
to be forgiven ; and the husband entreated his
wife to take him away on the instant and
lead him to a better frame of mind. As it
was late, and the husband was not in full walking
costume, she declined to remove him, but he ran
after her all the way back to the family residence.
After some hours of hard repentance he was for-
given and taken on again. A few months later
he fled once more to his co-respondent, and the
wife found them on board a steamer starting for
Sydney. Whereupon defendant repented on the
spot, and, abandoning his co-respondent, he in-
sisted on going home with the wife of his bosom,
and cherishing her for the rest of his life. She
flatly refused to be cherished, but he came home
and cherished her ah the same. Two days later
he disappeared for about the dozenth time in
search of his co-respondent, and this was his final
vanishment. For six months the deserted wife
lived in expectation of hearing any night a loud
snuffling at the keyhole, and finding her husband
again repenting on the doorstep, but he never
turned up again. So the usual proceedings were
resorted to, and Judge Holroyd granted a decree
nisi.

WHERE VISIONS MEET.
Here I stand on common land,
With all around me noon ;
For you afar there burns a star
And a red autumnal moon ;
Travellers we—you on the sea,
And I upon the sand.
But, friend, behold ! your Western gold
Is dawn to a land remote,
Where dews fall soft, and larks aloft
Send silvery songs afloat;
And one stands there with a purpose rare,
And watches the rose unfold.
Your gaze is cast where day dies fast,
And his on the dawning brink ;
Here at my feet two visions meet,
My noontide is their link ;
Your day is dead—his dawn is red
And my day midway past.
RODERIC QUINN.

BREAKER.
THE LAND OF NO AVAIL.
There is a land no eye hath seen
By day or night,
Unlit of moons, unloved of stars,
And mantled in twilight.
’Tis haunted by dim hooded shades
That may not smile,
With wan white lips that whisper low
But make no mirth the while.
No word is said, no song is heard,
Sick winds go by
And cry their ghosts to shrouded stars
Beyond the marbled sky.
It is the land of no avail,
Of fruitless pain,
Of poet-songs that craved a voice
But craved a voice in vain.
It is the land of broken loves,
Of sweetness missed,
Of dreams dethroned and fancies lost
And kisses never kissed.
RODERIC QUINN.

The Norton-Carruthers case provided at
least one disappointment for the public last week.
When an order was issued that Susan Blackburn’s
evidence should be kept strictly dark, and sat
upon, and buried until further notice, the general
assumption was that the lady’s revelations were
something quite unspeakable. This opinion
was strengthened by the appearance of her photo.
—a very stout and aggressive photo., with a
squashy hat—in the Daily Telegraph. Yet,
when the evidence did come out, it wasn’t nearly
so picturesque as most people anticipated. It was-
mostly a rather ordinary story about a woman
who used to clean out an office 12 years ago, and
the tale was so like any number of other tales,
that the public didn’t get the beautiful shock it
expected.

This is the time of year when the dailies
are filled with wearisome reports of school speeches
and notices of songs and recitations given by un-
fortunate infants who would rather be playing
cricket or fishing. The only gleam of humor in the
whole arrangement is the awful names under
which most of the youngsters stagger to “dux”
and glory. In a Melbourne suburb one small
Kindergarten with 30 pupils gives away no less
than 12 prizes, to Eileen arid Popsy and Dulcie
and Trixie and Yera and Reva and Rita and
Reggie and Finley and Norman. Plain Jack
Smith and Tom Jones are getting to be as un-
common as a fat woman on a bike.
* * * V
Four or five visitors, one of them a Minis-
ter of the Crown, dropped into a N.S.W. charity
institution lately, and, after a look around, made
modest donations. A reporter called in, a little
later, on the hunt for news. “ Oh,” exclaimed the
manager, “ the Minister and party were here.
The Minister subscribed a guinea. Don’t forget
to • say the Minister subscribed a guinea. Oh,
everything was right; they liked all they saw.
But don’t forget to mention in your report that
the Minister subscribed a guinea.” “Did the
others give anything?” “Yes; Captain Dash
gave two guineas, and Mr. Blank J>l. Oh, yes,
they all subscribed liberally, and will appear in
our own report. But put the Minister in the
paper. Don’t forget to mention that the Minister
gave a guinea.” As the reporter got through the
gate, the manager again yelled, “ Don’t forget the
Minister’s subscription ” ; and that evening a note
reached the newspaper office, the manager once
more informing the editor that “ the Minister
subscribed a guinea.”

chemists from other parts of the world from being
registered here? In my own case, I served four
years’ apprenticeship to a qualified chemist in
England, was ten years assistant to leading Lon-

“WATCH - NIGHT SERVICE. ”
A “lydie once ” sees the old year out, etc. Sketch in Sydney Domain , ’97-98.
Victoria is evidently going the way of
America as regards education of the sexes. Xmas
exam, returns from Melb. University give the
girls an overwhelming victory all along the lines
in algebra, geometry, trigonometry, English and
history ; French, German, elementary apatomy,
physiology and botany; whilst the boys have
taken exhibitions in only chemistry and physics,
and the dead and decomposed languages. All
sport and no study is the matter with town-bred
Jim now-a-days. His intellectual pursuits are
confined to the evening newspaper, and, when he
has learned how to smoke, he seems quite con-
tent, as a rule, to let Sissie represent the mind-
culture of the family. Perhaps the first result of
Woman Franchise, when it comes, will be a bill
for the compulsory higher education of Jim.
* * * *
Some time ago The Bulletin commented
on the miserable failure of the average suicide.
Particularly it commented on the inefficiency of
the double-barrelled suicide; when a man and
woman agree to die together, the almost in-
variable result is that the man kills the woman,
and then, after feebly carving a bit of meat off
himself, is arrested and gets five years. Apropos
of which painful subject this paper pointed out
that a reasonably certain way of death for
two is to go out in a boat to some place where
there are 10 fathoms of green ocean under-
neath, and tie the anchor tightly to both parties
by a thick rope and eight hard knots, and then go
overboard. Apparently that idea has not been
wholly wasted. Within the last few months a
Maoriland suicide with a grindstone lashed to him
has been fished out of Auckland harbor. A de-
funct has been found in a western river with' a
bag of stones and a bit of iron rail attached as
sinkers in order to prevent him changing his
mind, and as a guarantee against unauthorised
interference. Also an old gentleman at Banks-
town, near Sydney, was found last week in his
own well with two large flat-irons suspended to
his neck.
* * * *
In connection with the proposed N.S.W.
legislation for the control of inebriates, Dr. Creed,
M.L.C., relates some curious experiences. One of
them concerns two Maoriland country doctors.
They were both under legal alcohol-pro hi bit ion, so
they used to fall sick by turns and prescribe for
each other the otherwise unprocurable whisky.
And in due time they both drank themselves into
the grave.

T.D. : “Thanks to for his remarks
anent the wizened progeny of the dancing-girl.
At a recent public picnic I met a young married
woman, just recovered from serious illness, flushed
and almost fainting as the result of half-a-day’s
dancing, with the thermometer close up to 90 in
the shade. It is no uncommon thing, either, to see
fair trippers on the light untimely toe who would
obviously be more wisely employed in the pre-
paration of baby-linen.”
* * * *
A Westralian mine-manager lately had a
trip to London with specimens. “The night
before the meeting of directors,” he says, “I got
mixed up in a row which left me with a black eye
and a cut nose. Next day, when I came before
the directors, they hustled me out, and were going
to give me in charge; so I put my hand in my
pocket and produced a specimen, and was imme-
diately offered a chair. Then they whisperingly
suggested some dirty work, but I wasn't on—sim-
ply having a black eye doesn’t necessitate' doing
dirty work. Yet I know they would never have
dared to suggest it to me if I hadn’t had that
black eye. In the opinion of the world, a man
with a black eye is capable of anything.”
* * • *
A Melbourne brewer estimated that the
city’s consumption of beer on Boxing Day was
about 40,000 gallons. “ The figures don’t look
very big,” says the tanglefoot authority, “but,
after all, there’s a good deal of steady drinking to
be had in 40,000 gallons of beer —about a pint for
every man, woman, and child, not counting the
babies.”

Dear Bulletin, — You may remember that
some time back a bad young man was discovered
with his eye at a knot-hole at a Melbourne
ladies’ baths, and was removed to gaol for six
months. That is all right, but I have just dis-
covered that there is a man constantly on duty at
the ladies’baths, and it is his business to parade
around, keeping his masculine eye on the fat
females, and the lean females, and those who are
just middling, as they splash about in the brine,
and to see that no accident happens to them. The
law never feels called upon to remove him with
violence, somehow.—S.S.
« * « *
An average Boulder (Wesfcralia) washing-
bill for a fortnight runs to about 10s. But that isn’t
a circumstance to the washing-bills that used to
happen in the early days of Broken Hill. A lonely
young silver-king once went south and brought
back his bride. When they landed there was an
accumulated “ wash ” of two weeks and three
days, which the unsophisticated darling “put
out.” It came back the next Saturday evening.
“How much?” “<£3 10s., please.” Water was
then 255. per 100 gallons—present price also in
Boulder.
• * * *
Cairns (Q.) has got its regular Kanaka
leper on hand. At least, they have a very strong
suspicion that he is a leper, but, until absolute
certainty is arrived at, the suspect is confined in
the yard of the police-station. This opens up a
lot of possibilities for the local police. Said yard
is divided from the street by a picket fence only,
and, when the wind rises, the germs blow into
Cairns. Also, the local small boy stops to poke
borak at the leper through the fence —which im-
plies more possibilities.
* * • *
On Ist January the Federal Coffee Palace,
Melbourne, formally recreated itself as the Federal
Palace Hotel, with a colonial wine license to work
upon at present, and the expectation of being able
in the near future to meet neighboring grog-shops
on level terms. There is no vestige of teetotal
cant attaching to the Federal Hotel management
nowadays. Shareholders in the business ask for
a dividend, and temperance principles having
been tried and found wanting in annual profits,
the Rechabite conscience has become educated up
to the maxim that half a pub. is better than no
bread. For outsiders who don’t care a button
whether the business eventually gets a spirit
license or otherwise, the Federal’s chrysalis state
is interesting. Thusfar there has never been a
palatial boarding-house where the sale of Aus-
tralian wines was pushed in a way likely to benefit
the national industry. The Federal Hotel will do
more to encourage a taste for light versus heavy
alcoholic liquor than all the other Melbourne pubs,
put together.
From the cross-examination of Mrs. Susan
Blackburn, in the Carruthers-Norton libel case:
“ Can you produce any letters of Mr. Carruthers’
beginning anyway except ‘ Dear madam ’? ” Wit-
ness : “ Those are the only letters I haVe kept. I
did not know I had them until a few weeks ago.
He has written to me, ‘ Dear Miss Thompson,
yours, in love, till we meet again.” It is a truly
painful thing when the letters which breathe an
atmosphere of red-hot love can’t be found, and the
only surviving correspondence starts “Dear
madam.” The public has a genuine grievance just
here.
If for nothing else than that the Crown
were unable to produce any earth or blood-stained
clothes worn by the unfortunate Suffield on the
night of the alleged assault, the case should have
fallen through. Almost everyone who knew
Suffield knew also that he had only two suits —
a working and an out-of-doors one —yet there
could be found no marks on either of these ! Pos-
sibly the Crown theory is that Suffield wore the
old fig-leaf costume of Genesis on that cold and
drizzly night.

Dear Bulletin, —How the Narrabri (N.S.
Wales) sleeper-cutters get jolly and fat. The
men get 2s. 3d. a sleeper delivered in the yard.
They have to pay Is. a sleeper to get them carted
from the bush, a distance of about 20 miles. The
Govt. exacts 7d. per sleeper for the revenue ; then
the railway department confiscates over 10 per
cent.—Bd. per sleeper—for what are called “con-
demns.” These are re-purchased at a nominal
rate and used on the line. This leaves sd. a
sleeper for the cutter to get jolly and fat on. A
smart axemen by working hard can a erage 30
sleepers weekly, so his earnings amount to, say,
2s. Id. a day.— Sleeper-Chopper.

Messrs. Fletcher, Fletcher and Co., of
London, deserve the deepest thanks of the Aus-
tralian people for putting Vibronaon this market.
It has been recommended over and over again in
the most convincing language by the British
medical profession, and in this age of quackery
and false pretences it is most satisfactory to ob-
serve that Messrs. Fletcher and Fletcher take the
highly commendable course of offering any medical
man the right to inspect the many original
medical testimonies certifying to Vibrona’s effi-
cacy. Among others, Dr. B. H. Paul, the eminent
qumtologist, editor of the Pharmaceutical
Journal, has undertaken to periodically examine
add report on Vibrona. He says : “ The claims
of Vibrona are fully substantiated.” And these
claims are that in anaemia, neurasthenia, in coin
valescence from influenza, diphtheria, pneumonia,
or typhoid, &c., &e., the restorative power of
Vibrona is remarkable, while in neuralgia and in-
somnia it acts like a charm. One praiseworthy
feature is that it is never likely to produce any
craving for alcohol, and also that after a course of
Vibrona the system so recovers its natural tone
that stimulants are neither required nor desired.
The Sydney agent is Mr. G. F. Birks, 18 O’Con-
nelbstreet. *
SIEGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her eyes make valorous advance,
And so besiege my heart
With mocking archery of glanee,
Surrender seems the wiser part.
Yet, should I dare return the fire,
At the first flash her eyes
Desist from battle, and retire
In shy confusion and surprise.
THOROLD DALRYMFLP.
