November 12th, 1897.
To the Bile Bean Manufacturing Company.
Dear Sirs, —I have been for some years subject to
biliousness and headache, and, seeing Bile Beans were so
extensively advertised, I decided to give them a trial, and
accordingly put myself under a short course. I can
truthfully state that I experienced relief from the begin-
ning, and to-day I feel in perfect health. I can conscien-
tiously recommend Bile Beans to anyone suffering from
Biliousness and Constipation. You are at perfect liberty
to publish this statement when and where you please.—
Yours truly, W. HESTLEHURST.
The above letter is valuable not from its lepgih
and fulness of detail, but because Mr. Hestlehurst
is a well-known citizen and one of Sydney’s most
prominent business men.
BILE BEANS for Biliousness are a well-estab-
lished remedy, and not a quack preparation of
mushroom growth. They are made in one of the
largest and best-equipped laboratories of the world
in the city of Detroit, Michigan, U.S.A., and have
been used in that country for years by the best
physicians. BILE BEANS are an undoubted
specific for the ailments mentioned above. The
low price is due alone to the enormous sales every
year, amounting in the millions. To be obtained
at all Chemists. Price, 9£d. per box, small size,
or Is. lod. regular size, or send direct to the Aus-
Depot, the Bile Bean Manufacturing Co.,
19 Macquarie Place, Sydney.*
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
_ Sydney, January 4, 1898.
Mr Dear Mtee—
At Randwick races on New Year’s Day the
most gorgeously-apparelled persons were the book-
makers’ wives, sisters, and cousins. The gentle
Gentile public unostentatiously trotted its woman-
kind around, mostly in brown holland and washing
drill. Such a practical object-lesson of the com-
parative profits accruing from laying and backing
the gee-gee a makes you hesitate before placing
your modest half-sovereign on your fancy. The
wife of one well-known ringman surrounded her
large person with brocade and grey chiffon,
another with an hour-glass waist floated in black-
barred pink and a tea-tray hat. All had that
hall-mark of the puffeck lydy, a silk foundation.
.By the way, rustling isn’t going to be de riyueur in
the near future. A cheap imitation frou-frou hav-
ing been placed on the market, the lower ranks
no.w rustle at a nominal price, and next season’s
skirts will be absolutely as silent as a nurse’s gown
should be. Every second woman had a few
fathoms of gold cable hanging round her neck,
with a bright gilt heart like Mother Eve's fig-leaf
dangling therefrom. These chains and pendants
are the latest trinket-epidemic, along with the
bunch of keys which every smart woman now
carries.
The stalls at the Lyceum are just now exten-
sively patronised by the gilded (and otherwise)
youth. ‘ Dick ” appears to be a young man’s
panto., judging by the highly-interested male con-
tingent in the audience. One attractive stage-boy
had a whole row of admirers one evening last
week ; the following was lavish with clapping, and
its little button-hole sprays thudded on the boards
to emphasise every point. Daisy Holly, the grace-
ful back-bender—she will soon be able to rap the
floor with th(*back of her head, if her spine doesn’t
crack in the process—will shortly depart for the
Jr* , A;? ? * r 'P> as Bessie Clayton’s successor, in
the Chinatown ” co.
Pantomime rehearsal is no joke. Twenty-one
hours was a working day for one co. now performing
m Sydney ; the mummers went at it from 10 a.m.
one day till 7 a.m. the next. All-night sittings were
frequent during the preparation of all the current
shows. J. C. Williamson is an inexorable re-
hearser. Sitting in front, he directs the scenes,
which must be gone through with business-like
promptitude, and his commands are sharp and to
the point. Discipline is his watchword. “It’s
just like school,” murmured one astonished novice,
who had thought stage-life was all beer and
skittles.
The stage-boy’s idea of humorous biz is to slap
her tights briskly and give a little kick on the off-
side when she puts her arm round the stage-girl’s
waist. The book seldom allots an alleged joke to
the feminine hero, and she never thinks up any
funniments of her own. Nellie Farren still
remains as the one and only example of a humor-
ous stage-boy. She had no appealing “ pretty ”
ways, and never threw herself on the mercy of the
Court, with an air of “ Don’t you think I’m very
clever— for a woman ? ” She wasn’t self-conscious
and she had a sense of humor. Nobody says now
that women have no sense of humor, but critics
allege that women take for wit that element which
is unfit for publication. Because some “ smoking-
room stories” have humor, they reason that all
humor consists in smoking-room stories. There is
a type of face supposed to belong to the woman-
with-a-sense-of-humor; possessing a broad cheek,
flat nose, and large mouth, it suggests a cross
between Socrates and a South Sea Islander.
The New Year’s Eve noise-distributors last
Friday were helped along by their lady friends.
Some of the most loudly-yelling “squeakers”
were performed on by women. One young lady
in King-street towards midnight was using a
mouth-organ of such exceeding shrillness that
competitors near her gave up in despair. She and
her escort of “boys” were left in possession of
the street. Sand has nearly superseded flour as
the up-to-date bag-filler. It’s cleaner on the coat,
but much more blinding when the bag bursts full
in the eye. One man had a shower-bath of the
tiny colored-paper confetti which are scattered
around so freely in gay Paree in Carnival time.
It might pay an enterprising importer to intro-
duce these and the long paper-serpents and card-
board trumpets in time for next New Year’s hoot,
and thereby impart a touch of boulevardian ele-
gance to the yellings of the push. Disgusted
globe-trotters, surveying the midnight revels from
balconies of swagger hotels, professed to be flab-
bergasted at the scarcity of bobbies in the throng
as compared with the übiquitousness of the men
in blue in England.
Some anonymous person is attempting to work
up a Stoddart matrimonial boom by firing into
various society organs an abrupt bullet about the
English cricketing captain. “ Stoddart is looking
out for a wife ” —only that and nothing more. If
the correspondent would only obligingly state
whose wife the cricketer is looking out for, the
missive would take on a deep and libellous in-
terest.
All the Gregory family are great cricketers.
Mrs. H. Donnan, Mrs: Varley, and two Misses
Gregory, all sisters of “Syd.,” took part in an
exciting ladies’cricket-match at Lady Robinson’s
Beach, lately, between Kogarah and Rockdale.
The teams were clad in blouses and short skirts
and business-like playing-accessories. Miss G.
secured top score with 45 runs, and her younger
sister proved the best bowler of her team.
In one of the theatres, the other night, a be-
reaved widow sat in full panoply of woe fenced off
from the rest of the d. c. by the correct amount of
white cap, cuffs and collar on stiff and voluminous
crape. The weeds of this sad weeper after the
departed were agitated by the giggles of the
wearer at all the stage funny business; as an
ostentatious display of mourning they failed dis-
mally. Another queer example, of taste was
seen in a high-toned tea-room, last week, when
into the midst of the tea-imbibing throng there
sailed the respondent in a recent divorce-case,
accompanied by several of the lady-witnesses.
Everybody present dropped his or her bath bun in
amazement, but the party, apparently, rather
enjoyed the sensation it created than otherwise,
and chatted loudly and happily.
Miss Rose Scott and some of her friends are
agitating on the subject of having cooking made a
oompulsory item for girls in public schools. The
opinion of these forethinkers for the digestions of
posterity is that the vast subject of boiled pro-
vender needs to be systematised. The drawing-
room meeting stage of the campaign has been
passed, and heavy practical leaders on the subject
now begin to appear in the dailies. Domestic
economy as a newspaper topic can’t make any-
thing but dry, uninteresting reading, which the
public shy at.
Women are exasperating shoppers. The other
day a girl went into an Arcade toy-shop in search
j toy. She was hard to please,
and in a short time the obliging proprietor had a
whole menagerie in clockwork jerking itself spas-
modically about the floor, and a Lilliput Redfern
in full working order under a chair. But the
alleged customer arose without purchasing, and
the enraged toy-proprietor followed her to the
door with some withering home-truths. “You
come in here for a rest, get me to waste my time
and amuse you by turning over all my stock, and
don t buy a penn’orth. There’d be a lot in shop-
keeping if all customers were like you. Yah ! ”
Another, case in point: A breathless matron
walked into an oyster shop and said abruptly,
without any punctuation whatever: “I want a
oysters they must be fresh there were three
tt&d in the last bag and five in the bag before I
think you ought to make up the eight I’ll
take the eight with me.” Her husband, who had
followed her in, said meekly, “ Nonsense, my
dear,” blushed apologetically at the oyster-man,
and walked her off. It was only a dark and deep-
laid scheme to get at the hot and harried fish-man
for eight gratis oysters. I beg to enter these two
as earnest triers in the Mean Woman’s Cup.
Boiled down, the substance of all factory legis-
lation js the cheap blouse. Incidentally, trousers
come into reformers’ plans, and skirts also, but
the too-familiar slop-made cotton misfit, with
badly - laundried cuffs and collars, and a brass
safety-pin at the back failing to hold up the
sagging skirt, is the crux that worries legislators,
rouses philanthropists into organising factory-girls’
clubs, and leads pessimistic speakers to allude
despairingly to woman’s ineradicable and fatal
passion for cheap-and-nasty shopping. At the
annual meeting of the Working and Factory
Girls’ Club, the other night, the general tone
of all the speakers was one of regret that
such an institution was needed. Women,
working or playing, won’t combine even to
better themselves—they hold their own abilities
in such contempt that they snap at any work at
any wages, and they force sweating-rates on em-
ployers by their own craving for bargains. Alto-
gether, the sex politico-economically is a dismal
failure. The emblem of all its industrial work is
the shabby, sloppy two-bob-a-dozen blouse.
A shriek against modern dressing in one of
the recent magazines, in which Ouida has an
an g r y say about the unspeakable awfulness of
everything cut to present-day patterns except
the free and flowing tea gown, is merely an in-
stance of how wildly astray are those soulful folk
who put art before common, everyday sense. The
great clothes-idea consists in being unobtrusively
useful. Men’s garments, especially, should be
business-like and not get in his way. A man
hasn’t time to lean classically about in flowing
draperies, as they used to do before the world was
tired. How would a man in a toga or doublet and
hose look driving a train, or doing pick-and-shovel
work, or laying a telegraph-wire, or editing a paper,
or leaning up against a bar, or playing golf, or
backing a winner? A man can wear what he
likes now within certain limits, and he never had
any more liberty than that even in the toga days.
Also, in present-day fashions, he can keep himself
perfectly clean at small cost, but, in the good old
days, immaculate spotlessness was the privilege of
the high and lofty, and they don’t seem to have
attained it altogether. In women’s attire the
same sartorial virtue is attained at small expense by
the neat-and-natty, though commonplace, belted-
in girl, whereas the tea-gown woman is a floppy-
sloppy horror in the morning, washing up the
breakfast cups and cleaning up house perfunctorily
in a draggled, shapeless wrapper, that whisks up
all the floor-dust, and gets the cat tangled up in its
untidy tail, and causes her husband to muse re-
gretfully over her vanished waist. The tea-gown
is too luxurious a garb in a world where nine out
of ten women have to put in a day’s hard work
between morning and night.
An inquiring person has traced the royal and
aristocratic game of golf to a humble origin. A
lonely shepherd, to kill time, used to knock round
stones into a rabbit-hole with his crook. In itself
golf is a long-drawn-out pastime just fit for shep-
herds and other cranks. As a game for girhath-
letes, however, it has caught on, because on such
a large playground a man and a girl can get a
considerable distance from other people and take
a long time getting back to them. In this it
ranks only second to the bicycle. Golf and the
bike together are killing tennis. You are too
near your neighbors all the time in tennis.
The two De Moleyns, brothers of Lady Hope-
toun, who have a sugar-plantation near Cairns
(Q.), have made their cane-growing pay. They
started in these alligator-haunted wilds with a
modest four-roomed humpy, but in place of this
primitive tenement there now stands a swagger
verandah-all-round mansion, and the industrious
brown islanders have surrounded it by a tidy
flourishing garden. To be asked to dine at the
De Moleyns mansion is to be top-notched for sas-
siety among the Cairnsites and surrounding
planters. ISlobody ever mentions now the sinful
libel that the name used to be Mullins.
Station - owners Out - back don’t appreciate
“johnny” wool-classers, but prefer a rough-and-
ready person who gets on well with the shed men
and has no irritatingly superior airs. A well-
known Western squatter was disappointed in
securing his own tried and trusted “ grader ” this
season, and wired his Sydney agents to send him
a classer. In response there arrived at the station
an innocent-looking, blue-eyed babelet elegantly
tailored, who announced himself as “ the-ali-
new wool - classah.” The staggered employer
didn’t care to try the experiment of inti’oducing
that to the shed and to the ribald mirth of Bill
and Jim. But he wired indignantly to the
agents: “Wool-classer duly arrived, but you’ve
omitted to send along the child’s mother.”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent: —
“ During the Sydney test-match, people win
assembled outside Melb. newspaper-offices to
watch the progress of the game were harangued by
a crack-brained enthusiast, bubbling over with a
March-hare system of his own for ‘ cooking ’ the
scores. ‘ See here,’ he hissed, oornering a startled
spinster who was hurrying past to keep an ap-
pointment with her dentist at the top of Collins-
street, ‘you say the English have made 510 ! Well,
you’re wrong. Put nought before, a number,
number remains the same—oslo equals 510. Put
nought after it, number remains same—-510 equals
5100. So the blessed Britishers have made 5100.’
On the same mathematical principle he fixed Ire-
dale’s second score as ‘ 180 ’ instead of 18.
‘‘Last Thursday afternoon, when Melbourne
thermometers registered 109 degs. in the shade,
the ardent admirer, who looks at his Maudie as
though he would like to eat her, might easily have
secured some of her sweetness in a liquid form by
wringing the sleeves of her white linen blouse.
Several Maudies on the Block were observed to
drip from the elbows as they bore their sunshades
aloft, and visions of pink skin glowing through
the dampness added to the warmth of the effect.
“ Somehow, the frocks on the first day of the test-
match fell short of the high standard set up by
cricket costumes earlier in the season. Perhaps
women felt too limp, after the heat, to get into
full war-paint, or, maybe, the first tender bloom
has worn off most people’s summer things. Any-
way, this time, wearers of blouses and sailor hats,
instead of skulking in their seats all day, held up
their heads with the best. Lady Brassey clung to
her black brocade, but cooled it down with a cream
vest and summery hat.
“ On Boxing Night seamen ad. lib. were asked
to tea at Tour Mont, the residence of Premier
Reid’s brother-in-law. Every Jack brought his
Jill by special invitation, and thanks to musio,
games and girls, the signal for the plaintive home-
ward - bound chanty came all too soon. Mrs.
Mason and her pretty daughters helped with the
programme, and the Speaker himself cut up the
Christmas cake, rich in hidden treasures such as
rings, sixpences and thimbles. Tom Bowling, of
course, is superstitious and moreover a double-
dyed man, so his mates did not scoff when
he nearly piped his eye at unearthing the thimble,
ominous of old-bachelorhood.
“ The marriage of Miss Hetty Morrison, young-
est daughter of Dr. Morrison, principal of the
Scotch College, sets very old Scotch collegians
dreaming of that long-past day when their proud
pedagogue treated them to a spread in honor of
the birth of his first girl. The infant of those
times, now the wife of a prominent Melb. bar-
rister, goes back to the college, hubby and all, to
kfpr, house for her father, vice the bride of last
month.
" m Dr. Morrison’s case, the scholastic goose
has been an untiring layer of golden eggs. As
many of his numerous ‘bairns’ as could be in-
duced to mind their books were exported Europe-
wards, regardless of expense, to have their minds
and manners revised and improved by the best
Continental authorities. All the Morrison girls
have married well, and, like their brothers, have
been fairly prominent figures in Melb. gaieties,
thanks to their money-making dad, who, not con-
tent with educational grist, dabbles profitably in
butter, eggs, and the care of bees, supplying the
college direct with dairy produce from his own
farm.

competitions girls seem determined to make hacks

Queensland Govt. Exhibitions (value £3OO each)
went to the so-called ‘ignorant sex.’ This year
Miss Theodora Copas, of Maryborough (Q.),
winner of fiye medals and the Fairfax prize at the
Sydney Senior, has also gained an exhibition at
Trinity College (Melb.), while a whole swag of
Ormond College (Melb.) scholarships have been
jumped by girls. And at the recent Melb. matri-
culation, four out of six exhibitions and 17 out of

“A touring widow, returning to Australia and
her grown-up family by a recently-arrived mail-
boat, plighted her .troth en route to the sweetest
young thing on board, a boy apparently younger
than her own elder children. She taught her cap-
tive to call her ‘Tiny’ in the prettiest way all
over the ship, and, in return, she coined for his
pet-name ‘ Pie-jo ’! At one of the first Australian
ports a detachment of male sons and daughters
met in a and pa that is to be,’ as Belos Hodges,
of the ‘ Claudian ’ company, calls his father-in-
law elect. Strange to say, peace and harmony re-
mained unbroken, and the bride-elect, watching
her girls promenade with ‘Pie-jo,’ told onlookers
smilingly that ‘ Mr. X. was learning to know his
daughters ’!
“In the good old boom days a Melb. family
built a handsome vault in a suburban cemetery.
Another branch of the family acquired the adjoin-
ing section, and with the ruling passion for allot-
ments strong even in the bone-yard, it cast
covetous eyes on the symmetrical little tomb
alongside. Then hard times came ; the elder
branch of the family moved to Sydney in very
reduced circumstances, and the one which re-
mained in Melbourne offered to buy the burial-
place at a reduced price. But the refugees clung
to their little necropolis. One day, after years of
fruitless negotiation, the Sydney people received a
wire to the effect that mother was dying, and had
expressed a last wish to be buried in uncle’s vault,
and would they kindly sell out? But even then
the fallen boomers refused to part, and, as mother
is in perfect health to this day, and the price of
fashionable tombs is going up again in Melbourne,
they are glad they held on.
“A Melbourne doctor, giving journalistic ad-
vice re the best food for hot weather, casually
describes his own repast of the previous day.
Beginning with ‘ a delicate preparation of soles in
aspic, served on ice,’ he next dallied with a roast
pigeon, and finished up with baked custard and
ripe fruit. En passant, he admits that this menu
might not suit poor people, and I don’t suppose it
would. Ice alone was a ruinously expensive com-
modity during the hot spell.
“A Melbourne Judge, by the way, was sud-
denly bereaved of his cook on that awful Friday.
Poor wretch ! I wonder if she had been roasting
birds and trying to make aspic ‘ jelly ’ throughout
that torrid week.
“ What a fortune awaits the author of a cheap,
durable, wooden olothes-peg, warranted to hold
“ heavy washing ” on the line, in defiance of all
the strong winds that blow! Whenever, under
the present old-fashioned system of pegging-out,
the voioe of the tempest roars in the back yard, a
wet sheet is bound to break away from its moor-
ings, and Bridget’s valuable time is devoted to
rescuing fallen linen from the dust. As for
handkerchiefs and other small goods, they are apt
to get blown no girl knoweth whither, owing
to the absurd lack of grip in the primitive two-
pronged delusions which profess to keep even
blankets in captivity. True, there is a more re-
cent wooden peg, with a spring fastening, but its
spring goes wrong on slight provocation, and of
the two evils the old original forked-radish
arrangement seems to be generally preferred.
These remarks are inspired by a clatter of falling
pegs, followed by bitter cries of woe from the
family wash-house. Certain “ things ” are no
doubt doomed to another soaking, and all for the
want of some simple patent contrivance not yet
invented. How long, how long ?

pedagogues, has taken to doing the ‘city ’ in a
brown velvet coat and waistcoat, and a brilliant
necktie. Tucker and Harrison Moore are the
only Melbourne professors who are not mere
slouchers in their attire, but this brown-velvet
rig is an aesthetic departure.

Over 40 more Japs—including nine women
—reached Queensland by the Tsinan last week.
This in spite of Nelson’s and Tozer’s reiterated
declarations that a diplomatic arrangement had
been made to prevent further arrivals. There are
now upwards of two thou sand Japs in Queensland;
and a special line of steamers —felicitated by
Tomjoe Byrnes and the Govt.—is bringing a fresh
consignment every month. Soon Japan will have
such a hold on Queensland that it will be im-
possible to legislate effectively against her little
brown men. For Japan is at this moment
the greatest naval power in the Pacific. When
her ships now building are completed she
will have (says an expert in the North American
Magazine) the most modern and thoroughly-
equipped fleet in the world—not excepting Britain’s
—and she is the only nation to whom recent war
has taught the potentialities of a modern fleet.
And this is the power which aspires to the hege-
mony of the Pacific; and which Australia, through
Queensland, is permitting to establish a solid
settlement on her territory (Thursday Island at
this day is nothing but a Jap. settlement)!!
The push had a lively time at Brighton
(Victoria) on Boxing Day. The push spent) all
the morning filling itself with beer, and then it
sailed out into the blazing 154 deg. sunlight, and
began to fight it out. At one time fights, more’or
less free, were going on in 17 different directions,
and five or six weary, kicked, sunstruck, foundered
policemen were struggling heroically to suppress
the tumult. Just when they were in the thick of
their troubles, an 18th insurrection broke out, and
a hairy man started to strip himself naked on a
cliff, and hurl his garments into the sea. Then the
force abandoned all hopeof makingheadway against
the battle, and contented itself with snatching
rioters from the outskirts of each scrum, and limp-
ing with them to the lock-up, where presently all
the cells, and the henhouse, and the woodshed
were full of prisoners, and there was only room
for one more in the dog-kennel. Next to stoking
the middle furnace in Gehenna, there is probably
no hotter shop open for a policeman than Brighton
on Boxing Day.
N.S.W. Marine Board will doubtless en-
quire into the circumstances attending the
foundering of the Sydney-owned brig Minora,
carrying coal from Newcastle to Port Jackson.
Seven lives were lost. The vessel was of 249 tons
register, and was carrying either 450 or tons
when she foundered. Did the bottom fall right out
of her ? Capt. Gallant—good name !—was the
only one of all hands saved ; after being 23 hours
in the water he was picked up by a passing vessel.
Soon after the brig went down, and while several
of the crew were still afloat, the captain says :
Before the mocn went down a schooner-rigged steamer
came in sight. We called out, and she heard us, and
turned her head round and came within speaking distance.
Someone on the forecastle head sang out to me, “Have
you got a boat?” I answered, “ No, we are on a piece of
plank. Send a boat for us.” The steamer then went
astern, turned her head round, circled round us once, and
finally lay with her starboard and masthead-lights visible
to us all night. At daylight she stood out to the east-
ward, and then went south, as if going to Sydney. If the
steamer had laid as she w r as when I spoke to her, instead
of going astern, we could all have swam to her. At that
tune I could hear the crew singing out two and three men
at a time.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they wiM
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Personal Items.
Francis Lord, an Australian by birth and
N.S.W. ex-politician, who has just died in his 86th
year (he was bom three years ahead of Waterloo)
was the last survivor of the Mixed Parliament of
1844.
Baronet Saul Samuel’s heir is an ex-hussar
officer, who had to retire from the army through
an accident in Ireland, whereby he lost a leg.
It is an unlucky title that has a wooden leg in the
second generation.
Out of four Melbourne University Chancel-
lors two were Roman Catholics. Also, the first
and last (Barry and Madden) were Irishmen and
nati' es of Cork. The other two (Brownless and
Moorhouse) were English.
Andrew Lyell, accountant, who lately de-
ceased at Melb., was, very long ago, a Foreign-
trade M.L.A. for Emerald Hill. He went down
when M'Culloch introduced his Protectionist
tariff, and vanished out of politics.
“Neild, Major J.C., M.P., Commanding
7th Regiment, ‘Greycairn,’ Edgecliff.” This is
not a Chinese puzzle, nor a character in the panto-
mime.. It is only the way Jawbone Neild ap-
pears in the N.S.W. telephone-list.
Chief-Justice and Waxellency Madden
must have about the thickest skin in Victoria.
He delights in submitting his shiny bald pate to
the friction of a whalebone machine-brush, which
is more than the average hairy-headed customer
can bear with equanimity.
James Russel, mining pioneer, who died a
few months back in Victoria, was a survivor of
the wreck of the Royal Charter (1859). Having
made a “ pile ” in the early gold days he, with his
wife and two daughters, went home in the Charter,
and the three ladies went down with her.
The other day the convict Butler, of the
notorious Sydney divorce case, made a confession
of bigamy and attempted to commit suicide in
prison. The reason assigned is that the widow
whose chemist’s business the convict managed and
coveted, has re-marrisd. A case of blighted affec-
tion !
Captain Russell, of Maoriland, interviewed
the other day in Sydney, seems to be the only
sanguine man among the seven Opposition leaders
of Australasia, unless Clark of Tasmania be the
other. Lyne, MTntyre, Downer, and the man,
whoever he be, who runs the show in Queensland,
all drag a dejected tail in the dust and have no
hope worth mentioning.

teur carpenter, and made most
of the furniture in his pri-
vate residence at Mt.
Barker (S.A.)
WHAT SUSIE KNEW.
SUSIE : “ Don’t make such a noise, Tommy!”
TOMMY (who receives religious teaching at school) :
“ Before thou takest the mote out of thy
brother's eye ”
SUSIE : “ Now, none of your horrid school-slang!
You know quite well mother doesn't like it."
Geo. Alfred Lloyd, M.L.C., who died the
other day in Sydney, aged 83 years, was a cute
business man of the olden time. In* the days
when arriving ships were signalled in the primi-
tive semaphore fashion from South Head to the
Observatory Hill, Lloyd was largely engaged in
the importation of breadstuffs. One Sun-
day, so goes the ancient story, word was
brought him in church that a ship was out-
side consigned to him. Flour was then very
scarce, and on the rise. Lloyd promptly made for
the station, where he signalled the ship to keep
out for two or three days till the price of flour got
still higher in the hungry settlement. The result
was a large increase in his profits. In later years,
however, G. A. Lloyd’s luck deserted'him, and he
died comparatively poor, after long suffering from
paralysis.
G. R. Dibbs swore in the witness-box in
Arm id ale, giving evidence in the Carruthers-
IN orton libel-case, that he lost his Tam worth elec-
tion through the circulation of the canard that
he had told working-men to eat grass. Dibbs
stood twice for Tamworth. In 1894, when the
canard was in the heyday of its publicity and in-
fluence, he was elected ; in 1895, when it was dis-
proved, rejected, and practically impotent, he
was defeated. Dibbs comes very close to Wise in
the confused state of his facts.
The feature of Brisbane’s general ceme-
tery is the hill in the centre on which the local
one-time big guns are at rest—two Governors, two
J udges, several old politicians, and various private
citizens who left money enough to pay for their
half-ton monuments. Just beyond this summit,
as though anxious to be away from the crowded
ground, stands a newly-erected granite monolith
to the memory of Drury, of Q.N.B. fame, and
carved in conspicuous letters beneath the usual
formal details one reads—“ Now the laborer’s task
is o er. ’ What a task it was, too !
One millionaire, at least, is about to re-
tire from the clump of London newspaper owners,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
amidst the heartfelt thanks of every j’ournalist.
ihe pioneer ’a’penny evening daily, the Echo,
seeks to bestow .£lO,OOO annual profits on a new
proprietor, whilst Passmore Edwards, broken-
hearted by the weekly expense for paste and
scissors-sharpening, intends to devote his latter
years entirely to battling with old women and
work-girls for a seat on the ’a’penny ’bus. Seventy-
three years of age, his hat bronzed with the wear
that has greenery-yalleried his coat, mean as a
society man with a small income, Passmore passes
out of I leet-street, let us hope forever. Once a
zealous reporter rich with ‘ copy, ’ ragged through
rough usage, tore himself and some of his gar-
ments from the violence of a Trafalgar-square
meeting, and, with the shredded remnants of a
255. silk hat around his neck and ears, ripped out
a capital report for the Echo, and asked that he
might charge for a new hat. The self-advertising
philanthropist raised his hands in horror. ‘ A
new hat?’ he screamed; ‘ why, I’ve never bought
a new hatpin my life !’ The scribe removed his
concertma-’d necklace, gazed long and contemptu-
ously at the economist’s head-covering, said ‘ I
believe you,’ and retired from the Echo. The
money that should have gone in paying his em-
ployes a fair wage has been spent bv Passmore
Edwards in erecting suburban libraries, &c., with
his name glaring o’er the portal, and with the
stipulation that not only his left hand, but both
feet, each sock, and the world’s ears should know
what P.E.’s right-hand was doing.”

Librarian :
“ The current idea in
Queen Victoria’s young
womanhood, that she would
never bear children, perhaps
gave some edge to the appre-
hensions of the Duchess of Kent
for her safety. The Duchess had
morbid fears that Victoria would
be poisoned. This fearfully enraged
old William IV., because they were
chary about dining with him. In those
days the Duke of Cumberland, who had a small
following of disreputable ex-army officers and
others, really had ideas of seizing the crown by
a coup d’etat, and it is just within the limits of
possibility that his crowd would have poisoned
Victoria had the chance presented itself.”
Bishop Thornton, of Ballarat, who, accord-
ing to a puffy press claqueur, has just declined the
sixth pressing honor of a minor ecclesiastical
billet in England, was, when a young man and
perhaps less anxious for an eminently respect-
able benefice than now, twice arrested for obstruct-
ing the thoroughfares in the London slums.
Police-reports of that date are said to designate
him as an eminently vigorous street-preacher.
Died, in Wesfcralia, a few weeks
back, Dave Armstrong, a Barrier pioneer,
one of the first workmen to put a piek into
the Broken Hill Proprietary. Of course,
being a pioneer, he didn’t make a pile.
Dr. Walter Roth, author of the new
and epoch-marking book on north-west-
central Q. aborigines, was not “made in
Germany.” He was born in London in
’6l of an English mother and a naturalised
English father. The latter was one of the
Hungarian patriots who fought with Pul-
ski, Kmeti, and Kossuth, and took refuge
in England in famous ’4B.
Rabbi Davis, chief Rabbi of Sydney
Jewish Synagogue, is in the 36th year
°f his ministry in N.S.W., and is 70 years
old. It was reported recently that he
would presently retire in favor of some
younger functionary ; but the ancient
gentleman intimates that while his strength
holds out he will remain at his post, which
has a most liberal stipend attached.

John Calvert, who died on Nov. 5 in Eng-
land, aged 86, ‘ claimed that he was the discoverer
of gold m Australia.” Heavens, he could talk!
He was out here seven or eight years ago, with a
private secretary,” whose chief duty was to dis-
tribute copies of a glaring account of the old man’s
• an aw ftJ portrait of him in the
middle. Calvert was then full of a marvellous

making millions a year—or was it a week ?—out of

mg it—or thought he was.
Victorian Leg. Councillor Thomas Brunton
is notable principally for his deeply studious ex-
pression. Once he was buttonholed by a Melb.
representative citizen ” who is liberally provided
with feet, and who wanted to lay a grievance be-
fore him at great length. Brunton listened, gazing
downwards, and presently the citizen broke off
?< voPii ln °f his views and said,
Well, you needn t look at ’em ; yours are not so
small.” And now a corpulent man goes round
mentioning to all the fat men he meets that
Brunton is not the “ right-thinkin’ pusson ” he’s
cracked up to be, and ought to be opposed next
election by a man of “sound principles.”
Since Henderson Africanus died, Melb.
journalism has not boasted anything strikingly
picturesque in the shape of imported ebony, but
the local ’Ekald recently made a valuable find.
It is tall and thin with lofty cheekbones and a
weirdly fascinating smile. It was a “ hand ”on
board a ship, and somehow the ship sailed
without it one Saturday. But that night the big
Melb. fire broke out, and next day 6ft. of live mid-
night volunteered tosell ’ERALDspecials. The ebony
was dressed, as befitted the holy day, in a blue-
black belltopper (b.c.), blue-green frock-coat (valu-
able relic, uncertain era) and coke-colored trousers
the earthly ends of which flapped about the place’
where most people wear the calves of their legs.
Of course, the ebony wore white socks, and its
boots were enormous and so densely hobnailed
that when it walked the noise was like that made
by bagman Raphael’s grey pony when he ties it
to a verandah-post. But the ebony cleared 15s. on
his Sunday launch into journalism, and since
then it has gone into the business reg’lar.
It sleeps the Salvation Army’s newly-opened
“ Metropole,” but it leaves a change of clothes at
the newspaper office, for it has lost three pairs of
boots at the “ Army ” doss-house. Its most
treasured possession is an old watch and chain,
and it roosts with the watch clutched in one hand’
and the chain wound tightly round its fingers.
Mauritius, probably, bore it, and it threatens to
become a character. It had no authentic name
up to the time of going to press.
SYDNEY'S NEW MAYOR.
W. K. Simms, the rich Adelaide brewer,
who died lately, was at one time the biggest
Australian holder of Broken Hill shares, and was
still one of the biggest at the date of his death.
Died, suddenly, in S.A., Fred May, in-
ventor of May’s jigs, mostly used in concentrating
the B.H. sulphide ores. May’s and Hancock’s
machines, in fact, divide the work. The works of
the May brothers were at Gawler, S.A,, to which
town May was introduced by James Martin, at a
time when the engineering output of the place
consisted exclusively of a few agricultural imple-
ments.
The Maoriland Salvationists deny that
Philpott, accused of the murder and secret burial
of his mate, Hawthorne, was ever a full-fledged
member of the Army ; though he played in the
band and helped to spread “ the Word ” among
the Maoris. Bosher, hanged for the murder of
an old couple, at Petone, a very little while ago,
was a full-blown member of the blood -an d -blazes
brotherhood.
The best ant-eradicator is sugar mixed
with tincture of arsenic to form a paste. The
ants that .eat it die, and their arsenicated bodies
are cannibalised by the survivors, who in turn
perish, and are eaten, and pass the poison along
to their consumers. This goes on till all the colony
is dead and done for.
Dear Bulletin, —Are insects—red-back
spiders and the like—more venomous some seasons
than others? Experience out West (N.8.W.)
seems to indicate that they are and that this
summer the poison has been particularly deadly.
The list of victims is much longer than usual ;
one of those who died was C. M‘Rae, formerly
manager of Yanko and Mt. Wood, a man who
reckoned that he was proof against most of the
plagues of the bush. —X.
Indian police item from the Western
Star
A correspondent expresses amused amazement at a
street episode witnessed by him, the other day. A couple
of Pullayar women were caught in the act of cutting
grass on the roadside, which, of course is against T.I.C.
Rules ; wherefore the Nair bobby, who saw the culprits
thus infringing the law, called them to account. But as
it would be rank pollution to touch the lowly culprits
our policeman found himself in a dilemma, as he’
obviously, could not take them into custody without con-
taminating his too clean hands. The law, however, must
be vindicated ; and this was effected by the women being
made to slap themselves on their cheeks with their own
sinful hands, by order of the law’s representative—who
stood afar off while sentence was being executed.
It must be comparatively easy work to be a police-
man among a population which is so docile that it
can be made to spank itself.
It is a compliment often hurled at the Aus-
tralian weekly press that, as foster-nurseto budding
Australian genius, it usurps the place of some-
thing or other not specifically clearly stated. This
is quite true. It is claimed for the Sydney Mail
for instance, that it “discovered” Rolf Boldre-
wood, and gave, through its columns, “ Robbery
Under Arms.” It appears, from a foot-note
to a “poem” in a booklet which has
reached this office, that it also “discovered”
Ida Lee, and published, per medium of its
Christmas number (1896), that brilliant effort
of genius, “ The Bush Fire.” It will probably go
on “discovering,” and showering on a grateful
world intellectual gems of the same calibre. There
are other poems in Ida Lee’s volume besides “ The
Bush Fire,” which is itself unique. As Mark
Twain would observe, “ There is nothing like it in
the Vatican”; and, it should be safe to add,
“nor anywhere else.” An eerie suspicion arises
just here, however. Among the Sydney Mail’s
gifted ] Kiel’s contributions to eternal song there is
one entitled “ White Sea Horses.” Now is Rud-
yard plagiarising? Or did Ida Lee plagiarise
Rudyard ? This will be one of the curiosities of
literature when Kipling is forgotten.

Aboriginalities.
Dear B. —Marvellous ho w the far- back bush-
fuan, as a rule, remains immune from hydatids
and kindred diseases. Out on the runs he swal-
lows all sorts of stagnant water, mostly teeming
with insects, often covered with a fetid green
scum. The holes are few and far between, and in
hot weather he comes to each with a ravenous
thirst. He seldom carries a pannikin, and pull-
ing a ‘strainer' of grass or herbage is too
much trouble. Dropping on all fours, he dabs his
mouth in the water, and drinks like any other
animal—germs, insects, scum and bully-frogs,
just as they come. Each water has its own par-
ticular taste. One has such a decided cow-yard
flavor that you expectorate frequently after
quaffing it, and hurry to get your pipe alight.
Another is so impregnated with eucalyptus that
it seems more like a dose of medicine than a plain
drink ; while a third is simply gidgea tea, which
gives you the “ barcoo ” for a week after. Then
there are the mineral waters, each one asserting
its own little peculiarity in regard to your innards.
For color you can’t very well go wrong. They’re
all there—from lime-white to jet black. Often
the water is in mere potholes. But the thirsty
one never takes into consideration the probable
variety and condition of his predecessors—dogs
that lapped it and rolled in it; the tired old gin
who soaked her sore feet in it; sheep with can-
cered lips, oxen with pleuro pneumonia, and
crows and hawks with beaks freshly stained with
the oily entrails of the ’guana. I’ve seen it all at
different holes, and there is nothing to prevent
the lot happening at one. It makes one shudder
to think of the incongruous mixture that a man
thus carries home in his stomach after a day on
the run. I once drank at a small running stream,
on the road from Nanango to Jondaryan. It was
spring water, clear as crystal, but of rather
sweetish taste. I attributed this to the presence
of minerals. I also noticed a lot of fibrous matter
floating down. Roots of herbs, I thought. Rid-
ing towards the spring just afterwards, I dis-
covered a dead cow lying lengthwise in the
middle of the course. She was pretty well washed
out, and the water was running clean through
her—a sort of tunnel as it were. The herbage
roots, I noticed, were being dislodged from the
more ancient parts of her.—6xB.
THE BEAUTY OF HOLINESS.
SKY PILOT (to bullocky): “ Oh, my good man, it’s — ah—fearful to hear you—ah—
taking holy names in vain."
BULLOCKY (just after offering up a prayer): “Lord bless you, mister — ’tain't in

Dear Bulletin,—Saw some blacks travel-
ling lately north of Milparinka, N.S.W. The
gins were loaded with the usual paraphernalia—
wardrobe, blankets, piccaninny, tucker, and spare
weapons. The old man carried a couple of
boomerangs in his belt, a spear in one hand, and
a water-bail in the other. In no other part of
Australia have I seen him burdened with a water-
bag. The country must be passing dry when the
aboriginal monarch gets down to that. — Werri-
MEE.
Out in the far North-West a zealous Baptist
farmer named Gumridge lent a corner of his pad-
dock to the dippers whereon to build a chapel. A
boss Baptist came up and declared it well and
truly laid, blessed it, and took up a collection. A
few days afterwards a breeze came down a certain
gorge to the west, lifted the chapel up and took it
over the fence into Smith’s paddock. Smith ob-
jected, so the sect hitched on a couple of bulls and
dragged the building back to Gumridge’s. Every-
thing went along all right after that until one
Sunday, just as the audience was leaving and the
parson was counting the funds on the back step,
a gentle cyclone, accompanied by a whirlwind’
carried the doomed church skyward, spun it round
a few times, and landed it on its head over in
Smith’s. The whirlwind then went back for the
parson and blew him over among the ruins, where
he was found in a dazed condition clutching the
collection in both hands. Smith was very much
annoyed at this second invasion, and said it would
give a bad tone to his farm if they didn’t at once
remove the wreck. The congregation then
organised a bee and threw the church back
over the fence bit by bit, and decided
they would abandon the luckless tabernacle
for good. Gumridge volunteered to build
her up again, which he did, even putting in
a new bell and rope. But it was no go ; the folks
weren’t taking any. Then Gumridge opened the
front door and turned it into a cow-shelter. One
Sunday after this the inhabitants were astonished
to hear the chapel-bell tolling about 10.45, and
thinking some celebrated dipper had come up on
a special trip, and the weather being calm, they
all dressed up, and, armed with Bibles, started off
to church. When they arrived at the old familiar
entrance, they found the chapel wasn’t there.
They could still hear the bell tolling, however, in
the hazy distance, and at length discovered Gum-
ridge’s black cow dragging the church round the
paddock by the bell-rope, which had become
twisted round her leg during the night. Gum-
ridge cut the rope, and remarked that, as the
building was now handier to his homestead, he
would turn it into a milking-shed. The people
returned home rather disgusted. What they want
now—and want it badly—is another wind to come
across and carry the old cathedral clean out of the
district. And they wouldn’t be very sorry if
Gumridge’s black cow went along with it.

The beetle-catching industry is proving a
golden harvest to the freckled kiddies of Banana-
land sugar districts. The youngsters were first
paid so much a score or so much a hundred
beetles, but the wearied officials finally decided to
give up counting and pay the urchins by weight.
And every boy in the beetle industry immediately
commenced to soak his beetles in water over-
night, and the beetle, being absorbent, grows
heavy, and fetches double price when pay-day
comes.

* * * *
Jacky grinned from ear to ear as he
entered the store. “ You gib it bottle gin—tick
um up,” he suggested to the storekeeper in his
most seductive manner. “No fear, Jacky; no
tick here. Bring the money or clear out,” re-
sponded the storekeeper severely. “ Gib it bottle,”
persisted the uncrowned king. “That one gin
mine been have um baby.” The storekeeper’s
daughter looked interested. “Poor thing,” she
remarked, and the father, relenting,' said, “ All
right, give him a small bottle. I suppose we’ll
never get paid, but, under the circumstances, let
him have it.” The girl got down the square face,
and handing it over to Jacky, followed him to the
door. “When did she have the baby, Jacky;
this morning?” she asked as the dusky one
stepped into the street. “ Hoo, no, gin have um
baby long timo. Baby grown up now.”
* * * *
Anyone who loves to write poetry about
the bushman’s lonely grave should see an abori-
ginal “ mourning.” A blackfellow comes in with
news of the death of a countryman. The gins at
once commence a blood-curdling howl, which anon
drops into a dismal chant or death-song. They
throw themselves down in apparent anguish, some-
times on top of another noisy mourner, when they
bash and kick and hug one another, and roll over
into agonies. This lasts perhaps an hour. The
males squat round the fires, beating time on the
ground with sticks, or with their hands, their
heads bowed down —silent. When the news ar-
rives early in the day a preliminary “mourning ”
is gone through, and the “ big feller cry ” begins
in earnest about nightfall. w hen it’s all over,
another black, ignorant of what has been done,
may arrive with the same sad news. Then the
cheerful function and all the variations have to be
gone through again—the little fellow cry and the
big fellow cry and all the rest of it.
Dear Bulletin, Besides many other
things, a drought has a fine knack of upsetting
bush signs of rain. “ When the sugar gets damp,”
they say in the bush, “it’s going to rain.” So it
is, according to mulga weather-prophets, when
ants run up a tree, and frogs quawk at night, and
when the flies are bad. When your corns ache,
and rheumatic pains run up and down your spine,
and black cockatoos screech and fly westward, it’s
a dead certainty to rain soon ; and when ground
spiders cover their holes, and there’s a ring round
the moon, you can lay heavy odds that it’ll rain
within the next 24 hours. Should your dog bark
at a quarter to 1 a.m., it’s the deadest certainty of
all that it 11 rain, and you’d better repair your
spoutings and stuff up the leak in the roof before
daylight breaks. These “ signs ” are undeniably
THE SHANTY-INDICATOR.
THIRSTY CYCLIST (after a twenty -miles run out back): “We’re nearing a
pub. at last."
“ montes ” when rain is close at hand or shortly
after it begins to come down ; but in time of
drought they’re the most unreliable tokens of wet
weather or anything else on this beautiful earth.
For the past four or five months I’ve noticed every
one of these “signs” in their turns, but up to-
date they’ve all turned out miserable failures. Not
a drop of rain has fallen to uphold their reputa-
tions. But, perhaps, ’97 happens to be the very
year in which their term of service has expired,
and the new “signs” which have replaced them
are still unknown, and have not yet had an oppor-
tunity to prove their reliability.—6 xB.

Messrs. J. Albert and Sons, the well-
known Sydney firm at 118 King-street, claim to
have revolutionised the musical trade in their
town by selling good articles at low prices. They
are retailers and wholesalers of pretty well every-
thing from a jews-harp to a church organ.
Specialities are violins, mandolines, autoharps ;
while of guitars zithers, anglos, flutes, accor-
deons, cellos, cornets, banjos and strings, there is
an immense collection, sold on time-payment or
for spot cash. The firm is particularly proud of its
very own Boomerang and King Billy mouth-
organs, which have brought Joy to Bill and Jim,
not to mention Augustus. Also, the firm sells all
sheet music at a much-reduced rate ; and the har-
mony with which it has flooded Australia is a fact
which takes the same size in corsets as Kosciusko.*
PRETENSION.
In the long run a man almost always passes in
the world for what he is personally and morally
worth. Very idle is all curiosity as to what other
people may think of us, and all fear that we may
remain unknown. What we can do, and are
doing, is seen and virtually acknowledged by
those who know us, and with whom we are from
day to day associated. The world is full of judg-
ment days ; and in all the relations and actions of
life, men are gauged and stamped, and their true
value understood.
All men and all things must stand on their
respective merit. Among the scientific agents
which have stood the test is Warner’s Safe Cure.
Mr. D. Giovannoni, the well-known vigneron, of
Daylesford, Vic., reports : “ I was suffering
from a bad liver, and used Warner’s Safe Cure to
great advantage, and can recommend this medicine
to anyone suffering from the same.”

The Antipodean makes its annual reap-
pearance this year after a considerable break in its
existence (this way of stating the case is distinctly
Irish, and may be set down to the disturbing
influences of the ’9B centenary). The book is well
printed and admirably illustrated by such artists
as Frank P. Mahony, Geo. W. Lambert, A. J.
Fischer, Percy Spence, and Alf. Vincent; and it
contains a couple of reproductions from etchings
by Livingston Hopkins, many reproductions of
capital photographs, and some drawings to illus-
trate an article on Polynesian art by C.
Hedley, F.L.S. The reading matter consists
of 17 contributions in all, many of them by
well-known Bulletin writers. Probably the
best of the prose is Roderic Quinn’s “On the
Dividing Chain,” but “Steele Rudd’s” tale of
a selection and Dyson’s “ Fossicker ’’areclose up ;
also, Essex Evans’ “ Gulf Idyll.” Henry Lawson’s
“ Daughter of Maoriland ” is a blighting satire on
Hinemoa, that legendary sweet brown girl, who
has filled these provinces with so much beautiful
delusion, and made so many readers forget the
fact that the head of the Maori maiden is apt to
be rancid with much old whale-grease, or some
similar compound. Also, there are two poems by
“Banjo” Paterson. “ Saltbush _ Bill’s Second
Fight ” has many streaks of humor in it, and,
if it isn’t quite so desperate an encounter
as Bill’s first conflict, that probably arises
from the fact that even Bill can’t keep
up to quite the same level of ferocity all the time.
Saltbush Bill, if followed up, might become an
enduring personality like Bret Harte’s Jack Ham-
lin and Colonel Starbottle. He seems to have
good lasting qualities about him. J. B. O’Hara
and Miss Ethel Turner are among the contri-
butors, and various people supply articles on
various subjects of deep Australian erudition—
Meston on the local aborigine being among the
most interesting of the lot. Also, veteran J.
Brunton Stephens contributes a poem which com-
pares well with the best of his early work.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
When the Bogan sent a cricket-team, the other
day, to play the men of dreary Dandaloo, it was found
that the Dandaloonatic bats had gone a-missing, and the
match was played with pick-handles. The top score was
a whole half-dozen.
* * * *
An umpire story. The city team was playing the
Gum-Flat Excelsiors and their crack batsman came in. In
the first over he was obviously caught, but the umpire said,
“ Oh, I’ve no doubt he hit the ball; but I’ve ’eard this
young feller is a great bat, and I want to see wot ’e can
do. Not out! ”
* * • •
In Victoria the Law seems conducive to cricket.
Bruce and Johns are solicitors, and James Quirk, captain
of Melb. University team for years, has only come out of
his shell since his recent admission as a barrister. His
latest score is 94.
* * * *
Cynic: “ Cricket consists of 11 grown men
running after something worth 2s. 6d.” Another cynic :
“ Cricket consists in running all day after something, and
throwing it away as soon as you get it.”
* » • •
A very clever timber jock., whose mounts are
always backed by the lambs, has been losing for the last
12 months in a most masterly manner. One day he will
accidentally give some steward a pill, and then—down
goes M‘Ginty.
* * * •
A broken-down Melb. bookie recently induced a
money-lender to advance him £3 on the only pawnable
asset he and his Leah had left in the world, i.e., his wife’sen-
gagement-ring. He wanted the money to make up their
joint fares to the West, but finding he was still short he
“ gave the £3 a chance ” the same day at Aspendale. He
backed five winners, won £llO, and is happy. A very rare
instance of fortune favoring a last-chance gamble.
* * * »
Dear Bulletin, —I am quite of your opinion ;
the time has arrived for Tatt.’s to protect the public from
the barefaced robbery they are subject to. Why not drop
the horse element altogether and make them all money-
prizes at once, and so strike a death-blow to the barnacles.
The horse element is quite unnecessary.—A. B.

Rudyard Kipling, in one of his “Plain Tales
from the Hills,” makes one of his characters a Victorian
jockey, who was smashed up whilst riding over the stiff
jumps in the Maribyrnong Plate ! Next.
« * * •
A beautiful moral lesson is embodied in the fact
that there are eleven heavy professional backers of race-
horses confined in Pentridge at the present time, but not
a single bookie of any pretentions.
*'m * *
A Northern jockey had just lost a coveted race
that he had tried his best to win. He had been in the
habit of advising the owner to win small races at first,
but the owner had acted contrariwise and won a big event
(for that part of the country). This had weighted the
horse, and the specially-coveted stake was lost. Jockey
led the horse back into the saddling-paddock, with down-
cast face. Owner: “ Well, you didn’t doit?” “ No, an’
it serves you bloomin’ well right. You wasn’t content
with a cottage at a time, but you mus’ go an’ snavel a
bloomin’ terris in one shot an’ get hover-’andicapped.
Bah ! you blokes knows nothin’.”
* * * *
Few pugilists have the knack of teaching
the noble art in addition to being first-class with both
knuckles and gloves. Mace was a wonder with knuckles,
but, by the time he had finished that portion of the game,
his hands were smashed up, and, when glove-fighting
came in vogue, he had the good sense to travel on his
past phenomenal reputation. When Jem came to Aus-
tralia his hands were too far gone even for teaching, and
the writer has seen him wince again when he jarred
them in setting-to with a pupil—whereupon James
usually brought the lesson to a close with a hard punch
from the other hand. A broken-handed man of even
Mace’s wonderful science is about as useful as a Carbine
with faulty feet. Larry Foley stands, perhaps, as the one
man who has succeeded at the three phases of the game-
knuckles, gloves, and teaching. Captain Macdonald was
chock-full of sound theory, which Larry had the sense to
pick up ; and, when Mace came along with the practical
portion of the game, it put the finishing touch on Foley.
Larry proved himself a first-class man both in the fields
and on the boards, and when he took to the teaching
business, he turned out such men as Fitzsimmons, Peter
Jackson, Griffo, George Seale, Slavin, Dooley, Jack Hall,
Jem Hall, Jack Molloy, and others too numerous to men-
tion. A truly brilliant record.
* » • *
The news of Dan Creedon’s defeat by “ Kid ”
M‘Coy, the Yankee boxer, comes as no surprise to The
Bulletin. Modern glove-fighting has proved that the
old-time style of stocky, short-reached fighter has no
show with the modern six-footer under Queensberry
rules. M‘Coy, who is stated to be a very clever fighter,
must be the beau-idtal of a modern middleweight,
spreading his lOst. 101 b. over sft. llin. in height. Creedon
never showed himself a “ clever ” boxer in this country,
and the writer ventures to predict that, when details
arrive, it will be found M‘Coy landed when and where he
liked on the slow, short Australian.
* * * »
The dispute between Melb. City Council and
the Crown authorities re the opening of a road right to
the gates of the M.C.C. Ground is peihaps the most fat-
headed deadlock in Australia at present. Nobody knows
the exact circumstances of the case, and nobody can
understand why a road is not opened, but there is a vague
idea that the dignity of the City Council is at stake, and
the Mayor would be lowered in the popular estimation in
some way if that road were opened. Nobody ever sus-
pected the Council of having any dignity, but, all the
same, the public has to get out of its cab afar off, and
wade through the dust to the ground, and the Australian
adjective gives the atmosphere a crimson tint. The
other day a cab was wanted at the ground gate for a
busted cyclist, and the sports-managers had to ’phone to
the town-clerk for permission to bring that vehicle into
the park, and, by the time permission arrived, the
damaged rider had gone home on a barrow, or some-
thing of the kind.
(Continued on page 24).
NORMAN HARDY.
fhe above is a representation of Norman
Hardy, for the last six years special artist of the
Sydney Mail, to which paper he has contributed
many admirable sketches of Australian life and
scenery. Mr. Hardy started his art career before
he was out of infantile petticoats, but the only art
school in which he studied was that of 1 red
Brown, then President of the New English Art
Club which has counted among its members,
Whistler, Wilson Steer, Stott, and other eccen-
trics. For the rest Mr. Hardy followed his in-
stincts ; and, considering that his grandfather,
father, mother and brother (Paul Hardy) are all
votaries of Art, it was natural that his instincts
should lead him a very considerable way on the
path to success. Mr. Hardy, who is leaving for
England, says he has enjoyed in Australia six
most pleasant years, and, from a social point of
view,much regrets leaving a country where he has
made friends so many and so valued.

WIDESPREAD IN ITS EFFECT
SYDNEY CRICKETERS AFFECTED.
Occasional hot days are naturally expected
dn summer, but it is unusual to experience
.such enervating and oppressive heat as has
prevailed in Melbourne during the last few ■
days. Many strange things have happened,'
but nothing is more singular than for a:
cricketer born and' bred in Sydney to be
seriously affected by the Melbourne
weather,

WHO ARE THE FREE ?
&

Those who have tongues that never lie,
Truth on the lip, truth in the eye;
To Friend or to Foe,
To all above, and to all below.
These are the happy, these are the free,
So may it be with thee and me.

TESTIMONIAL.
Maldon (Victoria), 21st September, 1896.
I have much pleasure in stating that the South
German Gold Mining Company has used Griefen-
dorfTs “KEPRO” to paint the inside of our
Cyanide Vats to save the timber from absorbing the
cyanide and gold liquor. We are pleased to state
that we found it admirably suited for that purpose,
and can safely recommend it to others.
W. B. GRAY,
Managing Director South German G.M. Co., Maldon.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Judge Chubb (Q.) : “There is not in
Queensland one newspaper whose reports you can
take as accurate.”
“ However much a political party may
gain by making promises, it always loses by ful-
filling them.”—Joe Chamberlain.
British Premier Salisbury is said to be far
gone with diabetes. If he collapses, the chance
of Judas Chamberlain should have about arrived.
Scavengers in Melb. city work, as a rule,
between 12 and 13 hours a day, and get ss. for it.
Melb. City Council is the wealthiest corporation
and about the hardest incorporated sweater in
Australia.
Q. Labor-member Dunsford’s latest raw-
meat metaphor: “The Minister for Obstruction
mutilated what was at the first a misshapen abor-
tion, and the Premier, on his return, had gouged
out its eyes and cut out its tongue.”
Vic. : ‘ ‘ Gillies and Shack ell are yet the
champion Damon and Pythias. Their amity, like
that of Brougham and Lyndhurst and other dis-
tinguished ones, began in enmity. Shackell spent
£3OOO trying to disqualify Gillies for Rodney.”
A TIGHT PLACE .
CONTORTIONIST REID : “ I've wriggled through a
few in my time, but this is a teaser! ”
Untrustworthy rumor has it that Q. Labor-
leader Glassey intends contesting Toowoomba
against Groom at the next elections. Groom has
held his seat for 35 years and is the father of the
House, also its grandfather and most of its other
ancestors.
The death of G. A. Lloyd still leaves nine
N.S.W. ex-Treasurers living—See, M‘\Tillan,
Bruce Smith, Burns, Dibbs, Saul Samuel, W. A.
Long, H. E. Cohen and James Watson. When
Reid meets the deserts of his awful finance and
becomes “ ex ” also he will make ten.
N.S.W. Government is preparing a big
irrigation scheme according to which canals are to
be carried across the mournful plains that lie about
Hay, Jerilderie and Moulamein. Considering the
nature of the country, this should be a nice, easy
plaoe for the first big irrigation experiment.
Vic. Legislative Council complains that the
income-tax is driving capital out of the country
and produces less and less revenue every year.
The tax actually collected was .£140,000 in ’95;
£167,000 in ’96 and £179,000 in ’97, so the 48
Rich Landlords seem beastly inaccurate as usual.
Q. boss Tozer :
If the State-aided grammar schools are to remain in
the province, it becomes the duty of the Government not
enly to find the money but to find, the boys and girls.
!!! Seems as if there is a text here on which a
happy and well-informed viceregal speech might
be made.
N.S.W. Crown Law Department has pecu-
liar ways in criminal prosecutions. In the sedition
case against John Norton, Pilcher, Q.C., engaged
at a big fee, prosecuted at the police-court, while
a junior (Wade) conducted the Crown case at the
Superior Court. Again, in Carruthers v. Norton,
Pilcher, Q. C., again appeared at the police-court,
and two juniors (Garland and Noble) at the Court
above.
The Maoriland Old Age Pensions Bill had
1021 speeches made about it in the House of Re-
presentatives before it got through. The small
Tory Opposition made 509 of these in the course
of its bitter stonewall, and the waverers who are
described as the Government Left Wing made
200. Then the Council made about two casual
remarks and threw the measure out.
John Douglas, resident magistrate at
Thursday Island: “My own impression is that
all coast tovvns will receive contingents of
Japanese ladies, who, though they behave very
properly, are not a desirable addition to our popu-
lation.” Considering the occupation of these
little brown ladies who behave very properly what,
in Douglas’s opinion, is the kind of lady who be-
haves improperly ?
If Dr. Cockburn goes to England as S.A.
Agent-General, and Playford returns to Australia,
the probability is that it will simply be an amic-
able exchange, of billets. Cockburn will take
Playford’s job in London, while Honest Tom will
run for Cockburn’s seat at Mount Barker, and, if
he gets in, take Cockburn’s portfolio. Playford
is 60 years of age now, and has been leaving his
large footmark on S.A. politics for 30 years.
It is now openly admitted that Joseph
Chamberlain’s scheme of imperial union was
simply Ereetrade within the empire and a Protec-
tionist. tariff, uniform throughout the British
dominions, against everybody outside, which
would be simply straight-out Protection-as it is
understood in such countries as Russia and the
United States. They, also, have internal Free-
trade and Protection against everybody outside.
Queensland Minister Barlow, M.L.C., told
the Leg. Council recently how a man once came
to him with a claim for an electoral right to be
attested, and said, “You’re a J.P. ; sign this.”
He refused. Pat Perkins: “What difference
would one vote have made?” Barlow: “One
vote might elect a member of the Assembly. One
M. might become a Minister. One Minister
might do a lot of interfering with the rights and
privileges of the Council.” i
The Federal Convention meets this month,
and the finance committee, which was to be ready
with some scheme to get over the awful Federal
money difficulty, is, at time of writing, no further
advanced than it was at the beginning. Reid, of
N. however, “thinks” that Walker, of
N.S.W., has a scheme partially prepared. The
other members of the committee, apparently, are
not even so far advanced as to think that some
other fellow is moving in the matter.
Victoria has a specially red-hot law against
bribery and corruption “at Parliamentary elec-
tions.” The courts, however, have just decided
that this term only covers the short period be-
tween the day of nomination and the day of
election, and before nomination-day any candi-
date can be as corrupt as he pleases. If this re-
markable decision holds water an amending act
is wanted in hot haste, else nobody but the Fab
candidate will have much show hereafter.
After nearly two months’ earnest canvass-
ing for signatures to a congratulatory address to
A. I. Clark, Tas. ex-Attorney-General, the
Hobart committee announces that it has 1500
names. Hobart Mercury chronicles the fact and
remarks:—
Many more could have been had, hut for the extraor-
dinary difficulty of finding many of the people, even of
such a small city as Hobart.
Probably they couldn’t be found because they
were in bed.
The very latest technical objection to Vic.
Eactories’ Act was raised last week by a big Mel-
bourne tailor. The Act stipulates that the
factory or shop door shall be closed at a certain
time, but it does not say who is to close it, so
the tailor pleaded that he was relieved of all re-
sponsibility in the matter, and the man-in-the-
street was just as blameworthy for the shop being
open as the proprietor himself ! And this wondrous
piece of logic was duly “ noted ” by an allegedly
sane magistrate!
Faiz Mahomet, boss Afghan merchant of
Westralia, kicks at the proposed alien legislation
of that province. He recounts all the good things
the Afghan camel men have done in opening up
the goldfields of the country, and even makes a
proposal to clear the lot—lock, stock, and barrel-
out of W. A. if reasonably compensated. Also, he
alludes to other colored races in the country, and,
in his classification, he puts down Japanese as
“ Laundrymen and cool-drink women.” As if a
Mahomet didn’t know better than that.
Saul Samuel gets a New Year’s baronetcy.
Australia can hardly be kept loyal by K.C.M.G.*
ships much longer, for K.C.M.G.-ships are so
numerous that hardly anyone weeps with joy now
on getting one, and even Privy Councillorships
are growing common. A judicious sprinkling of
baronetcies —-just enough to make every loyal poli-
tician feel that he may get one, yet so few that
the commonness of the thing won’t make him feel
sick when he does get it—will help materially to
make the “bonds ” hang out a while longer.
H. : “ What a barbarism it was, in Melb.,
for us to be left without a morning newspaper twc
whole days—Christmas Sunday and Boxing Day !
Up in China they were making history at a furious
rate, and the news was kept on hand from Satur-
day to Tuesday with as much indifference as the
stale butchers’ meat and bread. This sort of thing
is a relic of the old pre-newspaper world. Very
soon the Sunday paper will become a necessary of
life. My ideal is the Chicago newspaper office
which never shuts its door, like an ear always
listening.”
Apropos of a recent slander-case :
Under an old English law which still pre-
vails in Maorilaiul a woman who is
slandered by being called immoral
and unchaste has no redress un-
less she can show that the slan-
der caused her to lose her
situation. She may lose her
peace of mind, her friends
and her intended husband
through it, but the bagman
instinct of the law takes no
account of these ; the only
thing it takes account of
is her billet. Verily the
law is a—commercial tra-
veller.
The latest sew ei
catastrophe has in
spired Premier Turne
with a determination to
race through with a
Workmen’s Insurance
Bill. And it is about
time. Fitzgibbon’s drains
run blood by now, having
killed over a score, and
wounded and maimed many
more. Possibly the House
of Pat Men would not dare
to fight such a measure to a
finish, if it could be put forth
whilst public sentiment is still
warm, but, unluckily, it can only
come along six months hence, when the
great public heart will be beating violently
over a cricket - match or a new burlesque,
and these four dead men will no longer wield
any political influence worth mentioning.
Premier Nelson alludes to tlie prosecuting
of the individuals responsible for the Q.N. Bank
disasters as “dirty work.” And the Brisbane
Courier quite logically remarks that if the prose-
cution be “dirty work,” then all the antecedent
enquiry and tabling of reports is “ dirty work ”
also. This stigmatising the execution of justice
as “ dirty work ” is a truly fine effort. Did any
Queensland Minister lament about the dirty work
which the Government undertook when it prose-
cuted the leaders of the shearers’ strike a few
years ago ?
Ex-Governor Norman has resigned the
Queensland Agency-General, on the ground that
he is very old (71 years) arid battered and war-
worn, and is in much need of rest. At latest
advices Horace Tozer was reaching out for the
billet. The great trouble in the Queensland
Ministry is that Tozer and Byrnes can’t be safely
left alone together for fear of breakages, and since
Byrnes is coming back the best thing, is obviously
for Tozer to get out. The latter has now had
consecutive years of office, so he has had his fair
share of the bread and the whale.
A canvasser, giving evidence before the
Elections and Qualifications Committee in Melb.
the other day, said he had gained a lot of votes
for White, M.L.A., at Albert Park, by tel-
ling the electors that White once lived in a tent,
and slept on the hard ground under a bit of can-
vas. So the electors pictured White as a horny
pioneer, and voted for him. Yet the real White
is a fat and flabby old man with the most terrific
monkey-shave in the province, and his tangled
sentences are nearly all without predicates, and
have no object worth mentioning.
MOSES IN THE BULRUSHES.
(A suburban character.)
Bryan O'Loghlen, recently returned again
to Vic. Assembly after three years of private life,
is the one Victorian politician who has learnt ab-
solutely nothing from the crash of the boom.
O’Loghlen continually bewails the cessation of
THE ADVENT OF SUMMER.
The N.S. W. school-teacher as swimming instructor.
borrowing, and can’t understand why any Minister
should be troubled for ready cash when the British
Hebrew is just bursting with millions which he
would be glad to lend. Bryan’s idea of good
Government is to borrow early and often, whilst a
willing lender remains on the face of Europe.
“ Borrow money and build railways,” is Bryan’s
war-cry.
The lately-deceased. “Hoppy” Hopkins,
of Geelong, was one of the men whom the Agb
ousted from Vic. Assembly when J. B. Patterson
made his appeal to a. scornful “country.”
Hopkins had been casually referred to in the
almighty organ as a sleeping iguana, or something
of that sort; anyway, the description was so
damaging that Geelong lost faith in its wealthy
representative, and cast him forth. The rich old
man’s seat seemed so sure, and the idea of Geelong
rousing sufficient energy to eject any established
institution seemed so wild that, when news came
of Hopkins’ defeat, the Patterson party recognised
that all was over.
Apropos of Premier Reid’s idea of turning
the N.S.W. Council into a mild, unobstructive
and comparatively democratic body by making its
members irresponsible. nominees for a term of
years instead of irresponsible nominees for life,
Maoriland adopted Reid’s temporary - nominee
system in ’9l, and since theh all appointments
have been made under it. The visible result is
that the Council, last session, put up a record by
throwing out every democratic measure presented
to it. The nominee who doesn’t represent the
people and isn’t responsible to the people is about
as hopeless a case whether he is a snag for life or
only a snag for a term of years.
A Tipperary man, Pennefather, is chief
law-officer in Westralia, where it has just been
decided for the first time in sand-groping history
that murder of the blacks by a white is a crime
to be met by legal retribution. Murder of
blacks by whites in N.S.W. was first stamped
with full legal reprobation in the Attorney-
Generalship of the Roscommon man Plunkett.
Plunkett’s fight for the recognition of the principle
that an aboriginal is entitled to a decent chance of
dying at a good old age by the natural process of
rum was fought in the face of opposition which
iems incredible nowadays. Myall Creek station
ost seven of its hands to the gallows when the
point was finally settled.
Westralia preserves a rigorous quarantine
against all manner of Eastern pests, which, how-
ever, seem to get there just the same. Rabbits
have crossed the border and got as far as Esper-
ance and Norseman—a serious matter for the pas-
toralists, who have a hard struggle to raise any-
thing at the best of times. Tick has made its
appearance in East Kimberley, coming overland
from the Northern Territory. The fruit-fly,
brother demon to the codlin moth, is established,
and the codlin moth cannot long be kept out, in
view of the smuggling of fruit by passengers.
Also, a member of the Assembly recently an-
nounced the appearance of sparrows in Perth, and
asked the House to take action.
The Vic. Government’s proposal to appoint
a board to overhaul and re-classify the public ser-
vice aroused sundry Oppositionists to demand
indignantly why Turner didn’t do the work him-
self. Yet Turner attempts to do so many things
already that he has hardly time to get his clothes
off at night, and his fate, if he sticks at it, is
obviously to fall down dead at the pump like
Ballance. The willing horse who sits up till the
small hours of the morning pegging away at State
matters (patent metaphor) gets very little con-
sideration. Nelson, of Queensland, who lounges
through his sunshiny existence, is never harassed
with demands that he should “do it himself.”
The idea of Nelson doing anything has never yet
occurred to anybody.

LAND AND INCOME TAX ASSESSMENT ACT OF 1895.
'M'OTICE is hereby given, under section 30 of the above
Act, that Returns of Income received during the year
1897 for the" purposes of the assessment of Income Tax for
the year 1898ifcre required to be furnished to the Commis-
sioners of Taxation, at their office, on or before the Uh
day of Februjary, 1898 ; and all persons who are in receipt
of income, arK all persons liable under the provisions of
the Act in ady representative capacity to furnish returns
for the purposes aforesaid, are hereby informed that the
prescribed Forms of Returns are now available, and, ex-
cept those for Companies, may be obtained on application,
at any Post Office, Police Office, Customs House, Railway
Station,' and'the Office of any Clerk of Petty Sessions in
New South .Wales, Stamp Duties Office, Phillip-street,
Sydney, or at the Taxation Office, Custom House, Sydney.
Forms for Companies can be obtained at the Stamp
Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at the Taxation
Office, Custom House, Sydney.
All persons carrying on any business, trade, or pro-
fession on their own account are required to furnish In-
come Returns irrespective of the amount of their income ;
but no Retard of Income is required from any person in re-
ceipt of salary or usages only if the gross annual amount
of such salary or wages does not exceed £2OO.
All employers of labor, whether they-themselves are
liable to make' Incotne Tax Returns or not, are required to
furnish to the Commissioners of- Taxation the name,
occupation, aiid’address of all employees whose salaries or
wages for the ygar 1897 aggregated £l6O and upwards.
Public attention is called to the fact that a penalty not
exceeding Twenty Rounds is imposed on any person
neglecting to furnish the Return required of him within
the time above mentioned, and that persons failing to
furnish such Return are not relieved from liability to the
penalty by. reason only that they may not have received a
notice to furnisgßthe Return, or that the prescribed Form
may not have been delivered to them.
The attention.of owners of taxable, lands subject to mort-
gage is invited to the necessity for furnishing the requisite
particulars, in order that they may derive the benefit of
the deduction toVwhich they are entitled in respsct of the
interest on thefjpiQrtgage. of their lands, including im-
provements thercSm; Without these particulars the Com-
missioners will be unable to make the statutory deduction
from the tax on the unimproved values.
Forms of the prescribed Return may be obtained on
application at any of the abovementioned offices, and
should be furnished on or before the above date.
N. LOCKYER,
R. N. JOHNSON,
L. S. SPILLER,
