Dear Bulletin, —I meant to say some-
thing in reply to “R.’s” remarks re the “bard
who does nothing at all.” But since the “ bard
who does nothing at all,” doesn’t do any barding
and isn’t of bardic or any other account, his very
existence demolishes “R.” more effectually than
anything I could say.—6 xB.
From S.M. Herald’s remarks upon the
action of Maoriland Legislative Council in re-
jecting the Old Age Pensions Bill, one would
think that awful gang of depraved old men
was a popular Chamber of hairy Socialists
dragging a slow-going Assembly by the ear along
its Democratic track. The Herald has so often
told the lean old lie that M.L. Council represents
the people that at last it believes it to be true.
The Old Age Pensions Bill was not “a leap in the
dark.” At present, M.L. and N.S.W. both pro-
vide for their destitute old people (provided the
said d.o.p. make noise enough), in a sort of round-
about way, by sending them, at the public expense,
to gaols or benevolent asylums—to wit, Liverpool
and other dreadful places. And the O.A.P. Bill
tot ? lerely an a^eni Pt to do decently what both
M.L. and N.S.W. are at present doing in a
hashed-up, indecent way, at about the same cost.
Dear Bulletin,— You put forward only
one side of the game when you refer to Graham
Berry s choice — when you say a man of his ability
would have worked that South Yarra grocery into
something better than all his honors put together
long ere this. Surely there is something beyond
the mere coin to be looked at. Surely the man
who does what Berry has done has his reward—
outside and above all pecuniary considerations !
Berry, as a great “universal provider,” probably
would have died a semi-millionaire, but it is
doubtful if Berry’s thoughts on Berry would in
that case have been pleasant thinking. There
could be no choice between Graham Berry as we
know him and Councillor Berry, ex-Mayor of
Prahran ; proprietor of the big supply stores, with
a mansion and a carriage and a fat bingie. The
old Tribune has his reward, though few of us can
appreciate it!— F. Croson.
Ellis, ex-M.L.A., in the correspondence
column of Sydney Telegraph :
Each loan has been issued on the good faith that certain
public works would represent the borrowings and the ex-
penditure. In what way are we keeping faith with .the
public creditor ? As the loans fall due we renew them for
the full amount, notwithstanding that a large proportion'
of the works are now not in existence. Bridges built 30
3’ears since on borrowed money have been worn out, and
rebuilt on newly-borrowed money, and the Government
has not hesitated to even carry out repairs with borrowed
money. Every conceivable sort of public work has been
treated in the same way. To clearly define what is going
on I will refer to the shipping appliances at Newcastle.
Over 30 years since steam-cranes were erected with bor-
rowedmonev; those cranes paid forthemselves many times
over ; but when they were worn out new hydraulic cranes
were erected, but with borrowed money again, and New-
castle stands debited with two sets of cranes, although
only one set is in existence. The present Government re-
newed a loan in which was originally included coal shoots
at Newcastle and Morpeth ; but although these are W'orn
out and not now in existence, the money is re-borrowed.
All our public assets, such as tugs, dredges, wharves, &c.,
are treated in the same way, and it is an everyday occur-
rence to see alterations and repairs carried out with !
freshjy borrowed money. The late Mr. Eddy eompla ne 1
that the railways were unduly weighted in the capital
account. Why ? Because rolling stock and rails, work-
shops, machinery, tools, &c., had originally been pro- |
cured with borrowed money, and, when worn out, replen-
ished with fresh borrowings.
A fortnight ago, in Brisbane, John Douglas
stated that divers in Torres Straits often attained
a depth of 30 fathoms, and sometimes exceeded it.
Outside time at that depth, five minutes; often
only three. No less than 20 divers lost their lives
during the last year. , r

SYDNEY.


The Prahran (Vic.) police have scored a
record. One day last week a hardened ruffian,
aged four, and his interesting victim, a little girl
of six, were dragged before the local Bench, charged
with “impropriety. ” The Bench heard the sicken-
ing details, and then declared the charge to be
ridiculous, and dismissed the depraved rouA of four,
and told his victim to toddle about her business.
And The Bulletin asks, in a voice of thunder,
why heartless libertines like this are permitted to
escape just punishment, and are set at liberty to
further pollute our homes? Also, where is the
W.C.T.U., and the Social Purity Society, and
our boasted civilisation in general ?
“ O.” to The Bulletin :
Brunton Stephens’ criticism on the bulk of Australian
verse is interesting- and just. Is it not equally just as
regards the bulk of all verse ? So much easier is it to
imitate or to pull to pieces than to create. Any savage
can do the former ; it takes a god, material or otherwise,
to do the latter. So easy is it to find fault—so hard to
enunciate a flawless remedy. This holds good for every-
thing, including politics (which are not poetry)— e.g.,
W. J. Lyne. Versifying is like measles—we all catch it,
sooner or later, some worse than others. We catch the
rhyming eruption—but the ideas ! no mere heating of
the blood can bring them to the surface. They enter from
without. Gleams and scents from other worlds, they are
not part of man’s corrupt clay. And when the fever
takes us, if the afflatus be not there, what can we do bub
rail and write rubbish ? .
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*

When the player of young hero characters is notoriously
growing old, paragraphists usually exaggerate the truth.
Somebody states that Kyrle Bellew must be “ nearly 60.’’
Why must Bellew be so old? The tale goes that he sub-
mitted his first serious attempt at acting to (among others)
Marcus Clarke, who would presumably be quite as old as
young Bellew at the time. Well, Marcus Clarke was born
in 1846. The writer remembers Bellew in London as a
man who didn’t look more than 30, off the stage, 17 years
ago. And considering that the curly one was then
(alas, with great success) “mashing” the writer’s girl, he
couldn’t have concealed very much of his age from the
jaundiced eye of the observer.
James Carter Edwards, who delivers much gusty blank
verse in “ Claudian,” should be remembered by old Eng-
lish playgoers as a member of an erstwhile company that
was very famous in its day. He played (mostly villains)
for years in the company of Mdlle. Beatrice (real name,
Beatrice Binda), who was the daughter of a distinguished
French exile. Carter Edwards used to be a prolific writer
of verses of merit, but a long career of stage crime has
probably caused the death of his muse. Barrett appar-
ently selected him as a sound, withering, vociferous
curser, and certainly he does “ cuss ” with the weight of
a corporation steam-roller.
Old Londoner : “Wilson Barrett has still got that peach-
stone in his mouth. Ninety-nine out of every hundred
elocutionists have it, and' the newspapers say the voice is
‘mellifluous.’ Listen to his peach-stone Hamlet, if he
ventures it here.”
M.S.: “ Some of the warriors of ancient Byzantium, in
‘ Claudian,’ appear in what seem to be Scotch kilts, of
faded Stuart tartan, and carry bucklers of leather, brass-
bound ! Perhaps some old ‘ Macbeth ’ properties have
been pressed into requisition. But what of the archeology,
of which a special bit of brag is paraded on the pro-
grammes ? One quite expected one of the hairy battlers
—several wore red beards, too ! —to describe Maud Jeffreys
as ‘ a braw bonnie lassie,’ and to offer Claudian a pinch
of snuff and a wee drap o’whuskey.”
“ At the Shaftesbury, on the opening night, William-
son and Musgrove’s ‘Scarlet Feather’ danced and
fluttered in the appreciative breezes of a waving welcome
—in the first act,” writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent. “ Early in the second it struck an equatorial
calm and slowly wilted to a sad death of weakness and
stagnancy. As there are but two acts'it may be summed
up that half of the comic opera is ciinking, and the re-
mainder is pallid failure.”
The 4th November last was the 50th anniversary of the
death of Felix Mendelssohn Barthold.v, which calls up a
little yarn of Abraham, father of Felix. Previously to
Felix becoming famous in music, Abraham had always
been alluded to as “ son of Moses Mendelssohn, the great
banker.” Afterwards, he became, in print, “ father of
Felix Mendelssohn, the great musician.” Asked, on his
deathbed, if he wished to give any Special instructions as
to his funeral, he answered “ No ; but, for Heaven’s sake,
let my obituary notices be about myself. For the last 10
years, I have been the father of my son ; before that, I
was the son of my father. Let my last passport be in my
own name.”
New York journalist W. G. Carleton is suing Nat Good-
win, our old friend of “ In Mizzoura,” for 50,000 dollars
damages on the ground that Nat’s play, “ Congress,” is a
barefaced plagiarism of Carleton’s “ Ambition.” Both
plays are to be performed before a jury of 12, which will
decide the question.
The Intendant of the Court Theatre in Dresden has
requested Mrs. Brown-Polter and Curly Bellew to per-
form there. It is stipulated that old English comedies
should predominate, but Shakespeare is also to be paraded
for inspection. Should this venture succeed, the Curly-
Potter co. may invade Austria-Hungary later on.
Royal females get very little of the giddier kind of re-
creation. The Duchess of York, for instance, had never
seen a London music-hall show until the other week,
when she was taken to the Alhambra.
New York Herald publishes interviews with the maids
of Maud Hobson and other lady members of a lately-
arrived Gaiety Co. One cf them told the reverent inter-
viewer that she had frequently been mistaken for an
actress when emerging from the stage door.
When an actress is mistaken for her dressing-maid, for-
sooth,
The mistaken soon awaken to a knowledge of the truth.
But, when the maid so lowly who a part has never
plaj'ed,
Passes muster as her mistress, with the stage-adoring
youth,
Well, then, by all that’s holy, it is rough upon the
maid.
The big cricket fixtures this year have been
planned with gate-money as the consideration. Viotoria
v. N.S.W. match was fixed for Monday, December 27, and
the second test for the Friday following, and this the
hottest time of the year. Again, N.S.W. and S. A. is fixed
for Jan. 7, and the third test for Jan. 14. With rail-
travelling in between, this conies terribly hard.

Member: “ After the England v. Victorian
match you published a letter from me re what is called
the ‘ boy’ nuisance in the M.C.O. reserve. Sul seq lentlyt
Melb. papers published similar complaints, and. the club
advertised, vaguely enough, that boys, and girls would
not be admitted to the reserve during the second test
match except on production of a ticket, and that ‘ boys
may be admitted on production of a lady's ticket.’ This
admits that hitherto boys and girls were allowed to swarm
vT “ ono P9 li se the pavilion seats without tickets!
The boy pavilion nuisance was worse than ever during
fhe recent N.S.W. d. Vic. match. But the seat-rushing
by boys is only part of the nuisance. The evil that needs
most attention from the ornamental committee is the
general conduct, and especially the obscene yarns, of gangs
of louts (aged 17 to 21) from the big public schools.
These individuals, as a rule, take no interest in the play ;
e yJ°. uc ily vote it a ‘ boah,’ and the only visible reason
for their presence is their desire to swap language unfit
for publication.”
* • • *
Writes S.S. : “ Truly dreadful was the inter-
provincial cricket tournament in Melb., Monday, Tuesday,
Wednesday and Thursday, with the thermometer fluctu-
ating between 98 degs. and 109 degs. in the shade, and
half the players in the club-room on ice half the time,
whilst the rest fried out in the Hadean atmosphere, and
played cricket like tired fatmen bearing great phj'sical and
spiritual burdens. The game was simply a farce, and the
men were all guilty of fatuous foolhardiness in going into
the field on the last day, when heat apoplexy was being
dealt out in the city, and a great, red-hot sorrow brooded
over the country. Nobody turned up to see the match,
but about two hundred looked in to encourage the caterer,
and the teams only staggered on to the green in response
to an alleged sense of honor, which did no earthly good
to anybody. There was really nobody there to know
whether the teams were honorable or not.”
* • * *
Captain Maclaren’s management of the
Britishers’ bowling, on New Year’s Day, was not likely
to strengthen public faith in the uncompromising earnest-
ness of gate-money “test” cricket. Richardson and
Hearne, both at “Home” and abr.'-d, stand head and
shoulders higher than the other English bowlers. Their
present season results in eleven-a-side games previous to
the second “test” were as follows, or thereabouts :
Richardson, 29 wickets, cost 27 runs per wicket. Hearne,
21 wickets, cost 20 runs. Briggs and Hirst together had
taken 10 wickets only for an average of about 72 runs per
wicket. Hirst, in his three latest attempts, had taken
no wickets for 157 runs. On Saturday the attack was
Opened by Richardson and Hirst, and, after the tall
Surrey man had bowled 6 overs for 2 runs, he was taken
off for some inscrutable reason, and put on again at the
other end, where he proved to be rather less successful.
After bowling 11 overs in all for 12 runs, the score then
being 54, Richardson was set aside and didn’t resume
operations until the board showed 115 runs. Meanwhile
Hirst and .Briggs were being freely pasted, whereas
Hearne, who didn’t bowl at all during the first hour’s
play, had managed to avoid heavy punishment.
When Richardson went on again (score 115) it
was in plaGe of Hearne The board at this time suggested
that the two “ crack ’’ English bowlers ought to have
been at either end, without intermission, from the start,
instead of which they had bowled on the “ one down
t’other come up” system whilst the batsmen were
making the game somewhat lively at the expense of the
vastly inferior Hirst and Briggs. And what was the ex-
planation of Maclaren’s fondness for these well-estab-
lished failures, in face of the fact that Hayward, bowl-
ing on the same ground against Victoria, had taken
6 wickets at an average of 19 runs? Hayward wasn’t
asked to bowl in the second test-match until the Austra-
lians had scored 155. Then he went on, with Briggs
at the other end. In his first over he nearly
settled Macleod, and in his second Hill was
caught at the wicket. And yet, when Maclaren ordered
another change, it was Havward, and not the much-wal-
loped Briggs, who made way for Hearne again. Although
Richardson, Hearne, and Hayward are clearly the three
most effective of the English bowlers—their average in
each case being fully 40 runs better than that of Briggs
or Hirst—-they were worked in shifts until the Australian
score had mounted to 210 for two wickets. Then, for the
first time, Richardson and Hearne bowled alternate overs
from opposite ends. The Bulletin doesn’t say that the
English captain played to give the game an attractive ap-
pearance for the Australian public. The Bulletin merely
says that the first object of a test-match, or any other
match, is gate money.
The monotony of the Australians’ first innings,
on New Year’s Day, was fairly broken when Storer took
his pads off and started to bowl. Gregory put one up
flukily, and, altogether, seven runs were scored off the
over, which concluded with the death of M’Leod—a clean
bowl. Five minutes later, Gregory drove one of Storer’s
tricky slows straight at Hayward’s “ bread-basket,” and
the fieldsman, in missing the catch, got his thumb dis-
located. More excitement. The crowd felt very grateful
to the versatile English wicket-keeper.
* • • •
The Melbourne crowd gave Storer a chivvying
over his repeated appeals for l.b.w. Australian cricket-
goers read the newspapers, and are perhaps getting a bit
full of the rather outspoken criticisms of the Britishers
on the “brutal barracking” of the Australian cricket at-
tendances. Even Ranji., who dearly loves to figure
in print, may get himself disliked.
• • • •
Nobody watching the return of a successful
batsman to the pavilion can doubt the awful importance
of these public performers. The man who has scored
his “century” or more comes back with wrinkled
brows and chin on chest. His vanity doesn’t permit him
to look satisfied with himself. He assumes the aspect of
one suffering from a painful accident.
* • * *
Reuter’s “ special ” with the English cricketers
is “Tom” Kelynack, of Melb. Herald. Kelynack is a
first-rate mimic, and has every Melb. celebrity in his
throat, or “ on the Kelynophone,” as he calls it. When
Grace broke his bat on the M.O.C. ground a few years
ago, and called out “ Another baht! ” Kelynack did such
a beautifully accurate reproduction of the champion’s
bellow in the press-box that Grace was half-inclined to
leave his wicket and run after and bash the man who had
trodden on his dignified professional corn.
• • • *
It was generally expected that, in the course of
the Melbourne Cricket Ground improvements, the press-
box would be removed from its present absurd position
to a better one. The box, however, remains where
it was, but it has been considerably improved and
enlarged. Also a wash-hand basin and tap have
been at last provided—the Melb. cricket authorities
formerly were under the impression that newspaper-men
never washed—and of the water from that tap the less
said the better. As the new box is composed mostly of
glass, it can be recommended, on the fifth of five blazing
days, as one of the hottest spots on earth.
* * • •
Veteran cricketer “ Tommy ” Horan has sud-
denly grown 16 years younger, also tall and thin. Ranji
was practising on the East Melbourne ground, one day
last week, and Thomas couldn’t resist the temptation to
have a shot at him. “ A beauty 1 ” exclaimed Ranji, as
the first ball was half-way, and he prepared for one of his
“marvellous” strokes. Next moment there was an
astonished Indian looking after a wicket that had flown
in three separate directions. For two days the Melbourne
cricket world dropped the painful subjeot of the first test-
match and calked instead about Horan's glorious wiping-
out of Eanji. “
London Sportsman says Iredale was onoe in the
hardware line. Iredale’s ancestors started the business
now carried on by Lassetters. The Iredales drifted out
with the ebb and the Lassetters came in with the flood
tide, which has been rising ever since.

Ranji says Charley Turner, with practice, would
be the Charley Turner of old. Maybe, but the showing

* • * *
The spectacle of benevolent Hebrew bookmakers
standing on barrels on S.A. racecourses and offering to
receive tote investments and return full dividends with-
out retaining any commission, is quite exhilarating to the
Gentile backers who have been plucked so long. The
bookies apparently rely on backing likely horses in the
machine for their profits. The flaw in the new Act is
that it does not prohibit totalisator agents. Anyhow, the
idea of Cohen reduced to trying to make an honest crust
by investing his own money on the tote is something that
never before entered into the public’s wildest dreams.
* • * *
The long arm again. Singular the number of
doubles brought off in Victoria lately by one horse
winning two races on the same day. Coincidence, or
——? Nov. 24, Aluminium ; Dec. 10, Leeze Me ; Dec. 12,
Vanity ; Dec. 18, Vanity ; Dec. 21, Sioux. No less than
five of these wins were credited to E. Lewis.
* * * *
Backers had a great outing at Randwick on
New Year’s Day, the opening day of Tattersall’s Club
races, no less than four favorites getting home. Kelso’s
stable was in luck again, carrying off the Carrington
Stakes and Pace Welter with Loch Marie ; and the Hor-
dern ditto won the Juvenile Stakes and Club Handicap
with Miss West and Mirella respectively, a couple by
Nordenfeldt. With his team in such form, Mr. Kelso
should feel tempted to postpone his trip to England until
later on. On the second day the reversals of form began,
only one favorite winning. Mr. Long’s Sweet Alice, who
started an even-money favorite for the Nursery Handi-
cap, was this lonesome exception. The feature of the
day was the extraordinary success of Lochiel, whose pro-
geny won three of the five flat races on the card—Cherry,
the New Year’s Gift; Old Clo’, Tattersall's Club Cup ; and
Duddington, the Welter Handicap. Mr. S. E. Kennedy
pulled off the Steeplechase with Bernal, a 12 to 1 chance.
Seven horses started, and all finished the stiff course
without a mishap.
• • • •
The aggressive demands of horse-owners for a
share of the sweep-money have led to trouble in W.A.
According to the North Coolgardie Herald “ When
the sweep on the Coolgardie Cup was drawn in August
last Le Var fell to a ticket held by Mr. Jessup, of Mt.
Malcolm, and he was apprised of his good fortune from
the Courier, which arrived in the same envelope
as one from P. Bolger, stating that unless Mr. Jessup
laid him £2OO to nothing he would scratch his horse.
... It was decided to lay Bolger £lOO, and a wire
was sent to that effect. The offer was accepted.
Le Var won the race, and Mr. Jessup remitted
his winning ticket by post to the Courier office. He
duly received a receipt for it, and, up to date, that is all
he has got, because the management of the Courier
declined to pay him over the full amount of his
prize. They state that Mr. Jessup must suffer
them to deduct from his prize the £lOO he laid to
Bolger, but Mr. Jessup takes up the position that
his wager with Bolger is no concern of the
Courier’s, but purely a private concern between him
and Bolger. The Courier management, however, are
steadfast in_ the position they have taken up, and so Mr.
Jessup has instructed his solicitors in Coolgardie to issue
a writ against the Courier proprietary for the full amount
of the prize. . . It is highly impolitic that the sweep-
promoters should be allowed to take any cognisance of
the betting transactions between their clients and the
owners of horses. Such a state of things would enable
a dishonest sweep-promoter to go to the different owners
of horses, disclose to them the names of the holders of
their horses in the sweep, and ‘ whack ’ with them any-
thing the holder would lay off. . . It is not suggested
that anything of the kind has been done in this case, but
if sweeps are legal there should be something to make
any such means of fleecing the public impossible.’'
If the above is a correct statement of the case
it is an interesting episode. When the demand of the
horse-owner for a bite out of the sweep arrives separately
it looks as if someone connected with the sweep supplied
him with the names of lucky ticket-holders, so as to assist
him in his nefarious demands, and that is bad enough.
But, if the demands of Bolger, or anybody else, for a share
of the sweep-money arrives in the same envelope with
the official notice of the drawing, as seems to have been
the case in this instance, then it is a great deal worse, and
the inference that the sweep management doesn’t object
to Bolger’s proceedings becomes irresistible. And why
should a sweep-promoter help Bolger to squeeze the
harmless, necessary prize-winner ?
* • » *
The A.J.C. stewards fined a jockey on Boxing
Day for appearing in soiled pants. As the jockey had
started in three races previously, it would be interesting
to know how many pairs of pants the official gentlemen
expect a jock, to carry around with him. Why don’t
they pass a regulation that no horse shall be allow’ed to
perspire, or throw up the dust? Then such troubles
would be averted.
• * * *
Dear Bulletin, —How long will lucky drawers
of horses in “ Tatt’s ” sweeps submit to be “ gathered in ”
by the sharks of the turf? The owners of a certain horse
engaged in a recent cup were offered £lOOO to nothing.
They wanted £l5OO, and because the drawer did not
agree the horse was scratched, although the writer was
assured by the owner that it was a certainty as far as
human knowledge could tell. “ Tatt.” has the remedy
in his own hands. Let him draw the sweeps on the day
of the race. The reason constantly advanced against
this arrangement is that it would not give the drawer a
chance to hedge. But very few drawers want to hedge,
and it doesn’t matter a straw even if some occasional
individual does want to. — Snake-Juice.
* * • *
The horse-owner who sent a wire to a Sydney
racing-dub,. “ Your handicapper ought to handicap pigs,”
has been given six months rest.
• * * *
Messrs. Barden, Megson, and Kellovv will all
have cause to remember the Protestant Alliance Gala,
after which they were disqualified for one month. The
effect of this disqualification will be (unless it is removed)
that neither of the three men will be able to compete in
the League and A. N. A. meetings. Megson is more seri-
ously affected, inasmuch as he was called upon by Walne
to defend the Brassard Badge on January Bth at the Mel-
bourne Exhibition track. If this disqualification is not
removed, then the Badge and the accompanying £5
a week will fall to Walne. In the event of this’happen-
ing, Don Walker will be called upon to meet Walne on
the Bth instant., so that whichever way things go there
will be a paced match for the Brassard Championship.
The Dunlop teams, consisting of six triplets and two
tandems, will pace the contestants.
* • » *
Barden threatens to take next boat ’Ome. Pro-
bably, however, the long Britisher will think twice before
he returns to the land where first-class men have to battle
for a stake of a couple of pounds.
Payne won the double with a couple of splendid
sprints, but, with the test-match in progress, cycling was
utterly forgotten, and there were more officials than
spectators on the track.
Megson’s place in the Brassard will probably be
taken by Green, and the battle between the sturdy New-
castle man and Walne should do more to revive Vio.
cycling' than anything short of the return of Bilmartin.
Both performed at Castleniaine on Saturday, each taking
a first to the other’s second in the two scratch races.
Green’s winnings within the last three months total over
£350.

Negotiations have been entered into for a match
of multicycles—England v. America. England would be
represented by teams selected from the famous “Dunlop”
stable ; and America—it is more than likely—by 3ome of
the speedy teams that have been pacing Michael.
» * * *
Platt-Betts again lowered his own time for the
five miles Australian record, at Newcastle (N.S.W.), on
New Year’s Day. Paced by two quints, and a quad., he
covered the distance in 9min. 58sec\, being 1 2-ssec. better
than his previous time. Betts leaves for Westralia
ln -ii u^ ’ w * le . re he will stay a fori night, and
will then proceed direct to England, to endeavor to regain
the world's records that he has lost whilst in Australia.
Betts, on his Dunlop-tyred machine, has lowered all Aus-
tralian records up to 10 miles, and, in one instance,
equalled the half-mile world’s record.
» » * ♦
Narandera (N.S. W.) bikists are proudly sticking
out their manly chests. Platt-Betts honored their course,
last Boxing Day, by riding up and down it once or twice
behind a triplet. The up and down part of the track
evidently made Betts feel a bit nervous, for he repeatedly
advised his pacers in a loud tone, after a specially hard
bump, to “be careful.” Previous to Betts’ arrival in
Narandera, a rumor had circulated that he was billed for
the same day at Yass, and many who had never seen him
before doubted his identity, and believed a pseudo-Betts
was “rungin” on them. But thegenuine article appeared
all right, and now every bikist in Narandera is going to
christen his next baby John Bicycle Platt-Betts Record
Hooligan.
• •
The pneumatic seat for bicycles, lately patented
in Philadelphia, sounds promising. The seat is “ blown-
up” with a tube, just as one inflates the tire, and the
result is a resting-place which adapts itself to the circum-
stances of the human form divine. For the lean and
angular kine among bikers the pneumatic saddle should
prove a blessed relief.

Scene: A road outside a Western Queensland
town. Sand, dust, flies, heat—losdeg. in the shade. A
party of cyclists comes along panting, puffing, perspiring.
As they pull up at the verandah of a wayside pub., a pass-
ing Chinaman in a cart thus apostrophises them : “ I say
—you lot allee same bloomin’great fool."’ John could
understand hard graft among the cabbagee for filthy
lucre on a hot day, but sport of this kind was clean beyond
him. And it takes a lot to make a Chow so verbally
aggressive as this.
••- • •
Some M.L. boroughs have put their incorporated'
heads together to block the scorcher :—“ A piece of road
which lends itself both to ‘ scorching ’ and observation
will be measured, and at one end Someone will be posted
to give a signal when a given mark is passed. At the other
end an observer will be placed with a stop-watch to
record the time taken over the measured mile. If the
time so taken denotes an unreasonable speed on the part
of the cyclist, a signal will be given, and further along
the road a barricade will be lowered, before which the
cyclist must perforce halt. Here his name and address
will be taken with a view to proceedings.”
**' * •
Vic. railway department now grants special
concessions to racing cyclists ; when six of these bump-
tious and utterly useless, and not particularly ornamental,
individuals travel together, and they are prepared to
assert that they are not engaged in any useful business,
but are bent on racing only, they get return tickets at
single fares. Six men going out to irrigate a patch of
mallee desert, or to open up a new reef, pay full fares—
quite naturally.
• * * •
The Yic. amateur cyclists, with cash pigeon-
shootist Coldham as their high-priest, are eternally
giving themselves airs about the intense purity of the
sport as run by them, and the complete absence of any-
thing like a craving for filthy profit in the ranks of their
riders. Yet it is a matter of common report that every
rider whose name is known is riding a machine presented
to him by the bike-shop as an advt., while a score of
others are getting for (say) a fiver machines which cost
the ordinary buyer four times that amount.
• • * «
Jackson is having a bad time in the States. The
Baldwin Hotel, San Francisco, where he used to stay, and
where he was a big draw in the heyday of his glory, has
turned him out on account of his color; and, greater
crusher still, scratching, gouging rusher sailorman
Sharkey says he will not meet a colored man in the ring.
It is about time Peter came home.

The other day Panton, P.M. (Melb.), fined
a youth £4 and <£l costs for “ stoushing ” a
church deacon. Accused had no business to lam-
baste the deacon, and so far deserved what he got.
But it wearies the ordinary brain to get at the
reasons for the scale of punishments. A man can
kick his wife to within five feet of the cemetery
for less money than it cost this young man to
wallop a church deacon in a very mild way. One
pound is the fixed rate for road-metalling a police-
man. And any festive larrikin in funds can
indulge, for a couple of quid, in the prime amuse-
ment of crippling a citizen or two, if they are not
deacons.
There is one thing which may generally
be reckoned on to wake up a heavy sleeper, and
that is a big fire in the house. Last week, there
were so many big bush fires in the S.E. corner of
Tasmania, down near Hobart, that the whole
Island of Fast Asleep has yawned and sat up in
astonishment. Unfortunately, several lives were
lost in the course of the unexpected awakening.
The country on Tasman’s Peninsula and the ad-
jacent mainland was ablaze for miles. At Mt.
Lyell, and the mines in its vicinity, the fire
destroyed every building, and the residents had to
fly for their lives. Large numbers of people found
shelter in the North Lyell Tunnel. One man
jumped down a shaft 50ft. deep. Another got
into the water and covered his burns with
mud, after which the fire, passing overhead,
literally baked the mud on him. At the noto-
rious Port Arthur, celebrated in convict history,
nearly all the old-time points of interest have
been wiped out of existence. The hospital peni-
tentiary and adjacent buildings are now heaps of
ruins, though the commandant’s old house, the
hotel, and other buildings have escaped. Amy
Sherwin, the one-time operatic singer, was driving
through the blazing bush, and had a narrow
escape. Heavy rains have now extinguished the
flames; and on Mt. Wellington, near Hobart, at
time of writing, snow was falling on the smoulder-
ing embers. The loss of property is very con-
siderable, and the oldest residents of Raspberry-
land cannot remember having received such
another shock.

Three horses proved three alibis in Melb.
County Court last week. A small boy had been
kicked in the jaw by a nag and wanted .£249 from
the animal’s alleged owner, one Barker. But
Barket satisfied the judge that one of his three
nags was shifting furniture at the time, and that
the other two were in the stable. None of the
three was called to say neigh on its own account.

In this nineteenth century science has made
rapid progress in treating with disease in every
form. Researches and study have brought to
light many remedies that are invaluable to suffer-
ing humanity, but, without doubt, the greatest
and grandest in the annals of medical science is
the accurate location of disease, which it would
seem from the great errors that are made could j
not be acquired, but must be a direct gift from
birth. That the POLYPATHS can, without
feeling the pulse, looking at the tongue, or asking
any questions of the patient, locate unerringly
each disease of which a patient may be suffering,
is a fact that can easily be tested at their Insti-
tute, 84 Collins-street, Melbourne. Never in the ■
history of Melbourne has any body of men created ;
such a sensation or been the object of such wide-
spread comment and universal praise as has been
accorded the POLYPATHS since their Institute
was founded in Melbourne. But it is as it should
be, and only goes to prove that the citizens of
Melbourne are fully competent to discriminate
between the good and the bad. Their success has
spread like wildfire far beyond the borders of Vic-
toria among those who had given up all hope of
ever being well again, and they are by hundreds
availing themselves of the Polypathie system in
both the diagnosis as well as the treatment of
chronic disease.
Here begins the very first starting point of van-
tage with the Polypathie school of practice, for its
chief exponents are men who from a natural and
overpowering sympathy with all forms of disease
and suffering are able to determine by quickened
sense, surely and with determination, the exact
cause of all that makes life a burden to thousands
all over the land. The highways of medical prac-
tice are strewn with the wrecks of wrong diagnosis,
with the going out of innumerable lives because of
improper location of disease, far more than from
the use of remedies of themselves hurtful. Here
Polypathy plants its foot firmly down and de-
clares that not alone the instinctive, keen sense
of its consulting chief should be taken as a finality,
but that what he determines is the real trouble,
with all the causes that led him to the clear
conclusion, shall be placed before the full faculty
of the Institute and his judgment meet their
approval.
Polypathy claims the freedom of accepting the
good and rejecting the bad in all systems ; it has
formulated special treatment for itself in all
chronic cases ; it prepares its own medicines, and
applies them in the light of nineteenth century
progress.
The success attending this new system is almost
miraculous, but it is in accordance with natural
and applied science, and wins its constant vic-
tories over disease because of the true following
out of that which natural ability and the highest
developments of Medical Science has shown to be
sure and true. All sufferers are welcome to the
fullest investigation of the POLYPATHIC
MEDICAL INSTITUTE, of 84 Collins Street,

write. All communications are strictly con-
fidential, and an opinion will be given in all

E. LENTHALL OLDFIELD, Principal.
Dear Bulletin,— lf “R. Williams,” Gil-
gunnia, refers to the plain turkey in his query, he
is wrong in thinking that he found eggs, in that
bird. Possibly he found one egg on dissecting
the bird ; but the other so-called eggs were merely
unimpregnated ovaries. Unquestionably the
plain turkey (Otis Australis, Gould) never lays or
hatches inore than two eggs annually. The term
plain applied to O.A. doesn’t mean common ;it
simply means that the bird, which isn’t a turkey
at all, is found bn or frequents the great plains of
the interior of Australia. Latham’s tallegallus, or
brush turkey, lays from 12 to 16 eggs annually,
and is a true turkey Otis Australis is a bastard.
The brush turkey is gregarious in its nidification ;
from 20 to 30 frequently lay in the same nest or
mound. The mound is composed of leaves, grass,
sand, &c., the heat of which, in decaying or fer-
menting, acts as an incubator, and the eggs are
successfully hatched ; not more than 4 per cent, on
on average prove infertile. The “ mallee hen ”
(Leipoa occellata, Gould) is also a true turkey, or is
classed as such ; its habits of nidification are strictly
analogous to those of. the brush turkey. The
plain turkey (0.A.) sits on its eggs - the male and
female birds taking turn and turn about at the
hatching. The nest is extremely simple, nothing
more than a depression scratched in the ground.
-H. J. M‘Cooky.
Dear B, —My father was an old school Scot,
who adored ‘‘ Robber Burns ” and despised fiddlers,
artists, or pianists. Had he lived to read the
recent articles on Burns the chances are he would
have Bilmartined The Bulletin staff and burnt
the office. For 364 days of the year he was a
peaceable, sober, hard-working citizen, but, on
that cursed “Februar’ morn,” the acrid smoky
aroma of Scotch whisky filled our house from
dawn. Even his bitterest foe on that day was
pressed ,to drink to Bobby’s memory. All day it
was a gentle simmer of “ Get up and bar the
door,” “Rob Tamson,” and “ A man’s a man for
a s "that.” The children were dressed in kilts, and
all day long endured with silent rage the taunts
and gibes of Irishers and Deutschers, who, if they
were cursed with a national poet, cared not one
blank dash for .his memory; but night brought
the climax. Dad was the boss Caledonian of any
“ Burns Supper ” he chose to honor with his pre-
sence. When he sang “ Scots-vvha-hae,” his voice
would jar the windows like a discharge of artillery,
and turn a hundred, hard-headed money-grubbing
Scots into roaring lunatics. Ten minutes later
he would have them wiping away tears with the
tails of their kilts as he sung the same tune to the
words of “The Land o’ the Leal,” given in a
solemn, pathetic Glenlivet voice. The next
phase would be bursts of Homeric laughter at
“Tam O’Shanter,” or “ The Laird o’ Cockpen.”
The last act would be a head like Andree’s bal-
loon and a big slump in words and music. And to
think there are thousands like him who have con-
sumed oceans of whisky and endured headaches
that buzzed like the engines of a tramp steamer,
all in honor of a miserable impostor. Truly,
“saith the preacher, all is vanity.’’--MauParritch.

Dear Bulletin, —It is a stupid error to
call the Australian tea-tree a “ti-tree.” More than
this, it is a new-chmnism, a relic of Gov. Phillip, a
Botany Bayism—on which The Bulletin ought
to jump with both its No. 10’s at once. The tree
in question received its name from Capt. Cook’s
sailors, owing to the close resemblance of its leaf
to tea ; and they used the leaves as tea. Tea-tree
is the name of this well-known shrub —not “ti-
tree.” —H. J. M'Cooey.
The chief obstacle of the philologist in the
isles of the south is the extreme politeness of the
natives, who seldom tell their instructor when he
is at fault. In translating the Bible into the
language of the Sandwich Islands some particu-
larly bail blunders were made. For instance, the
saying that it is hard for them that have riches to
enter the Kingdom of God came badly to grief.
In the island language wai-wai means property —
riches ; vae-wae means legs. And for years the
natives allowed their preachers to go on telling
them how difficult it was for a man with legs to
enter Paradise.
lie rabbit-proof fences. Along the border,
commencing at the meet'ng-point of the three
provinces (N.S.W., Queensland, and S.A.) and
separating N.S.W. and Q., is an alleged rabbit-
proof fence, which, with the necessary cottages,
gates, and other conveniences cost the country a
considerable sum of money. Here and there
boundary-riders are stationed to look after it;
there is also an overseer in receipt of a good
salary. And while the fence is persistently main-
tained, the country on each side is thickly infested
with rabbits, increasing by thousands every month,
and they can be seen night and morning exchang-
ing greetings through the meshes. What the
whole fabrication is there for nobody knows, and
nobody, save the tax-burdened working-builock,
seems to care.

Consider the J.P., how he performs. The
other day a man of “ respectable appearance ” was
| brought before the Fitzroy (Vic.) Bench, charged
j with indecency. The accused had already been
convicted in another suburb on a similar charge,
but the defence brought various gentlemen in
black clothes and belltoppers into court to swear
that he was a nice, quiet, married man, and a
church deacon ; whereupon the Bench concluded
that it was sure he didn’t mean it, and he was
discharged. Next case : A seafaring man, with
the ordinary appearance of the common person
who hauls tarry ropes and scrubs greasy decks.
He was charged with exactly the same offence,
and made exactly the same defence. There were
no convictions against him, however, and the
police did not know anything to his discredit, but
he failed to bring along a lot of persons instore
clothes and high black hats to say he attended
church regularly, and respected the cloth and the
plate. So the Bench took what it called “a
lenient view of the case,” and sent him to gaol for
three months.

Sciatica, Nervous Debility and
Insomnia Cured.
(From the Seymour Express, Victoria.)
In a neat little reel and white cottage, near the
old Victorian town of Seymour, our representa-
tive found Constable William Greenaway taking a
well-earned rest, having just carried off the Sep-
tember pigeon -shootrug trophy. For many years
he had been a sufferer from insomnia and nervous
debility, and consequently could never altogether
rely upon himself. For him a steadier was
necessary, and although he had divided many
pools, he had not before won outright. His story
is interesting, and we,give it in verbatim
“I have served in all sorts of places and seasons
I was engaged in the capture of the Kelly gang,
after the shooting of Sergeant Kennedy and
Ins comrades outside Mansfield. Our trial and
privations were so heavy that I contracted sleep-
lessness and nervous debility, and ultimately
sciatica. Nothing that I could do had any effect
upon the sciatica, until I was induced to try Dr
Williams’Pink Pills for Pale People. I treated
the idea at first with ridicule, but my condition
being desperate, and causing my wife great
anxiety, I tried them. I would lie awake, gazing
at the ceiling, turning restlessly from one side
to another, till dawn, when I would doze off
through sheer exhaustion. This went on for
years. My temper got unbearable, and my health
was breaking fast. I lost my appetite, and though
I consulted all the best doctors, and took all sorts
of nauseous stuffs, nothing but strong narcotics
ever brought me sleep. It was not a natural
slumber, but an artificial oblivion. My weight
went down to 10.2. I was but a shadow of my
former self, and was indeed in a very desperate
condition. It meant death or the mad-house if
this sort of thing went on ; so I tried one Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pill after each meal, at my wife’s
earnest solicitation. After a week I tried two
pills after each meal, and almost immediately ex-
perienced a change for the better.
“ This was April last year. My health improved
by leaps and bounds, and before the month was
over my sleep and appetite returned. I then in-
creased the dose to three pills after each meal.
During the six months that have elapsed since I
started the experiment my weight has increased
to 11.3, and I have no longer any sleepless nights,
nor do I suffer from nervous debility. The plea-

Rheumatic Gout Succumbs to
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
(From the Hobart Clipper).
What at first sight reads like an improbable
story, but the truth of which is beyond any doubt
6 sma^es t detail, comes to hand from Port
Arthur. Mrs. C. Fairclough, wife of the pro-
prietor of the Carnarvon Hotel, Port Arthur, and
formerly a nurse in the hospital at Launceston,
reports a case of marvellous cure by the use of
Hr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. For
years past she had been a sufferer from rheumatic
affections, which at first seemed trivial, but in
spite of ordinary precautions continued to increase
to such an extent as to give rise to considerable
alarm. The doctors pronounced the case to be
one of much severity, the complaint being rheu-
maticgout. _ Everything was tried that could be
tried, but without avail, and Mrs. Fairclough be-
came at length a pronounced invalid.
Worrying over her illness brought on a variety
of other complaints The least cold in the world
affected her to an abnormal degree. She was
seized with pains in the feet to such an extent
that she could hardly walk, even a feeble effort
her considerable pain. She was com-
pelled to sit m a warm place constantly, and her
appetite fell away to such an extent that she ate
but one meal m three days. It is, perhaps,
needless to remark that every effort was made to
secure relief, the best medical advice procurable
being obtained. It was without avail, however,
for she, so far from getting better, grew steadily
worse. J
For months she was hopeless of ever recovering
the use of her limbs, and the pains which she
endured were absolutely beyond conception.
-Hot sea baths were tried, but failed to make an
impression upon her; and, in fact, she was
looked upon as a confirmed invalid by all her
friends. J
In her extremity she was willing to try any
possible remedy, and hearing of some of the
marveUous cures effected by the use of Dr.
Williams Pink Pills for Pale People she decided
to try them. Her husband laughed at the idea,
and condemned the medicine as being useless and
inoperative. But, acting on the belief that if
Dr Williams’ Pink Pills did her no good a little
could not do any harm, she gave them a trial.
ohe noticed a decided improvement in her con-
dition after commencing to take the pills. The
pains were not of so aggravated a nature, and
sne could stand without assistance, a feat which
previously she was unable to accomplish. Soon
the pains in the feet ceased altogether, and she
felt like a new woman. Delighted with the dis-
s° ver y which she had made, she continued to use
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and, after carefully
aking them continuously, according to directions,
tor a period of two months, she felt that she had
im longer any occasion to use the medicine

said, in answer to enquiries made, “ how grateful
1 am for having discovered a cure for my com-
plaint. I was in torture, and completely broken
down with despair, for my condition. I felt that
life was a burden, and I seemed to be in every-

Extraordinary Cures.
IN THE NEIGHBORHOOD OF NEWTOWN.
A visitor to the .suburbs of Sydney might spend
a more profitless amount of time than would be
occupied in calling upon Mr. Salter, of 89 Rose-
street, Darlington, and inquiring into the veracity
of the marvellous cure which had been lately
effected in his own particular case by that weli-
known and unrivalled Blood Builder and Nerve
Restorer, “Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for P;.le
People.” Mr. Salter, upon being interviewed by
a reporter who was despatched to interrogate him
upon this point, replied :
“Yes, truly, I have had a marvellous escape
from an agonising death, but I am now able to
get about and pursue my calling, which is one
that requires considerable bodily stiength and
activity.”
“What have you been suffering from, Mr
oalter ?
V.
“Pleurisy in its worst form ; and when I tell
you that I was an inmate of Prince Alfred Hos-
pital for four months, and went through a terrible
operation there, you can fully realise that mv ill-
ness was no child’s play. ”
“How did you contract your illness, Mr.
Salter? ”
“Well, my occupation is one that compels me
to be out of-doors in all weathers, and getting cold
and wet, and not taking proper care of myself,
was, of course, the commencement of it.”
“ And this operation you speak of, Mr. Salter?”
“ It was an operation to remove the ‘ pus ’ from
the lungs, and a very critical one it was, too. I
was forced to have two ribs removed, and little
dreamt I should ever be able to go to work again.
On leaving the hospital, I had a number of
doctors, but I cannot say that they benefited me
much. Numbers of my friends were very kind to
me my landlord and others —for my being laid
up for such a length of time did not conduce to
my prosperity.”
“ You look fairly well now, Mr. Salter ? ”
“I feel wonderfully well, and it all came about
through a friend of ours—a Mr. Green.”
“Do you mean Mr. Charles Green, the well-
known provision dealer of Alice-street, Newtown?”
“ None other. He had been suffering for a long
time from indigestion and a bad cough, and he got
completely cured by taking a course of ‘ Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills.’ ”
“ So, I suppose, he persuaded you to try them?”
“ He did, and I am very grateful to him for
having done so, fori experienced relief after the
first box, which I took according to the printed
directions, and I went on taking them until I was
able to go to work again ; and I have now been at
work for a fortnight, as Mrs. Salter or any of the
neighbors could tell you. My wife here has gone
in for them herself, as she has not been at all well
lately.”
“ Is that so, Mrs. Salter ? ”
“ Undoubtedly. I have been suffering from a
severe pain in my left side, which has been on me
for six years, and which leaves me very weak and
languid. lam taking the Pills, and they already
have done me a great deal of good, as I feel twice
the woman I did, and I am going steadily on with
them.”
A Salvation Army Miracle.
EXHAUSTION AND PRIVATION
REDUCE A ZEALOUS OFFICER TO
DEATH’S DOOR.
HER RESCUE EFFECTED BY DR
WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS.
It is not London alone that is haunted with the
drums and trumpets of the Salvation Army. A
letter from a very distinguished Parisian physician
describes the incident in the life of a prominent
lady officer of the French Salvation Army, in
2
ABT.E TO MARCH.
terms very gratifying to the publishers of this
book.
The medical man referred to is the well-known
Dr. Thierry Mieg, M.D., a distinguished physician
of Versailles, who is decorated with the Honor-
able Ribbon of the Legion of Honor. He de-
scribes the ease of Ensign Bonjour, of the Salva-
tion Army, as one of many patients belonging to
the Army who have been treated in his practice.
Rightly or wrongly, this lady has thrown her-
self with enthusiasm into the work of the Salva-
tion Army. Exhausting marches, terrible priva-
tions, hard work, cold, damp, and all the fanatical
energy of a Salvation Army officer had brought
her very low, and in danger of death. Her ner-
vous condition was most pitiable. She could
hardly crawl into the consulting-room of Dr.
Thierry Mieg, and she had to be helped out of the
waiting-room by two or three of the hundreds of
patients who throng the learned doctor’s clinic.
Supported thus, she made her way feebly into
the preser.ce of the famous physician.
The reputation of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills has
extended to France for some time, and the French
doctors of medicine, more liberal-minded than
their English congeners, have for a long time been
among the best customers. Of course, there is no
reason why any English medical man should not
prescribe Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, as they are a
scientific remedy, discovered by a legitimate and
legally registered practitioner, a graduate of Edin-
burg University : but the fact remains that pro-
fessional prejudice is in this country stronger than
scientific conviction, and, as far as open recogni-
tion goes, the medical profession seems to know
no better than to place Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
in the same category as empirical remedies and
patent medicines, which is, of course, absurd to
anyone who knows the facts.
France, which is behind England in every other
respect, honorably takes the lead in this matter.
1* re rich physicians will prescribe whatever remedy
will cure their patient. In this case, Dr. Thierry
Mieg at once prescribed Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
THE SALVATION ARMY MIRACLE.
In his own words this treatment “ wrought a
miracle.
Dear Doctor,” the lady wrote (of course in
Erench), only a few days afterwards, “ thanks for
the second box of Pills which you have sent me.
1 cannot sufficiently thank God for the good that
has been procured to me by Dr. Williams’Pills, on
which lam sure He lias cast his benediction. I

nervousness. Under the influenee'of the Pills you

Army without fatigue, and for yourself, my dear
doctor, I pray that heaven may richly bless you
tor all that you have done for me and my sisters
of the Salvation Army.
(Signed) “Ensign Bonjour.”

drooping and fading in the springtime of youth?

greenish complexion, and a languidness of step

and the functions of life become regular.

sponsibility at the time their daughters are bud-

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To all Malaysia was “ Flaming Flanagan ”
known ;to none of it favorably. Still he had his
uses, and, when it was discovered that Van
Booben’s unostentatious departure from the
Lugar ruby-mines meant also the evanishment
therefrom of a score of splendid stones, the
Dutch authorities saw Heaven’s finger in the fact
that Flanagan happened just then to put into
Landak. The Flamer, once Irish and sur-
geon, was now cosmopolitan and more than half-
buccaneer —a burly, ferocious, good-humored,
reckless, red-bearded ruffian, who, having missed
the 16th century, was doing his level best to make
the 19th atone for the blunder.
Unto Flanagan thus summed-up the mines-
superintendent in what he fondly imagined to be
the English tongue. “De sdones pack pring, unt
de man if can you—but de sdones pring everyhow.”
And then he named a number of guilders which
made the demi-pirate do some hard thinking as
he went back to his craft, a stout little sea-boat of
a schooner that had one night gone mysteriously
a-missing from a pearling-station up Luzon way.
“ Angelos ! ” he shouted the moment he got
aboard. Angelos, who rejoiced further in the
appalling patronymic of Spavidroskoulos, was a
skinny little Greek, three-parts moustache —the
rest rascality and seamanship ; and into this un-
angelic angel Flanagan briefly fired the facts.
“ What I want your opinion about, me boy, is
this. Aeht duizend guildern they’ll give, to get
these rubies back—say a thousand pounds. But
Mynheer’s motto, as ye know, is ‘ Nothin’ for
nothin’, an’ divilish little for a halfpenny ! ’
What percintage do ye think, now, they’d be
givin’ on the value of the jools ? ”
“ Two or tree per een’.! Not so mush as five ! ”
“ Take it at three, then ! That would mean for
the stones a value of —let’s see—thirty thousand
pounds, or so ! ”
“Tirty tousan’ poun’!” echoed the Greek.
“ Seven ’under’ an’ fifty tousan’ franc! ” and then
the Hellenic rascal and the Celtic looked hard
into each other’s eyes before Flanagan said: “ An’
now, me angel of innocence, is there anny parti-
cular idea that strikes ye in connection with the
highly interestin’ calculation ye have just per-
forrum’d? ”
The little man twinkled —eyes, ear-rings, and
teeth. “Yas! Tirty tousan’ poun’ mush bettair
dan von tousan’! ”
Flanagan pulled out his cigar-case and offered it
to the Greek. “ It’s a mighty quare coincidence,”
he said as he bit the end off his own weed, “ but
that's the very identical notion that was passin’
through me own brain at the moment! And now,
me noble descindant of Agammimnon, to arrms !
—or, rather, to say ! ”
“Varevego? Sout’?”
“Ay, south to begin with. I’ll tell ye more
when we get down the coast a bit. Get your
anchor now as quick as ye like. And—l say ! get
Dutch colors bent on the insign-halliards. It’s
not much in love I am with Johnny Bull’s red
rag ; still I’ll be d difl go thief-catchin’
under it ayther ! ”
* * * *
“ The divil fly away with this monsoon ! ”
quoth Flanagan. “ Look here, Angy, that’s
Boarjah Point, isn’t it ? ”
The Greek came over to the lee-rail, well down
into the churning of the schooner as she gnawed
her way up into the stiff sou’-wester.
“ Danjin ! ” he said—“not Boarjah ! ”
_ “ Danjin, is it? Come now, that’s better than
I bought! Still, me jintleman may be half across
Borneo be this time rubies an all.

The cocoanufc plumes tossed wildly still
upon the crest of Danjin Point, but, in the lee
thereof, the Kini-Balu lay motionless upon the
sunset-gilded river. The crew half-a-dozen
Javanis and a Chinky cook—were gobbling rice
and fish upon the forecastle-head, with many a
glance towards where, upon the skylight aft, the
Greek mate sat, bled freely from a cut over the
eyebrow, and cussed melodiously in many tongues,
The cut was from the fist of Flanagan, who,
after being on shore for half-an-hour at the Dyak
village, had come off again in haste, bluntly or-
dered the mate to remain on board, knocked him
down out-of-hand for expostulating, and gone
below for his revolver.
And now, was he, Angelos Spav-etcetera—hav-
ing so far shared the scheme—to be thus ignomi-
niously kicked out of its fruition ? By all his
gods he swore it should not be !
Up came Flanagan again in five minutes, with
booted legs and belted waist. He laughed as he
dropped down into the dingy,
“ Mind what I told ye, me little man,” he called
out, “or, bedad, it’ll be more than a knock in the
eye ye’ll get when I come back! ” Then he sculled
himself over to the bank, already fading into
darkness, and hid the boat beneath the drooping
nipa leaves. But he hadn’t gone 50 yards before
the Greek, dripping and determined, had him in
sight again.
* * • *
Yan Booben slept. It was a cave in the
limestone rocks above the Widambary valley-
clammy with mysterious moisture, faintly lit with
fluttering fire, ghostly with the flitting to and fro
of black and monstrous bats.
And now stole in another shadow—bigger, and
as black—as Flanagan came with bootless feet to
where the ruby-stealer lay, with his revolver
underneath his feather-fern pillow. Flanagan felt
f or it j drew it swiftly out, and flung it behind him,
a s Yan Booben gripped him desperately in the
dimness. The Dutchman was no chicken, but in
] ess than a minute the red-bearded Titan was
sitting on him and cheerfully expostulating,
“ Take it easy, now, me Dutch friend, and don’t
rejooce ine to the peenful necessity of squeezin’
your respected windpipe ! ”
Holding his captive with one iron fist, he threw
fresh sticks upon the fire, took a piece of rattlin-
stuff from his pocket, and coolly trussed the un-
lucky Dutchman neck and heel,
“ Webley’s, I purceive, mynheer,” he observed
cheerfully, as he picked up the other’s weapon.
“ And a very dacent stamp of pistol, too ; though
I prefer the second-size Colt, meself ! But now
to business!” And he drew his own pistol,
“ Where might these rubies be now ?—quick ! ”
Yan Booben had no rubies—so he said, in Dutch.
“ Speak English, me friend—ye know it well,
’Tisn’t much of a lingo, God knows !—but, anny-
how, ’tis better than Dutch. And, as for the
rubies, I know ye have ’em, an’ I’d find ’em easy
after I’d put a’ bullet through ye ! As I will—
without anny more palaver—unless ye speak up,
quick ! ”
He levelled at the Dutchman’s head, and in-
stinct warned Van Booben that it was no pretence,
He gave in.
“ Bedad, then, they’re beauties, an’ no mis-
take ! ” and Flanagan held the stones to the blaze,
“ But they’re not all here—eight, nine, ten ! Only
tien here, me injaynious young friend, and it was
twinty ye collared. Twintig, do ye hear!
Where’s the rest ? ”
The other groaned. “They’re round my
waist,” he said.
Flanagan turned him over like a log, and found
a hard-stuffed belt. He slit the leather with a
practised hand, and brought the contents to the
light.
Then he gasped. The other ten rubies were
such stones as he had never dreamed of. Again
and again he held them up to the blaze before he
f°™d speech. _
“Holy Moses! Young man’ he said I
dont wonder that ye made a dash for these.
They re worth the game But ye ve lost it, me
boy; and now ye ve got to settle up with lim
Flanagan, no less.” . .
He sat silent for a time again, fingering the
stones. Van Booben watched him wondering.

now, me boy . an, if we had a pack of cards be

“What are ye grinnin’ at, ye miserable
little shark-skin ? Tell me all ye know, or, be the
piper, I wouldn’t think twice about makin’ a hole
in ye ! ”
The chief of Widambary didn’t understand
English, but he understood eyes. He touched
his parang-handle.
“Red man,” he said, in Malay, ‘ thou art
foolish ! Needs but that I lift a finger ftr thee to
be sliced like a water-melon ! ”
Flanagan, blood-stained, burnt, and rueful,
clapped his hand to his side, then remembered,
with a groan, that his teeth were drawn —he had
no pistol.
“Goon ! ” he said, sullenly. “ Tell me all
ye know! ”
“ Not much is that! ” the Orang answered.
“ The little man of the ship—he whose upper lip
beareth hair like unto the horn of a buffalo—he
followed thee last night into the hills. This morn-
ing came he back, and with him was the Belanda,
about whom thou didst ask so many questions.
They said thou hadst started across the mountains
to the Galara country. That I knew was a lie —
no man ever crossed the Lupang in this part.”
“ And I might have been lying murdered up in
that cursed cave ! ”
“It is so! ” answered the chief, stolidly.
“What have we to do with white men’s quarrels ?
Enough have we of our own ! ”
“More of that to ye!” muttered Flanagan.
“I’ll get level yet with all hands, an’ I won’t
forget ye in the account, Mister Orang-Kaya ! ”
Then, in Malay, “Know you which way went the
vessel ? ”
“ Salatan 1 ” and the chief pointed to the south.
“Some writing did they leave for thee. Bring ! ”
he said to his son, who had been hopefully awaiting
a signal to cut the white man down. The disap-
pointed amateur went out, and came back in a
couple of minutes with a board—apparently broken
out of a boat’s stern-sheets. Upon it, in big letters
of black paint, was the single word “ Quits 1 ”

Life’s Overland.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Grey-lying miles to the nor’ward of Nor’ward,
Red-leaping leagues to the westward of West,
Further than keenest of sight follows forward,
Further than boldest of hearts ever guessed ;
Still with its secret to man unimparted,
Still with its beckoning wealth unattained,
Lies the dim goal that has never been charted,
Down the long road that has never been chained.
Day after day, and from morrow to morrow,
Pointing the way where the wide road begins,
Sweep the red scorpion-scourges of Sorrow,
Lashing her children out West for their sins ;
Beefwood and whitewood, and redgum and wilga,
Lead them and goad them, and guide them and
guard,
Till hidden in tangle of sandal and mulga,
The gates to the East and the Southward are
barred.
Westward and Nor’ward ! and fainter behind them
The roll of the waggons, the roar of the whips,
The tow’ring red dust-storms that waltz down and
wind them,
The blue mocking mirage that lies to their lips ;
Beyond the last camp of the furthest-west drover,
Beyond the last team-track, the last rotting
steer,
Beyond the last foot-pad the camels crossed over,
Beyond the lone grave of the last pioneer.
Westward and Westward! Out past the last
horror
Of thirst and starvation, of lorn lives and lost,
The bleaching white bones of the boldest explorer,
The scrubs and the plains that have never been
crossed;
Where the heat haze no longer in mockery dances,
Where no more the sand-drift whirls, brown on
the blue,
Where the pitying sun lays at rest his red lances,
With white flags of truce where his war banner
flew.
The last birds have waked them, they sleep now
no longer;
The last dark has lifted, the} 1 ' take no more rest,
For the aching feet heal and the tired heart grows
stronger
As every league bears them a league to the
W est.
Gold! Did they hear her sweet voice as they
started ?
Now she is dumb to them, scorned and dis-
dained,
And their goal is a goal that has never been
charted;
Their route is a road that has never been
chained.
Westward and—Homeward! Brown hands at
the back of them ;
Far in the distance white hands —and the rest;
One by one, outward, we lose the last track of
them,
All the world wending its way to the West;
One after one, till the last shall have started,
Yet no more the last than the first shall have
gained
In the lore of the goal that/has never been charted,
Down the long road that has never been
chained.

drived aglow with heat and excitement, and fixed
a runnin g noose on one end of it. Then—
T em r . ou £ d , ! hooried.
J° e threw his hat at them, and chased them
and rou “ d the J ard - P ad turned filo \ v ly
ln ie cen t re > hke a ring-master, his eye on the
co . v '', ’ a cod . °/, rop , e was bl hl s l hand, and,
with the right, he measuredly swung the
loop over and over his head for some time.
At last the cow gave him a chance at her
horns, and he let fly. The rope whizzed across
the yard, caught little Bill round the neck,
and brought him down off the post. Dad could
hardly believe it. He first stared at Bill as he
rolled in the yard, then at the cow. Mother
wished to know if he wanted to kill the boy, and
Joe giggled and, with a deal of courage, assured
Dad that it was “ a fine shot.” The cow and the
heifer ran into a corner, and switched their tails,
and raked skin and hair off each other with their
horns.
“What do you want to be always stuck in the
road for? ” Dad growled, taking the rope off little
Bill’s neck. “Go away from here altogether!”
Little Bill went away ; so did mother and Sal—
until Dad had roped the cow, which wasn’t before
he twice lassoed the heifer—once by the fore-leg
and once round the flanks. The cow thereupon
carried a panel of the yard away, and got out and
careered down the lane, bucking and bellowing
till all the cattle of the country gathered about
her.
Dad’s blood was up. He was hanging on to the
rope, his heels ploughing the dust, and the cow
pulling him about as she liked. The sun was
setting ; a beautiful sunset, too, and mother artd
Sal were admiring it.
“ Did y’ never see th’ blasted sun go—go down
be ” Dad didn’t finish. His feet slid under
a rail, causing him to relax his grip of the rope
and sprawl in the dust. But when he rose !
“ Are y’ going t’ stand staring there all night ? ”
They were beside the rails in an instant, took the
end of the rope which he passed to them, put it
once round the gallows-post, and pulled—pulled
like sailors. Dad hung on close to the cow’s head
while Joe kicked her with his bare foot and screwed
her tail.
“ Steady!” said Dad, “that’ll about do.” Then,
turning to the women as he mounted a rail and
held the axe above the cow’s head : “ Hang on
there now ! ” They closed their eyes and sat back.
The cow was very patient. Dad extended himself
for a great effort, but hesitated. Joe called out:
“L-l-ook out th’axe dud-dud-don’t fly and gug-
gug-get me, Dad ! ” Dad glanced quickly at it,
then took aim again. Down it came, whish!
But the cow moved, and he only grazed her cheek.
She bellowed and pulled back, and mother and
Sal groaned and let the rope go. The cow swung
round and charged at Joe, who was standing with
his mouth open. But only a charge of shot could
catch Joe; he mounted the rails like a cat and
shook his hat at the beast below.
After Dad had nearly brained her with a rail
the cow was dragged to the post again ; and this
time Dad made no mistake. Down she dropped,
and, before she could give her last kick, all of us
entered the yard and approached her boldly.
Dad danced about excitedly, asking for the long
knife. Nobody knew where it was. “ Damn
it, where is it?” he cried, impatiently. Everyone
flew round in search of it but Joe. He was
curious to know if the cow was in milk. Dad
noticed him ; sprang upon him ; seized him by the
shirt collar and swung him round and trailed him
through the yard, saying : “Find me th’ knife ;
d’ y’ heart ” It seemed to sharpen Joe’s memory,
for he suddenly remembered having stuck it in
one of the rails.
Dad bled the beast, but it was late before he
had it skinned and dressed. When the carcase
was hoisted to the gallows—and it seemed grue-
some enough as it hung there in the pallid light
of the moon, with the night birds dismally wailing
like mourners from the lonely trees —we went
home and had supper.
* * * *
Christmas Eve. Mother and Sal had just
finished papering the walls, and we were busy
decorating the place with green boughs, when
Sandy and Kate, in their best clothes-—Kate
seated behind a well-filled pillow-slip strapped on
the front of her saddle ; Sandy with the baby in
front of him—came jogging along the lane. There
was commotion ! Everything was thrown aside to
receive them. They were surrounded at the slip-
rails, and when they got down —talk about kissing !
Dad was the only one who escaped. When the
hugging commenced he poked his head under the
flap of Kate’s saddle and commenced unbuckling
the girth. Dad had been at such receptions
before. But Sandy took it all meekly. Arid the
baby! (the dear little thing) they scrimmaged
about it, and mugged it, and fought for possession
of it until Sandy became alarmed and asked them
to “mind.”
Inside they sat and drank tea and talked about
things that had happened and things that hadn’t
happened. Then they got back to the baby and
disagreed on the question of family likeness.
Kate thought the youngster was the dead image
of Sandy about the mouth and eyes. Sal said it
had Dad's nose, while mother was reminded of
her dear old grandmother every time the infant
smiled. Joe ventured to think it resembled
Paddy Maloney far more than it did Sandy, and
was told to run away and put the calves in. The
child wasn’t yet christened, and the rest of the
evening was spent selecting a name for it. Almost
every appellation under the sun was suggested and
promptly rejected. They couldn’t hit on a suit-
able one, and Kate wouldn’t have anything that
wasn’t nice, till at last Dad thought of one that
pleased everybody—“ Jim ! ”
* * * *
After supper, Kate started playing the
concertina, and the Andersons and Maloneys and
several others dropped in. Dad was pleased to
see them ; he wished them all a merry Christmas,
and they wished him the same and many of them.
Then the table was put outside, and the room
cleared for a dance. The young people took the
floor and waltzed, I dare say, for miles, their heads
as they whirled around tossing the green bushes
that dangled from the rafters; while the old people,
with beaming faces, sat admiring them, and sway-
ing their heads about and beating time to. the
music by patting the floor with their feet. Some-
one called out “Faster!” Kate gave it faster.
Then to see them and to hear the rattle-of the
boots upon the floor ! You’d think they were
being carried away in a whirlwind. All but Sal
and Paddy Maloney gave up and leant against the
wall, and puffed and mopped their faces and their
necks with their pocket-handkerchiefs.
Faster still went the music ; faster whirled Sal
and Paddy Maloney. And Paddy was ori his
mettle. He was lifting Sal off her feet. But
Kate was showing signs of distress. She leaned
forward, jerked her head about, and tugged
desperately at the concertina till both handles left
it. That ended the-tussle, and Paddy spread him-
self on the floor, his back to the wall, his legs ex-
tending to the centre of the room, his chin on his
chest, and rested.
Then enjoyment at high tide; another dance
proposed ; Sal trying hard to persuade Dad to
take mother or Mrs. Maloney up ; Dad saying
“ tut, tut, tut,” when in popped Dave, and stood
near the door. He hadn’t changed his clothes,
and was grease from top to toe. A saddle-strap
was in one hand, his Sunday clothes, tied up in a
handkerchief, in the other, and his presence made
the room smell just like a woolshed.
“Hello, Dave!” shouted everyone. He said
“Well!’’and dropped his hat in a corner. No
fuss, no kissing, no nothing about Dave. Mother
asked if he didn’t see Kate and Sandy (both were
smiling across the room at him), and he said
“ Yairs”; then went out to have a wash.
All night they danced—until the cocks crew—
until the darkness gave way to the dawn—until
the fowls left the roost and came round the door—
until it was Christmas Day!
